Definition and Types of Kafalah (Guarantee)
Defines kafalah linguistically and legally, and divides it into two types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define kafalah according to the Hanafi school, distinguish its two types, and state the ruling on kafalah bi al-nafs when undertaken without the principal's order.

Core Idea
Kafalah joins liabilities for claim — the guarantor's obligation is to produce, not to pay the debt itself.

Guarantees are common in financial and personal dealings, but their precise legal nature varies between schools. In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi definition of kafalah, its two main categories, and the ruling on guaranteeing a person without their permission. The key is understanding what exactly the guarantor owes: the production of the person, not the debt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic meaning
In Arabic, 'kafalah' means to join (ضم). The Quranic verse 'Wa kafalaha Zakariya' (Surah Aal-e-Imran: 37) illustrates this: Zakariya joined Maryam to himself to care for her.
Linguistic roots often illuminate legal concepts.
2
Identify the legal (shar'i) definition
Legally, kafalah is joining one liability (dhimma) to another for the purpose of claim, not for the debt itself. The original debt remains on the principal (asil).
This distinguishes kafalah from a transfer of debt (hawalah).
3
Distinguish the two types
Kafalah divides into kafalah bi al-nafs (guarantee of the person) and kafalah bi al-mal (guarantee of property). The former requires producing the person; the latter involves financial liability.
The classification is based on the subject matter of the guarantee.
4
Analyze the ruling on kafalah bi al-nafs
The author states it is permissible (ja'iz) whether or not it is with the principal's order. This is the Hanafi position.
Permissibility here means valid, not necessarily recommended.
5
Resolve the objection
An objection: without the principal's order, the guarantor cannot produce him because the principal can refuse. The response: the guarantee's validity depends on the possibility of performance (imkan al-ada'), not on the principal being obligated to comply. The guarantor can still bring the principal to court via legal means.
This clarifies that validity does not require the principal's cooperation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الكفالة
الكفالة ضربان : كفالة بالنفس، وكفالة بالمال .
فالكفاله بالنفس جائزة، والمضمون بها : إحضار المكفول به .
الكفالة.
كتاب الكفالة
في اللغة هي: الضم، قال الله تعالى: ﴿ وَكَفَّلَهَا زَكَرِيَا ﴾ آل عمران / ۳۷ : أي ضمها إلى نفسه؛ للقيام بأمرها. وإنما سميت الكفالة بذلك ؛ لأنها ضمُّ إحدى الذمتين إلى الأخرى. وفي الشرع : عبارةٌ عن ضَمّ ذمة إلى ذمة في المطالبة، دون الدين، بل أصل الدين في ذمة الأصيل على حاله. قال رحمه الله تعالى: (الكفالة على ضربين: كفالة بالنفس، وكفالة بالمال، فالكفالة بالنفس (جائزة، سواء كانت بأمر المكفول عنه، أو بغير أمره، كما تجوز في المال.
فإن قيل : إذا تكفّل بغير أمره: لم يقدر على إحضاره؛ لأن للمطلوب أن يمتنع عليه.
قلنا: إنه يقدر على إحضاره، ولكن لا يلزم ذلك المطلوب، وجواز موقوف على إمكان الأداء، دون استحقاقه.
Book of Guarantee. Guarantee is of two types: guarantee of the person, and guarantee of property. Guarantee of the person is permissible, and what is guaranteed by it is the production of the person guaranteed. Guarantee. Book of Guarantee. In language it is to join. Allah Almighty said: 'And Zakariya became her guarantor' (Surah Aal-e-Imran, verse 37): meaning he joined her to himself to take care of her affairs. It is called kafalah because it is joining one liability to another. In Sacred Law: it is an expression for joining liability to liability for claim, not for the debt itself; rather the origin of the debt remains in the liability of the principal as it was. The author, may Allah have mercy on him, said: (Guarantee is of two types: guarantee of the person and guarantee of property. Guarantee of the person is permissible, whether it is with the order of the principal or without his order, just as it is valid in property). If it is said: If he guarantees without his order, he cannot produce him because the demanded person can refuse him. We say: He can produce him, but that is not binding on the demanded person; permissibility depends on the possibility of performing, not on deserving.
Source pages p. 5

Commentary
This is a passage from the definition and classification of kafalah (guarantee). The author begins by stating that kafalah is divided into two types: kafalah bi al-nafs (guarantee of the person) and kafalah bi al-mal (guarantee of property). Kafalah bi al-nafs is valid, and its object is to produce the person guaranteed. Then the commentator provides the linguistic and legal definitions: linguistically, kafalah means 'to join,' citing the Quranic verse where Zakariya (peace be upon him) is made the guarantor of Maryam (peace be upon her). Legally, it is joining one liability (dhimma) to another for the purpose of claim, but the original debt remains on the principal (asil). The author's text states that kafalah bi al-nafs is valid whether or not it is with the order of the principal, just like kafalah bi al-mal. An objection is raised: if the guarantee is without the principal's order, the guarantor (kafil) cannot produce him because the principal may refuse. The answer is that the guarantor can still produce him in the sense of presenting him to the court, but the principal is not bound to comply; the validity of the guarantee only requires the possibility of performance, not that the principal is obliged to appear.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafalah): Joining one liability (dhimma) to another for the purpose of claim on a debt or person.
كفالة بالنفس (kafalah bi al-nafs): Guarantee to produce a specific person before the claimant.
ذمة (dhimma): Legal capacity or liability that can be encumbered with obligations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is kafalah bi al-nafs valid even without the principal's order, according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because the principal is obliged to comply.
B) Because the guarantee only requires the possibility of producing the person, not that the principal is bound.
C) Because the guarantor has a right to force the principal.
D) Because it is analogous to kafalah bi al-mal which is also valid without order.

Key Takeaway
Kafalah joins liabilities for claim; kafalah bi al-nafs is valid if production is possible, regardless of the principal's consent.

Classification of Hawalah: Mutlaqah and Muqayyadah
Defines two types of hawalah and highlights the key difference in effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between absolute (mutlaqah) and restricted (muqayyadah) hawalah and state the legal consequence of each type.

Core Idea
In muqayyadah, the transferor cuts off all claims; in mutlaqah, he remains liable.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Quduri introduces the two types of hawalah: absolute and restricted. The distinction determines whether the transferor retains any liability after the transfer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
The text states hawalah is of two types: mutlaqah (absolute) and muqayyadah (restricted). Understand the Arabic terms and their meanings.
2
Analyze the contractual wording
In mutlaqah, the transferor says 'transfer for this creditor from me' without specifying a debt from the transferee. In muqayyadah, he says 'transfer the debt I owe you' tying it to a specific debt.
The wording determines the type and its legal effect.
3
Determine the legal effect
In muqayyadah, the transferor's recourse against the transferee is cut off after the transfer. In mutlaqah, the creditor can still approach the transferor if the transferee defaults, as the transfer is not tied to a specific debt owed by the transferee.
This is the key practical difference between the two types.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الحوالة
ولا تجوز به الكفالة.
والحوالة على ضربين: مطلقة، ومقيدة.
فالمطلقة أن يقول لرجل: احتل لهذا عني بألف درهم، فيقول: احتلت
والمقيدة: أن يقول : احتل بالألف التي لي عليك، فيقول: احتلت المحال له، وليس
له بعد الحوالة على المحيل سبيل، إلا أن يتوى ما على المحال عليه. المطلقة والمقيدة فرق، وهو أنه في المقيدة: انقطعت
السبيل بين مطالبة المحيل من المحال عليه.
:
فإن بَطَلَ الدين في المقيدة، أو تبيَّن براءةُ المحال عليه من الدين الذي قيدت به الحوالة : بطلت الحوالة مثل أن يشتري من رجل شيئاً بألف، ولم يؤد الألف حتى أحال بها لرجل عليه، فقبل، ثم استُحِق المبيع، أو كان المبيع عبداً، فظهر حراً فإن الحوالة في هذين الوجهين تبطل، وكان للمحال له أن يرجع على المحيل بدينه.
وكذا لو قيد الحوالة بألف درهم عند رجل وديعةً، فهلكت الألف
عند المودَع قبل تسليمها إلى المحال له : فإن الحوالة تبطل. وأما إذا سقط الدين الذي قيدت به الحوالة بأمر عارض، ولم تتبين براءته الأصيل: لا تبطل الحوالة، مثل أن يحتال بألف من ثمن مبيع، فهلك المبيع قبل تسليمه إلى المشتري : سقط الثمن عنه ، ولا تبطل الحوالة، ولكنه إذا أدى : رجع على المحيل بما أدى؛ لأنه قضى دينه بأمره.
من
Book of Hawalah (Transfer of Debt)
And kafalah (suretyship) is not valid with it.
And hawalah is of two types: absolute (mutlaqah) and restricted (muqayyadah).
The absolute: that a man says to another: 'Transfer for this [creditor] from me one thousand dirhams,' and he says: 'I accept the transfer.'
The restricted: that he says: 'Transfer the one thousand that I owe you,' and he says: 'I accept the transfer.' The creditor (muhtal lahu) has no recourse against the transferor (muheel) after the transfer, except if what is due from the transferee (muhtal 'alayh) is lost. The distinction between absolute and restricted is that in the restricted, the transferor's claim against the transferee is cut off.
If the debt in the restricted [transfer] becomes void, or it becomes clear that the transferee is free from the debt with which the transfer was restricted: the transfer becomes void, such as when a person buys something from a man for one thousand and does not pay the one thousand until he transfers it to a man who is owed [by the buyer], and he accepts, then the item is reclaimed, or the item was a slave and it turns out he is free: then the transfer becomes void in these two cases, and the creditor has the right to revert to the transferor for his debt.
Similarly, if he restricted the transfer to one thousand dirhams as a deposit with a man, and the one thousand perishes with the depositary before delivery to the creditor: then the transfer becomes void. As for if the debt with which the transfer was restricted falls due to an incidental cause, and the original burden is not proven to be removed: the transfer does not become void, such as when he transfers one thousand from the price of a sale, and the item perishes before delivery to the buyer: the price is dropped, and the transfer does not become void, but if he pays, he reverts to the transferor for what he paid, because he paid his debt by his [the transferor's] order.
From
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) on the ruling from Mukhtasar al-Quduri regarding hawalah (transfer of debt). It begins by stating that hawalah is not valid for kafalah (suretyship), meaning one cannot transfer the obligation of being a guarantor. Then it defines the two types of hawalah: mutlaqah (absolute) and muqayyadah (restricted). In mutlaqah, the transferor simply asks the creditor to collect from a third party without specifying a particular debt owed. In muqayyadah, the transfer is specifically tied to a debt that the transferee owes the transferor. The passage explains the key difference: in muqayyadah, the transferor's recourse against the transferee is cut off after the transfer, whereas in mutlaqah it is not (as explained later). The main focus of this section is on the invalidation of muqayyadah hawalah. If the designated debt becomes void (e.g., the sale is invalid) or it turns out the transferee never owed that debt, the hawalah becomes void and the creditor can go back to the transferor. Examples are given: if the item sold is reclaimed as someone else's property, or if the slave sold turns out to be free, the transfer is void. Similarly, if the debt was specified as a deposit that perishes before delivery, the transfer is void. However, if the debt falls due to an incidental cause (like the item perishing after the transfer but before delivery, and the price is dropped), the transfer is not void; the transferee who pays can then revert to the transferor because he paid on his behalf.

Key Terms
الحوالة (al-ḥawālah): Transfer of debt; a contract whereby a debtor (muḥīl) transfers his debt to another party (muḥāl ʿalayh) who then becomes liable to the creditor (muḥtāl lah).
مطلقة (muṭlaqah): Absolute hawalah; a transfer where the transferor does not specify that the transferee owes him a debt.
مقيدة (muqayyadah): Restricted hawalah; a transfer tied to a specific debt owed by the transferee to the transferor.
المحال له (al-muḥtāl lah): The creditor in a hawalah contract; the person entitled to receive the debt from the transferee.
المحال عليه (al-muḥtāl ʿalayh): The transferee; the person who becomes liable to pay the debt after the transfer.
المحيل (al-muḥīl): The transferor; the original debtor who transfers his debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the key difference between mutlaqah and muqayyadah hawalah?
A) In muqayyadah, the transferor is released from liability; in mutlaqah, he is not.
B) In mutlaqah, the transferor is released; in muqayyadah, he is not.
C) Both release the transferor completely.
D) Neither releases the transferor.

Key Takeaway
Hawalah is classified as absolute or restricted, and the type determines whether the transferor remains liable after the transfer.

Invalidation of Muqayyadah Hawalah
Explains invalidation of muqayyadah and gives illustrative scenarios.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a restricted hawalah becomes void and when it remains valid, and apply the rule to new scenarios.

Core Idea
If the designated debt is void or non-existent, the hawalah is void; if it falls due to an incidental cause, the hawalah remains valid.

This passage details the invalidation rules for muqayyadah hawalah. It distinguishes between cases where the underlying debt is fundamentally invalid and cases where it is extinguished by an external event.

Guided Path
1
Identify invalidation triggers
The text gives two main triggers: (1) the debt becomes void (batala al-dayn), and (2) it becomes clear that the transferee is free from the debt (tabayyana bara'ah).
These go to the root of the debt's existence.
2
Study illustrative examples
Example 1: The item sold is reclaimed (istihqaq). Example 2: The slave sold is found to be free. Example 3: The deposited money perishes before delivery. In all these, the hawalah is void.
The underlying debt is essentially non-existent or void ab initio.
3
Contrast with incidental cause exception
If the debt falls due to an incidental cause (e.g., the item perishes after transfer but before delivery), the hawalah remains valid. The transferee who pays can then revert to the transferor.
Here the debt was real at the time of transfer; its fall is subsequent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الحوالة
ولا تجوز به الكفالة.
والحوالة على ضربين: مطلقة، ومقيدة.
فالمطلقة أن يقول لرجل: احتل لهذا عني بألف درهم، فيقول: احتلت
والمقيدة: أن يقول : احتل بالألف التي لي عليك، فيقول: احتلت المحال له، وليس
له بعد الحوالة على المحيل سبيل، إلا أن يتوى ما على المحال عليه. المطلقة والمقيدة فرق، وهو أنه في المقيدة: انقطعت
السبيل بين مطالبة المحيل من المحال عليه.
:
فإن بَطَلَ الدين في المقيدة، أو تبيَّن براءةُ المحال عليه من الدين الذي قيدت به الحوالة : بطلت الحوالة مثل أن يشتري من رجل شيئاً بألف، ولم يؤد الألف حتى أحال بها لرجل عليه، فقبل، ثم استُحِق المبيع، أو كان المبيع عبداً، فظهر حراً فإن الحوالة في هذين الوجهين تبطل، وكان للمحال له أن يرجع على المحيل بدينه.

Checkpoint
If a muqayyadah hawalah is based on a deposit that perishes before delivery to the creditor, what is the ruling?
A) The hawalah remains valid and the creditor must claim from the depositary.
B) The hawalah becomes void and the creditor reverts to the transferor.
C) The hawalah becomes void and the creditor loses his debt.
D) The hawalah remains valid and the transferee pays the creditor then claims from the transferor.

Key Takeaway
A muqayyadah hawalah is void if the underlying debt is fundamentally non-existent, but remains valid if the debt falls due to an incidental cause after transfer.

Absolute Hawalah and Post-Transfer Legal Effects
Explains absolute hawalah irrevocability and post-transfer actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rulings of absolute hawalah and distinguish the effects of ibra, hiba, inheritance, and settlement on the transferee's right of recourse against the transferor.

Core Idea
Absolute hawalah is irrevocable; ibra extinguishes debt without recourse, while hiba and inheritance grant recourse.

This passage from Al-Jawharah al-Nayirah expands on the rulings of absolute hawalah (debt transfer) and various post-transfer actions by the creditor. Understanding whether an action constitutes a pure relinquishment (isqat) or a transfer of ownership (tamlik) determines if the transferee can revert to the transferor for reimbursement. The commentary clarifies these distinctions with practical examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the nature of absolute hawalah
Understand that absolute hawalah does not become void under any circumstance and the transferor's claim against the transferee remains until payment.
2
Analyze the ruling of ibra (release)
Determine that ibra is valid without acceptance and does not give the transferee a right to revert to the transferor because it is an isqat (relinquishment).
3
Distinguish hiba (gift) and inheritance
Note that hiba requires acceptance and gives the transferee recourse, as does inheritance, because in both cases the transferee becomes owner of the debt (tamlik).
4
Evaluate sulh (settlement) scenarios
Differentiate between sulh for a different type of asset (full recourse) and sulh for part of the debt with release of the remainder (partial recourse).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتصح برضا المحيل، والمحتال له، والمحال عليه.
وأما إذا كانت مطلقةً : فإنها لا تبطل بحال من الأحوال، ولا تنقطع فيها مطالبة المحيل عن المحال عليه، إلا أن يؤدي، فإذا أدى: سقط ما عليه قصاصاً.
-
ولو تبيَّن براءة المحال عليه من دين المحيل: لا تبطل أيضاً.
ولو أن المحال له أبرأ المحال عليه من الدين: صح الإبراء، سواء قبل المحال عليه أو لم يقبل، ولم يرجع المحال عليه على المحيل بشيء؛ لأن البراءة إسقاط، وليست بتمليك، فلهذا لم يرجع.
وإن وهبه له : يحتاج إلى القبول، وله أن يرجع على المحيل، كما لو أدى؛ لأنه مَلَكَ ما في ذمته بالهبة ، فصار كما لو ملكه بالأداء. وكذا لو مات المحال له، وورثه المحال عليه: له أن يرجع على
المحيل ؛ ؛ لأنه مَلَكَه بالإرث ، فصار كما لو ملكه بالأداء.
ولو رضي المحال له من المحال عليه بدون حقه، وأبرأه عن الباقي، نحو أن يصالحه على بعض حقه، وأبرأه عن الباقي: فإنه يرجع عن المحيل بذلك القدر، لا غير.
وإن صالح على خلاف جنس حقه، كما إذا صالح على الدراهم عن الدنانير، أو على العكس، أو على العروض فإنه يرجع بجميع الدين؛ لأن ما أدى : يصلح أن يكون عوضاً عن جميع الدين.
* قوله : (وتصح برضا المحيل، والمحتال له، والمحال عليه). أما المحتال له : فلأن الدين حقه، والذمم متفاوتةٌ، فلا بد من رضاه.
And it [hawalah] is valid with the consent of the transferor, the creditor, and the transferee.
As for if it is absolute: it does not become void under any circumstance, and the transferor's claim against the transferee is not cut off except if he pays; if he pays, it drops as a set-off.
And if it becomes clear that the transferee is free from the debt of the transferor: it does not become void either.
And if the creditor releases the transferee from the debt: the release is valid, whether the transferee accepts or not, and the transferee does not revert to the transferor for anything, because release is a relinquishment, not a transfer of ownership; therefore he does not revert.
And if he gives it to him as a gift: it requires acceptance, and he has the right to revert to the transferor, just as if he paid, because he owns what is in his liability by gift, so it is as if he owned it by payment. Similarly, if the creditor dies and the transferee inherits from him: he has the right to revert to the transferor, because he owned it by inheritance, so it is as if he owned it by payment.
And if the creditor accepts from the transferee less than his due and releases him from the remainder, such as settling for part of his due and releasing the rest: then he reverts to the transferor for that amount only, nothing more.
And if he settles for something of a different type, such as settling for dirhams instead of dinars, or vice versa, or for goods, then he reverts for the entire debt, because what was paid can serve as a substitute for the entire debt.
* His saying: (And it is valid with the consent of the transferor, the creditor, and the transferee). As for the creditor: because the debt is his right, and liabilities vary, so his consent is necessary.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage continues the sharh on hawalah, focusing on the absolute type (mutlaqah) and various post-transfer actions. It begins by stating a condition: hawalah requires the consent of all three parties—transferor, creditor, and transferee. Then it explains that mutlaqah hawalah is irrevocable; it never becomes void, and the transferor's claim against the transferee remains unless the transferee pays, in which case it becomes a set-off. Even if it turns out the transferee owed nothing to the transferor, the hawalah does not become void. The passage then discusses what happens if the creditor releases the transferee (ibra): it is valid even without the transferee's acceptance, and the transferee cannot revert to the transferor because release is a pure relinquishment. However, if the creditor gives the debt as a gift (hiba), acceptance is required, and the transferee can then revert to the transferor, as he now owns the debt. Similarly, if the creditor dies and the transferee inherits, he can revert. The passage also covers settlement (sulh) for less than the full amount: if the creditor accepts a partial payment and releases the rest, the transferee can only claim that amount from the transferor. If the settlement involves a different type of asset (e.g., dirhams for dinars), then the transferee can claim the full original debt from the transferor, as the payment is considered a substitute for the entire debt. Finally, the commentary elaborates on the condition of the creditor's consent, explaining that since the debt belongs to him and liabilities differ, his consent is mandatory.

Key Terms
الإبراء (al-ibrāʾ): Release of a debt; the creditor's act of discharging the debtor from liability without receiving payment.
الهبة (al-hibah): Gift of the debt; the creditor gives the debt (which is a receivable) to the debtor, making the debtor own it and thus able to revert to the transferor.
المصالحة (al-muṣālahah): Settlement; an agreement between the creditor and transferee to settle the debt for less than the original amount or for a different type of asset.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a creditor (muhtal lahu) releases (abra'a) the transferee (muhtal 'alayh) from the debt, what is the ruling regarding the transferee's right to revert to the transferor (muheel)?
A) The transferee can revert to the transferor for the full amount.
B) The transferee can only revert if the release was conditional.
C) The transferee cannot revert to the transferor at all.
D) The transferee can revert only if the transferor was at fault.

Key Takeaway
In absolute hawalah, ibra (release) is a pure relinquishment without recourse, whereas hiba and inheritance transfer ownership and thus allow the transferee to revert to the transferor.

Post-Hawalah Dispositions: Ibra', Hiba, Inheritance, and Settlement
Rulings on post-hawalah dispositions and their effect on recourse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the legal effects of ibra', hiba, inheritance, and sulh on the muhal 'alayh's right of recourse after hawalah.

Core Idea
Ibra' is a waiver without recourse; hiba and inheritance transfer ownership and grant recourse.

Hawalah transfers the debt to a new debtor, but what if the original creditor (transferee) later absolves, gifts, or settles? The Hanafi school distinguishes these acts based on whether the debtor acquires ownership of the debt. This lesson breaks down the legal consequences of each post-hawalah disposition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenarios
The passage covers four events after hawalah: absolution (ibra'), gift (hiba), inheritance (irth), and settlement (sulh). For each, note who acts and who is affected.
The muhal lahu (transferee) is the creditor after hawalah.
2
Distinguish ownership vs. waiver
Determine whether the muhal 'alayh (new debtor) acquires ownership of the debt (tamlik) or simply receives a waiver (isqat). Ibra' is isqat; hiba and inheritance are tamlik; sulh is partially tamlik.
Only tamlik gives the right of recourse against the muhil.
3
Apply the rule on recourse
If the muhal 'alayh becomes owner (by gift, inheritance, or payment), he may seek recourse from the muhil. If he receives a waiver (ibra'), he may not.
Sulh on the same genus gives recourse only for the amount paid; on a different genus, for the full debt.
4
Analyze the settlement (sulh) variants
When the muhal lahu settles for less, if the settlement is on the same genus (e.g., part of the same currency), recourse is only for that amount. If on a different genus (e.g., goods instead of money), recourse is for the full original debt.
This is because the different genus can be seen as consideration for the entire debt.
5
Formulate the practical consequence
State clearly for each scenario whether the muhal 'alayh can claim back from the muhil. This determines the final allocation of liability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إذا كانت مطلقةً : فإنها لا تبطل بحال من الأحوال، ولا تنقطع فيها مطالبة المحيل عن المحال عليه، إلا أن يؤدي، فإذا أدى: سقط ما عليه قصاصاً.
ولو تبيَّن براءة المحال عليه من دين المحيل: لا تبطل أيضاً.
ولو أن المحال له أبرأ المحال عليه من الدين: صح الإبراء، سواء قبل المحال عليه أو لم يقبل، ولم يرجع المحال عليه على المحيل بشيء؛ لأن البراءة إسقاط، وليست بتمليك، فلهذا لم يرجع.
وإن وهبه له : يحتاج إلى القبول، وله أن يرجع على المحيل، كما لو أدى؛ لأنه مَلَكَ ما في ذمته بالهبة ، فصار كما لو ملكه بالأداء. وكذا لو مات المحال له، وورثه المحال عليه: له أن يرجع على المحيل ؛ لأنه مَلَكَه بالإرث ، فصار كما لو ملكه بالأداء.
ولو رضي المحال له من المحال عليه بدون حقه، وأبرأه عن الباقي، نحو أن يصالحه على بعض حقه، وأبرأه عن الباقي: فإنه يرجع عن المحيل بذلك القدر، لا غير.
وإن صالح على خلاف جنس حقه، كما إذا صالح على الدراهم عن الدنانير، أو على العكس، أو على العروض فإنه يرجع بجميع الدين؛ لأن ما أدى : يصلح أن يكون عوضاً عن جميع الدين.
And if it is unconditional: it is not invalidated in any case, nor is the transferor's claim against the person transferred upon cut off, unless he pays. So if he pays, what is upon him falls due as set-off. If it becomes clear that the person transferred upon was free from the debt of the transferor: it is not invalidated either. If the transferee absolves the person transferred upon of the debt: the absolution is valid, whether the person transferred upon accepts or not, and the person transferred upon does not seek recourse from the transferor for anything, because absolution is a waiver, not a transfer of ownership. Therefore he does not seek recourse. If he gives it to him as a gift: it requires acceptance, and he may seek recourse from the transferor, just as if he had paid, because he has owned what is in his liability by gift, so it is as if he owned it by payment. Similarly, if the transferee dies and the person transferred upon inherits from him: he may seek recourse from the transferor because he has owned it by inheritance, so it is as if he owned it by payment. If the transferee settles with the person transferred upon for less than his right, and absolves him of the rest, such as making a settlement for part of his right and absolving him of the rest: then he seeks recourse from the transferor for that amount only, not more. If he settles on a different genus of his right, such as settling for dirhams for dinars, or vice versa, or for goods, then he seeks recourse for the entire debt, because what is paid is suitable as consideration for the entire debt.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage presents several rulings related to what happens after the hawalah is concluded. First, if the hawalah is unconditional (mutlaqah), it is never invalidated and the muhil's claim on the muhal 'alayh is not cut off until payment. However, if it becomes known that the muhal 'alayh was not actually indebted to the muhil at the time of transfer, the hawalah remains valid because the contract was based on the assumption of the debt. Next, if the muhal lahu (creditor) absolves the muhal 'alayh of the debt, the absolution is valid and the muhal 'alayh cannot seek recourse from the muhil because absolution is a waiver (isqat) rather than a transfer of ownership (tamlik). By contrast, if the muhal lahu makes a gift of the debt to the muhal 'alayh, this requires acceptance, and the muhal 'alayh may seek recourse from the muhil because he has acquired ownership of the debt by gift, as if he paid it. The same applies if the muhal 'alayh inherits the debt from the muhal lahu. Finally, if the muhal lahu settles with the muhal 'alayh for less than his full right, the muhal 'alayh may only seek recourse from the muhil for the amount actually paid. However, if the settlement is on a different genus (e.g., dirhams for dinars), he may seek recourse for the full original debt because what is paid can serve as consideration for the entire debt.

Key Terms
الإبراء (al-ibra'): Absolution; legal release from a debt, extinguishing the liability.
الهبة (al-hiba): Gift; a voluntary transfer of ownership of a debt, requiring acceptance.
الإرث (al-irth): Inheritance; transfer of ownership of the debt from the deceased to the heir.
الصلح (al-sulh): Settlement; a compromise where the creditor accepts less than full payment in exchange for full release.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If after hawalah, the muhal lahu dies and the muhal 'alayh inherits the debt, what is the ruling on recourse?
A) No recourse because inheritance is like ibra'.
B) Recourse allowed because inheritance transfers ownership.
C) Recourse only if the muhal 'alayh pays the debt.
D) Recourse requires acceptance.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi distinction between isqat (waiver) and tamlik (ownership transfer) determines whether the muhal 'alayh can seek recourse from the muhil after a post-hawalah disposition.

Conditions of Validity of Hawalah: The Three Consents
Commentary explaining the three consents for hawalah validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid hawalah and explain why the Hanafi school does not require the muhil's consent.

Core Idea
In Hanafi hawalah, only the consent of the creditor (muhal lahu) and new debtor (muhal 'alayh) is required; the original debtor's consent is not a condition.

This passage from al-Jawharah al-Nayyirah explains the consent requirements for hawalah (transfer of debt). The matn lists three parties, but the commentary clarifies that only two consents are actually necessary. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind each requirement and the Hanafi position, supported by authority from al-Hidayah and al-Nihayah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three parties in hawalah
The text names three parties: al-muhil (original debtor/transferor), al-muhal lahu (creditor/transferee), and al-muhal 'alayh (new debtor/person upon whom the debt is transferred).
These are the key actors in a hawalah contract.
2
Locate the ruling on consent for each party
The matn states the contract is valid 'with the consent of all three,' but the commentary disagrees specifically for the muhil. Identify which parties the commentator says are necessary and which are not.
The commentator follows the matn but then clarifies exceptions.
3
Understand the reasoning for each consent
For muhal lahu: consent is needed because the debt is his right and he must agree to substitute the debtor. For muhal 'alayh: consent is needed because he is taking on a liability voluntarily. For muhil: consent is not needed because he benefits from being freed and is not harmed.
The underlying principle: one cannot impose a liability on someone without their consent, but removing a liability does not require consent.
4
Examine the proof from al-Hidayah and al-Nihayah
The commentary cites al-Hidayah and al-Nihayah as authoritative sources that affirm the muhil's consent is not a condition. In al-Nihayah, a scenario is given: someone says to another 'take the debt from me,' and the creditor agrees; the hawalah is valid even without the original debtor's consent, and the original debtor is freed.
This proof shows the practical application and the legal consequence.
5
Apply to a scenario
Imagine A owes B $100. C says to B, 'I will pay you the $100 that A owes, so A is free.' B agrees. The hawalah is valid even if A does not consent. A is freed, and C cannot seek recourse from A.
This illustrates the non-requirement of muhil's consent in action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وتصح برضا المحيل، والمحتال له، والمحال عليه). أما المحتال له : فلأن الدين حقه، والذمم متفاوتةٌ، فلا بد من رضاه.
وأما المحال عليه فإنه يلزمه الدين، ولا لزوم بدون التزامه.
وأما المحيل: فالحوالة تصح بدون رضاه؛ لأن التزام الدين من المحال عليه تصرفُ في حق نفسه، وهو لا يتضرر به، بل فيه نفعه؛ لأنه لا يرجع عليه إذا لم يكن بأمره. كذا في «الهداية»(۱).
وكذا قال في النهاية»: رضا من عليه الدين، وأمره ليس بشرط، حتى إن من قال لغيره: إن لك على فلان كذا من الدين، فاحتل به علي، ورضي بذلك صاحب الدين: صحت الحوالة، فإن أدى المال: لا يرجع على الذي عليه الدين، وقد برىء الذي عليه الأصل.
He said: 'And it is valid with the consent of the transferor (muhil), the transferee (muhal lahu), and the person transferred upon (muhal 'alayh).' As for the transferee: because the debt is his right, and liability persons differ, so his consent is necessary. As for the person transferred upon: he is bound by the debt, and there is no binding without his undertaking. As for the transferor: the hawalah is valid without his consent, because the undertaking of the debt by the person transferred upon is an action concerning his own rights, and he is not harmed by it, rather he benefits from it, because he (the transferor) will not be recourse upon if it is not by his command. Thus in al-Hidayah. Similarly, it is said in al-Nihayah: 'The consent and command of the one upon whom the debt is owed are not conditions. Even if someone says to another: "You have a debt of such and such upon so-and-so, so take it from me (in transfer)," and the owner of the debt consents, the hawalah is valid. If he pays the money, he (the debtor) cannot seek recourse from the original debtor, and the original debtor is freed.'
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage from the sharh of al-Jawharah al-Nayyirah explains the conditions for the validity of hawalah (transfer of debt). The matn states it is valid with the consent of three parties: the muhil (transferor), the muhal lahu (transferee), and the muhal 'alayh (the person upon whom the debt is transferred). The commentator then breaks down each: for the muhal lahu, consent is required because the debt is his right and he must agree to change the debtor. For the muhal 'alayh, his consent is needed because he is taking on a liability voluntarily. For the muhil, the commentator clarifies that his consent is not a condition, even though the matn mentions him. Why? Because the muhil benefits from the hawalah as he is freed from the debt. The muhal 'alayh's undertaking is an act in his own right, and the muhil is not harmed. This is the position of al-Hidayah. The commentator further cites al-Nihayah to illustrate: if someone says to another 'take the debt from me' and the creditor agrees, it is valid even without the muhil's (i.e., original debtor's) consent. The key point: the consent of the creditor and new debtor are conditions; the original debtor's consent is not.

Key Terms
المحيل (al-muhil): The person who transfers the debt (the original debtor).
المحتال له (al-muhal lahu): The creditor who receives the transfer of the debt.
المحال عليه (al-muhal 'alayh): The person who becomes the new debtor, undertaking to pay the debt.
الحوالة (al-hawalah): Transfer of a debt from one person to another, freeing the original debtor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as explained in this passage, which of the following is a condition for the validity of hawalah?
A) The consent of the muhil (original debtor)
B) The consent of the muhal lahu (creditor)
C) The presence of a written contract
D) The payment of a fee for the transfer

Key Takeaway
For a valid hawalah in the Hanafi school, only the consent of the creditor and the new debtor are required; the original debtor's consent is not a condition.

Hawalah Frees the Muhil: The Naql vs Damm Distinction
Dispute on freedom of muhil in hawalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that hawalah frees the muhil and evaluate Zufar's analogy to kafalah.

Core Idea
Hawalah transfers the debt; kafalah adds liability – once transferred, the original debtor is freed.

This passage presents a key intra-Hanafi dispute: after a hawalah (transfer of debt) is completed by acceptance, does the original debtor (muhil) become free? The majority holds yes, but Zufar analogizes to kafalah (guarantee) and argues no. The resolution rests on the fundamental distinction between transfer (naql) and addition (damm). Understanding this distinction is critical to correctly applying these contracts in practice.

Guided Path
1
State the issue
Identify the exact point of disagreement: whether the muhil is freed from the debt upon completion of hawalah by acceptance.
2
Identify the Hanafi position and its basis
The matn states the muhil is freed. The basis is that hawalah is a transfer (naql) of the debt; once transferred, it no longer remains in the original liability.
This is the majority view of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad.
3
Analyze Zufar's objection and proof
Zufar argues by qiyas to kafalah: since in kafalah the original debtor remains liable, the muhil should similarly remain liable. He also notes that the muhil is compelled to accept if he tenders the debt, indicating ongoing liability.
Zufar's analogy is a qiyas jaliy (clear analogy).
4
Examine the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response distinguishes hawalah from kafalah linguistically: hawalah is naql (transfer), kafalah is damm (addition). Shari'ah rulings follow linguistic meanings. The compulsion to accept when the muhil tenders is due to the risk of insolvency, not because the muhil remains liable.
This refutes Zufar's analogy and explains the compulsion.
5
Determine the practical difference and tarjih
Under the Hanafi view, the creditor can only claim from the muhal 'alayh; under Zufar's view, he could claim from both. The majority view is mufta bihi.
Al-Khujandi further clarifies that kafalah with a condition of freeing becomes a hawalah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( فإذا تمت الحوالة برىء المحيل من الدين) بالقبول.
وقال زفر : لا يبرأ اعتباراً بالكفالة ولهذا يُجبر على القبول إذا نَقَدَ المحيل، ولا يكون متبرعاً.
ولنا: أن الحوالة للنقل والدين متى انتقل من ذمة: لا يبقى فيها، أما الكفالة فللضم، والأحكام الشرعية على وفاق المعاني اللغوية. - وإنما يُجبر على القبول إذا نَقَدَ المحيل ؛ لأنه يحتمل عود المطالبة إليه بالتوى، فلم يكن متبرعاً.
قال الخجندي: الحوالة مُبرِئةٌ ، والكفالة غير مبرئة، ويكون الطالب في الكفالة بالخيار إن شاء طالب الأصيل أو الكفيل، إلا أن تكون الكفالة بشرط براءة الأصيل: فحينئذ تكون حوالة.
He said: 'Once the hawalah is complete, the transferor (muhil) is freed from the debt' upon acceptance. Zufar said: He is not freed, by analogy to kafalah (guarantee). For this reason, he is compelled to accept if the transferor tenders (the debt), and he is not considered a volunteer. Our proof: Hawalah is for transfer (naql); once the debt transfers from a liability, it does not remain in it. As for kafalah, it is for addition (damm). And the rulings of the Shari'ah follow linguistic meanings. He is only compelled to accept if the transferor tenders because there is a possibility of the claim returning to him due to insolvency, so he is not a volunteer. Al-Khujandi said: Hawalah is absolving, while kafalah is not absolving. The claimant in kafalah has the option: if he wishes, he can claim from the original debtor or the guarantor, unless the guarantee is with the condition of freeing the original debtor, then it becomes a hawalah.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage discusses the effect of completing a hawalah by acceptance. The matn says the muhil is freed from the debt upon completion. Zufar, a prominent Hanafi scholar, disagrees: he draws an analogy with kafalah (guarantee), where the original debtor remains liable. He argues that if the muhil tenders the debt, the transferee is compelled to accept, indicating the muhil is not automatically freed. The Hanafi response distinguishes hawalah from kafalah: hawalah is a transfer (naql) of the debt from one liability to another, so once transferred, it no longer remains in the first. Kafalah is an addition (damm) to the liability; the original remains. The rulings of the Shari'ah follow these linguistic meanings. The compulsion to accept when the muhil tenders is due to the possibility of the new debtor's insolvency, not because the muhil remains liable. Al-Khujandi further clarifies: hawalah is absolving (mubri'ah), while kafalah is not. In kafalah, the creditor has the option to claim from either. Only if the guarantee includes a condition of freeing the original debtor does it become a hawalah in effect.

Key Terms
النقل (al-naql): Transfer; the legal concept that the debt moves from one liability to another.
الضم (al-damm): Addition; the concept that the guarantor adds his liability to the original debtor's.
الكفالة (al-kafalah): Guarantee; a contract where a person becomes jointly liable with the original debtor.
إبراء (ibra'): Absolution; legal release from a debt, extinguishing the liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the key difference between hawalah and kafalah?
A) Hawalah is a sale of debt, kafalah is a gift.
B) Hawalah is a transfer (naql) that frees the original debtor, while kafalah is an addition (damm) that keeps the original debtor liable.
C) Both are identical in effect.
D) Kafalah requires the consent of three parties, hawalah does not.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, completed hawalah frees the muhil because it is a transfer (naql) of the debt, not an addition (damm) like kafalah.

The Conditions of Tawa (Loss) in Hawalah and the Assigned Debtor's Recourse
Defines tawa, presents inter-madhhab disputes, intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, and the case of the assigned debtor's setoff claim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Abu Hanifa's two conditions for tawa, explain the inter-madhhab dispute on discharge in hawalah, and apply the rule that the assigned debtor's claim against the original debtor is not defeated by a conflicting claim of setoff.

Core Idea
The creditor can only revert to the original debtor upon loss (tawa) defined by two strict conditions: denial with oath or death insolvent.

This passage from the commentary on al-Quduri's Mukhtasar presents the pivotal concept of tawa (loss) in hawalah. Tawa determines when the creditor (muhtal) can return to the original debtor (muheel) after an assignment. The author defines tawa narrowly in the Hanafi school and contrasts this with other madhahib, as well as an intra-Hanafi difference with the Sahibayn. Additionally, the passage addresses a practical scenario: when the assigned debtor (muhtal 'alayh) sues the original debtor, the original debtor cannot defeat the claim by asserting a setoff.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions of tawa
According to Abu Hanifa, what are the only two situations that allow the creditor to revert? Read the text carefully.
Look for 'إما أن يجحد الحوالة ويحلف ولا بينة عليه أو يموت مفلساً'
2
Distinguish hawalah from kafalah
Use the etymological argument: hawalah means transfer, kafalah means joining. How does this imply discharge of the original debtor?
The Hanafi response uses this linguistic basis to counter Zufar's analogy.
3
Compare the Hanafi position with Zufar, Malik, and Shafi'i
State each opposing view and the Hanafi response. Focus on the thamarah (practical consequence).
Malik: both discharge. Zufar: neither. Shafi'i: never revert even with tawa.
4
Contrast Abu Hanifa with the Sahibayn
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad add a third condition (judicial declaration of insolvency). Why does Abu Hanifa reject it?
The text says 'رزق الله تعالى غاد ورائح'.
5
Apply the rule on the assigned debtor's claim
If the original debtor claims setoff, why is his statement not accepted? What is the cause of the assigned debtor's recourse?
The cause is 'قضاء دينه بأمره' (paying his debt by his command).

كتاب الحوالة
ولم يرجع المحتال على المحيل إلا أن يَتْوى حقه .
والتَّوَى عند أبي حنيفة بأحد أمرين : إما أن يجحد الحوالة، ويحلف ولا بينة عليه، أو يموت مفلساً .
وقال زفر : الحوالة والكفالة سواء، وكلاهما غير مبرئة. وقال مالك): كلاهما مبرئة؛ لأنه الحق واحد، فلو لم يبرأ الأصيل: لصار حقين. قلنا : الحوالة مشتقة من التحويل، والحقُّ إذا تحول من ذمة إلى ذمة: تبقى ذمة الأول فارغةً؛ لأنك إذا حولت الشيء إلى موضع آخر: بقي مكان الأول فارغاً، والكفالة مشتقة من الكفيل، وهو الضم، وضم الشيء إلى الشيء: لا يوجب فراغ الأول.
* قوله: (ولم يرجع المحتال على المحيل، إلا أن ينوى حقه).
وعند الشافعي: لا يرجع وإن توى.
* قوله (والتَّوَى عند أبي حنيفة بأحد أمرين: إما أن يجحد الحوالة، ويحلف، ولا بينة عليه أو يموت مفلساً أي ولا بينة للمحال له على المحال عليه بقبول الحوالة.
وقال التمرتاشي: ولا بينة للمحيل، ولا للمحال له.
وقوله : أو يموت مفلساً : أي لم يترك عيناً ولا ديناً ولا كفيلاً عن المحال عليه للمحال له.
وقالا : هذان، ووجه ثالث، وهو : أن يحكم الحاكم بإفلاسه في حال حياته .
وإذا طالب المُحالُ عليه المحيلَ بمثل مال الحوالة، فقال المحيلُ : أحلتُ بدَيْنِ لي عليك : لم يُقبل قوله، وكان عليه مثلُ الدِّين .
فإن مات المحالُ عليه، فقال المحتال : مات مفلساً، وقال المحيل خلاف ذلك :
قال في «المبسوط ) : القول قول المحتال، مع يمينه على العلم؛ لأنه يتمسك بالأصل، وهو العُسرة.
وفي غير «المبسوط»: القول قول المحيل، مع يمينه على العلم. كذا في «النهاية».
قوله: (وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد وجهاً ثالثاً، وهو: أن يحكم الحاكم بفلسه في حال حياته).
هذا على أصلهما : أن القضاء بالإفلاس صحيح.
وأما على أصل أبي حنيفة : فلا يتحقق الإفلاس بحكم القاضي ؛ لأن رزق الله تعالى غادِ ورائح.
* قوله: (وإذا طالب المحال عليه المحيلَ بمثل مال الحوالة، فقال المحيل : أحلتُ بدين لي عليك : لم يُقبل قوله، وكان عليه مثل الدين)؛ لأن سبب الرجوع قد تحقق، وهو قضاء دينه بأمره؛ لأن المحيل يدعي .
Book of Hawalah. And the creditor does not revert to the original debtor except if his right is lost. And loss according to Abu Hanifa is by one of two matters: either that he denies the hawalah and takes an oath and there is no evidence against him, or that he dies insolvent. Zufar said: Hawalah and kafalah are the same, and both do not discharge. Malik said: Both discharge because the right is one; if the original debtor were not discharged, it would become two rights. We said: Hawalah is derived from transfer, and when a right is transferred from one liability to another, the first liability remains empty; because if you transfer a thing to another place, the first place remains empty. And kafalah is derived from kafil, which is joining, and joining a thing to another does not necessitate the emptying of the first. * His saying: (And the creditor does not revert to the original debtor except if his right is lost). And according to al-Shafi'i: He does not revert even if lost. * His saying: (And loss according to Abu Hanifa is by one of two matters: either that he denies the hawalah and takes an oath and there is no evidence against him, or that he dies insolvent) i.e., there is no evidence for the creditor against the assigned debtor concerning acceptance of the hawalah. And al-Tamartashi said: There is no evidence for the original debtor nor for the creditor. And his saying: Or he dies insolvent: i.e., he did not leave property nor debt nor a guarantor for the assigned debtor on behalf of the creditor. And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: These two, and a third way: that the judge declares his insolvency during his lifetime. And if the assigned debtor demands from the original debtor the equivalent of the hawalah money, and the original debtor says: I assigned for a debt due to me from you: his statement is not accepted, and he owes the like of the debt. If the assigned debtor dies, and the creditor says: He died insolvent, and the original debtor says otherwise: It is said in al-Mabsut: The statement is the creditor's, with his oath on knowledge, because he holds to the original, which is difficulty. And in other than al-Mabsut: The statement is the original debtor's, with his oath on knowledge. Likewise in al-Nihaya. His saying: (And Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said a third way: that the judge declares his insolvency during his lifetime). This is according to their principle: that judgment of insolvency is valid. And according to Abu Hanifa's principle: insolvency is not established by the judge's ruling, because the sustenance of Allah Almighty comes and goes. * His saying: (And if the assigned debtor demands from the original debtor the equivalent of the hawalah money, and the original debtor says: I assigned for a debt due to me from you: his statement is not accepted, and he owes the like of the debt) because the cause of recourse has been realized, which is paying his debt by his command; because the original debtor claims.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage from the Sharh of al-Quduri's Mukhtasar addresses key rulings of hawalah (assignment of debt). The commentator begins by stating the core rule: the creditor (muhtal) cannot revert to the original debtor (muheel) unless his right is lost (tawa). He then defines tawa according to Imam Abu Hanifa in two ways: either the assigned debtor (muhtal 'alayh) denies the hawalah and takes an oath with no evidence against him, or he dies insolvent—meaning he leaves no property, debt, or guarantor. The commentator then presents inter-madhhab disputes: Zufar equates hawalah with kafalah (suretyship), holding that neither discharges the original debtor. Malik holds that both are discharging because the right is one. The Hanafi response uses the etymology of hawalah (transfer) to argue that the original debtor's liability becomes empty, while kafalah (joining) does not empty the first. Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the creditor never reverts, even if the right is lost. The commentator then adds a note from al-Tamartashi that there is no evidence required from either the original debtor or the creditor. An intra-Hanafi dispute follows: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad add a third way of tawa—when a judge declares the assigned debtor insolvent during his lifetime, which is valid according to them. But Abu Hanifa rejects this because wealth comes and goes; only actual death or denial with oath constitutes tawa. The passage then turns to a separate ruling: if the assigned debtor demands from the original debtor the equivalent of the hawalah money, and the original debtor claims it was for a debt owed to him (setoff), his claim is not accepted; he must pay. The reason is that the assigned debtor's recourse is established by paying the creditor at the original debtor's request. Finally, there is a conflict in the sources: in al-Mabsut, if the assigned debtor dies and the creditor claims he died insolvent, the creditor's statement is accepted with oath because he holds to the original presumption of difficulty. In other sources, the original debtor's statement is accepted. These rulings reflect the Hanafi school's precise conditions and proofs for hawalah.

Key Terms
الحوالة (al-hawalah): A contract where a debt is transferred from the liability of the original debtor (muheel) to another person (muhtal 'alayh), with the creditor's (muhtal) consent.
التَّوَى (al-tawa): Loss of the right; specifically the conditions under which the creditor can revert to the original debtor.
المحتال (al-muhtal): The creditor in a hawalah contract, who accepts the assignment.
المحيل (al-muheel): The original debtor who assigns his debt to another.
المحال عليه (al-muhtal 'alayh): The person to whom the debt is assigned; he becomes liable to the creditor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a condition for tawa according to Abu Hanifa?
A) The assigned debtor denies the hawalah and takes an oath.
B) The assigned debtor dies insolvent.
C) The judge declares the assigned debtor bankrupt during his lifetime.
D) Both A and B are conditions.

Key Takeaway
Tawa (loss) in hawalah is strictly defined: only denial with oath or death insolvent allow the creditor to revert.

Dispute between Assignor and Assignee in Hawalah
Ruling and commentary on whose word is accepted in hawalah dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on whose statement is accepted in a hawalah dispute and apply the Hanafi rule on evidence to determine the outcome.

Core Idea
In a hawalah dispute, the assignor's word is taken with oath because he is the defendant.

In hawalah transactions, disputes can arise over the nature of the assignment. The assignor may claim it was for collection (agency), while the assignee claims it was for a debt owed to him. This lesson examines which party's statement is given weight and under what condition, based on the Hanafi rule on evidence and denial.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their claims
The assignor (muḥīl) claims the hawalah was for agency (collect the debt for him). The assignee (muḥtāl) claims it was a transfer for a debt owed to him by the assignor.
Each party has a different interpretation of the same transaction.
2
Apply the evidence principle
The assignee is claiming a debt, so he is the claimant (muddaʿī). The assignor denies this, making him the defendant (munkir). The Hanafi rule is: the defendant's denial is accepted with his oath.
Burden of proof lies with the claimant.
3
Consider the linguistic flexibility
The term 'hawalah' can mean both transfer of debt and agency. This makes the assignor's claim plausible, further supporting his position.
This linguistic point is highlighted in the commentary.
4
Determine the consequence
If the assignor swears, he takes the money collected. The assignee cannot prove his claim without evidence (bayanah).
The assignee must bring witnesses or documents to prevail.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن طالب المحيلُ المحتال بما أحاله به ، فقال : إنما أحلتك لتقبضه لي، وقال المحتال له : بل أحلتني بدَيْنِ لي عليك : فالقول قول المحيل مع يمينه .
عليه ديناً، وهو ينكر، والقول قول المنكر، ولا تكون الحوالة إقراراً منه بالدين عليه ؛ لأنها قد تكون بدونه.
* قوله : (وإن طالب المحيل المحتال بما أحاله به، وقال: إنما أحلتك لتقبضه لي، وقال المحتال : أحلتني بدين لي عليك: فالقول قول المحيل، مع يمينه ؛ لأن المحتال يدعي عليه الدين، وهو منكر.
- ولفظة: الحوالة مستعملة في الوكالة فيكون القول قوله، مع يمينه، فإذا حلف: أَخَذَ الألف المقبوضة، ولا يصدق المحتال على ما ادعى من الدين إلا ببينة
If the assignor demands from the assignee that which he assigned to him, and says: 'I only assigned to you so that you may collect it for me,' and the assignee says to him: 'Rather, you assigned to me for a debt you owed me,' then the statement is the statement of the assignor with his oath. If there is a debt due on him and he denies, then the statement is the statement of the denier, and the hawalah is not an admission of the debt against him, because it can occur without it. His statement: (If the assignor demands from the assignee that which he assigned to him, and says: 'I only assigned to you so that you may collect it for me,' and the assignee says: 'You assigned to me for a debt I have against you,' then the statement is the statement of the assignor with his oath, because the assignee is claiming a debt against him, and he is denying. And the word 'hawalah' is used for agency, so the statement is his statement with his oath. If he swears, he takes the thousand dirhams that were received, and the assignee is not believed regarding what he claimed of the debt except with evidence.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the matn followed by its commentary. The text states that if the assignor (muḥīl) claims that he assigned the debt to the assignee (muḥtāl) merely for collection (i.e., as an agent), and the assignee claims it was a transfer of debt (hawalah) for a debt owed to him, then the assignor's word is taken with his oath. The Hanafi reasoning is that the assignee is claiming a debt against the assignor, who denies it. The principle is that the burden of proof is on the claimant, and the denial is accepted with oath. The commentator adds that the term 'hawalah' can be used for agency, so the assignor's interpretation as agency is plausible. If the assignor swears, he retains the money collected. The assignee cannot prove his claim of a debt without evidence. The passage also covers a related scenario: if the assignor claims a debt from the assignee who denies, the denial is accepted (with oath), and the hawalah itself is not considered an admission of that debt since it could be for other purposes.

Key Terms
الحوالة (al-ḥawālah): A contract transferring a debt from the debtor to a third party, or from the creditor to another person.
المحيل (al-muḥīl): The assignor who transfers the debt or appoints an agent to collect.
المحتال (al-muḥtāl): The assignee who receives the transferred debt or acts as agent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The assignee's statement without oath
B) The assignor's statement with his oath
C) Both statements are rejected
D) The assignor's statement without oath

Key Takeaway
In a hawalah dispute, the burden of proof is on the claimant, so the assignor's denial is accepted with oath.

Definition and Permissibility of Sulh (Conciliation)
Defines sulh, its pillar, condition, and proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define sulh (conciliation) in Hanafi law, identify its pillar and condition, and cite the primary evidence for its permissibility.

Core Idea
Sulh is a binding contract to settle disputes, but only over compensable rights.

This lesson introduces the Book of Sulh from the Hanafi manual al-Quduri. You will learn the technical definition of sulh, its essential elements, and the conditions that determine what can be conciliated. We will also examine the proofs from Quran and Sunnah that establish its permissibility.

Guided Path
1
Define sulh linguistically and legally
Understand the derivation from musalahah (amicability after opposition) and the shariah definition as a contract to repel dispute by mutual consent, carried on disposition contracts.
2
Identify the rukn (pillar) of sulh
Locate the essential elements: offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul) specifically made for the purpose of sulh.
These are the constitutive parts that must be present for the contract to exist.
3
Identify the shart (condition) for the subject of sulh
Determine that the subject (al-musalah ‘anhu) must be property or a right for which compensation is permissible (e.g., qisas). Excluded are non-compensable rights like shuf‘a or kafala bi al-nafs.
This condition distinguishes valid from invalid conciliations.
4
Locate the proofs for permissibility
Find the three sources: Quran (4:128), Sunnah (hadith ‘al-sulh ja’iz…’), and Ijma (consensus of the Ummah).
The phrase ‘la junah’ indicates no sin, hence permission; the hadith explicitly allows sulh with the exception of making haram halal or vice versa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصلح هو مشتق من المصالحة وهي المسالمة بعد المخالفة. وفي الشرع: عبارة عن عقد وضع بين المتصالحين؛ لدفع المنازعة بالتراضي، يُحمل على عقود التصرفات. - وركنه: الإيجاب والقبول الموضوعان للصلح. وشرطه: أن يكون المصالح عنه مالاً، أو حقاً يجوز الاعتياض عنه، كالقصاص، بخلاف ما إذا كان حقاً لا يجوز الاعتياض عنه، كحق الشفعة والكفالة بالنفس. والدليل على جواز الصلح: الكتاب، والسنة، والإجماع. أما الكتاب: فقوله تعالى: {فَلَا جُنَاحَ عَلَيْهِمَا أَن يُصْلِحَا بَيْنَهُمَا صُلْحاً وَالصُّلْحُ خَيْرٌ} [النساء: 128]. وأما السنة: فقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: 'الصلح جائز بين المسلمين إلا صلحاً أحل حراماً أو حرم حلالاً'، وأجمعت الأمة على جوازه.
Book of Conciliation. It is derived from al-musalahah, which is amicability after opposition. And in the Shariah: it is an expression for a contract placed between the two conciliating parties to repel dispute by mutual consent, and it is carried on the contracts of dispositions. - Its pillar: offer and acceptance placed for conciliation. Its condition: that the subject of conciliation is property or a right that is permissible to give compensation for, like qisas, unlike if it is a right that is not permissible to give compensation for, like the right of preemption and surety for a person. The proof for the permissibility of conciliation: the Book, the Sunnah, and Ijma. As for the Book: His statement (the Quran 4:128): 'There is no blame upon them if they make a settlement between them, and settlement is best.' As for the Sunnah: His statement (peace be upon him): 'Conciliation is permissible among Muslims except a conciliation that makes lawful something unlawful or unlawful something lawful.' And the nation has agreed upon its permissibility.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage opens the Book of Sulh (Conciliation) by defining the term. It states that sulh is derived from musalahah, meaning amicability after opposition. In Islamic law, it is a contract between disputing parties to end their dispute through mutual consent. It is treated as a type of contract of disposition (like sale, etc.). Its pillar (rukn) is the offer and acceptance made specifically for sulh. Its condition is that the subject of conciliation must be property or a right that can be compensated for, such as qisas (retaliation), because in qisas, one can take blood money instead. Conversely, rights that cannot be compensated for, like the right of preemption (shuf'a) or surety for the person (kafala bi al-nafs), cannot be settled by conciliation because they are not transferable for compensation. The author then provides the proofs from the Quran (4:128), the Sunnah (hadith 'Conciliation is permissible...'), and Ijma, establishing the permissibility of sulh. This is the standard Hanafi position.

Key Terms
الصلح (al-sulh): A contract to end dispute by mutual consent, treated as a disposition contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the condition for the subject of conciliation?
A) It must be property only
B) It must be a right that can be compensated for, like qisas
C) It must be a debt
D) It must be a criminal matter

Key Takeaway
Sulh is a valid contract to end disputes over compensable rights, supported by Quran, Sunnah, and Ijma.

The Legal Definition and Rulings of Sulh (Reconciliation)
Opening of the chapter on Sulh; defines and gives evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the legal definition of sulh, identify its pillar and condition, and explain the evidence for its permissibility along with the three types.

Core Idea
Sulh is a binding contract to end disputes, permissible in all forms unless it violates shari'ah by making halal haram or vice versa.

This lesson introduces the chapter on sulh in al-Jawhara al-Nayyira. Sulh is a key mechanism for dispute resolution in Islamic law. The passage defines it linguistically and legally, gives its essential elements, and provides textual evidence. Understanding these foundations is critical for applying sulh correctly in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic and legal definition
Note that sulh is derived from musalahah (reconciliation) and is defined legally as a contract to end a dispute by mutual consent.
This distinguishes it from other contracts.
2
Determine the pillar and condition
The rukn is offer and acceptance intended for sulh. The shart is that the subject matter must be property or a right exchangeable for compensation.
Non-exchangeable rights (like shuf'a) cannot be the subject.
3
Locate the evidence for permissibility
Identify the Quranic verse (4:128), the hadith 'al-sulh ja'iz bayn al-muslimin', and the consensus of the ummah.
The hadith includes an exception that invalidates stipulations conflicting with shari'ah.
4
Understand the exception
A sulh that makes halal haram or haram halal is invalid. Examples: sulh on wine (haram in itself) or sulh forbidding sale of a slave (halal in itself).
Refer to the explanation from al-Hidayah.
5
Distinguish the three types
Sulh is permissible whether the defendant admits (iqrar), stays silent (sukut), or denies (inkar).
All are valid per the Hanafi school.

وصلح مع إنكار .
وكل ذلك جائز .
وقال عمر رضي الله عنه: رددوا الخصوم؛ لكي يصطلحوا، فإن فَصْلَ القضاء : يُورِثُ الضغائن (۱).
ومعنى قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام إلا صلحاً أحل حراماً»: هو الصلح على الخمر.
وقوله: (أو حرَّم حلالاً» وهو الصلح على عبد على أن لا يبيعه، ولا يستخدمه.
وفي «الهداية»(۲): الحرام المذكور هو الحرام لعينه، كالخمر، والحلال المذكور هو الحلال لعينه كالصلح على أن لا يطأ الضَّرَّة. قال رحمه الله تعالى: (الصلحُ على ثلاثة أضرب: صلح مع إقرار، وصلح مع سكوت، وهو أن لا يُقِرَّ المدعى عليه، ولا يُنكر، وصلح مع إنكار، وكل ذلك جائز).
وينظر البدر المنير ١٣٩/١٧.
(۱) مصنف عبد الرزاق (١٥٣٠٤) ، المصنف لابن أبي شيبة (٢٣٣٤٩).
.۱۹۲/۳ (۲)
Book of Sulh. Book of Sulh. It is derived from al-musalahah, al-musalamah, which is after conflict. In sacred law: it is an expression for a contract placed between the two parties to remove dispute by mutual consent; it is understood on the basis of transactional contracts. Its pillar: offer and acceptance intended for sulh. Its condition: that the subject of sulh be property or a right that is permissible to exchange, like qisas, contrary to when it is a right that is not permissible to exchange, like the right of pre-emption and surety of the person. The evidence for the permissibility of sulh: the Book, the Sunnah, and Ijma. As for the Book: His statement the Most High: 'There is no sin upon them if they make peace between them; and peace is better' (4:128). As for the Sunnah: His statement, peace be upon him: 'Sulh is permissible among Muslims except a sulh that makes permissible what is prohibited or makes prohibited what is permissible' (1), and the ummah has agreed on its permissibility. (1) Sunan Abi Dawud (3594), Sunan al-Tirmidhi (1352), and he said: Hasan. Sulh is of three types: sulh with admission; sulh with silence, which is that the defendant neither admits nor denies; and sulh with denial. All that is permissible. And Umar, may Allah be pleased with him, said: 'Send back the opponents so that they may reconcile, for the separation of judgment breeds grudges' (1). And the meaning of his statement, peace be upon him: 'except a sulh that makes permissible what is prohibited' is: the sulh on wine. And his statement: 'or makes prohibited what is permissible' is: the sulh on a slave that he not sell him nor use him. And in al-Hidayah (2): the prohibited mentioned is that which is prohibited in itself, like wine, and the permissible mentioned is that which is permissible in itself, like a sulh on not having intercourse with the co-wife. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'Sulh is of three types: sulh with admission, sulh with silence - which is that the defendant neither admits nor denies, and sulh with denial. All that is permissible.' And see al-Badr al-Munir 17/139. (1) Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq (15304), al-Musannaf of Ibn Abi Shaybah (23349). (2) 3/192.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage opens the Book of Sulh in al-Jawhara al-Nayyira, a commentary on al-Quduri's Mukhtasar. The author first gives the linguistic derivation: sulh comes from musalahah and musalamah, meaning peace after conflict. He then defines it legally as a contract placed between contesting parties to remove a dispute by mutual consent. The contract is categorized among transactional contracts (uqud al-tasarrufat). The pillar (rukn) is offer and acceptance intended specifically for sulh. The condition is that the subject matter must be either property or a right that can be exchanged for compensation, such as the right to qisas (retaliation). It cannot be a right that is not exchangeable, like the right of pre-emption (shuf'a) or surety of the person (kafala bi-nafs). The author then provides evidence: the Quranic verse from Surah al-Nisa (4:128) which declares peace is better; the prophetic hadith from Sunan Abi Dawud and Tirmidhi that sulh is permissible except one that makes halal haram or haram halal; and the consensus (ijma) of the Ummah. He then explains the exception: making haram halal refers to sulh on wine (since wine is inherently haram); making halal haram refers to sulh on a slave stipulating that he cannot be sold or used (since selling and using are inherently permissible). He cites al-Hidayah to clarify that the haram mentioned is that which is haram li-aynihi (haram in itself) and the halal is halal li-aynihi (halal in itself), giving an example of sulh on not having intercourse with a co-wife (which is halal in itself, so such a stipulation is invalid). The author then lists the three types of sulh: (1) with admission (the defendant admits the claim), (2) with silence (no admission or denial), and (3) with denial (the defendant denies the claim). He rules all three as permissible, citing a saying of Umar ibn al-Khattab that encourages reconciliation to avoid grudges. The passage is foundational for understanding the Hanafi view on sulh as a valid and recommended means of dispute resolution.

Key Terms
الصلح (al-sulh): A contract between parties to end a dispute by mutual consent, derived from musalahah (reconciliation).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many types of sulh are there according to the text?
A) Two: sulh with admission and sulh with denial
B) Three: sulh with admission, sulh with silence, and sulh with denial
C) Four: including sulh with counterclaim
D) Only one: sulh with mutual agreement

Key Takeaway
Sulh is a valid contract to end disputes, with the pillar being offer and acceptance and the condition that the subject is exchangeable property or right, and it is permissible even without admission.

The Three Types of Sulh and Their Permissibility
Classifies sulh into three types and rules all valid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three types of sulh according to the Hanafi school and explain why each is valid.

Core Idea
Sulh is valid whether the defendant admits, denies, or remains silent — any settlement ending dispute is permissible.

Sulh (compromise) is a noble act that ends disputes. The Hanafi school classifies sulh based on the defendant's response to the claim. This lesson unpacks the three types and their shared ruling of permissibility, supported by Quranic and prophetic evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of sulh
The passage explicitly names three types: sulh ma'a iqrar (with acknowledgment), sulh ma'a sukut (with silence, where the defendant neither admits nor denies), and sulh ma'a inkar (with denial).
The silence type is defined clearly: the defendant does not confess or deny the claim.
2
Note the ruling for all three
The author states 'wa kullu dhalika ja'iz' — all of that is permissible. This is the Hanafi position.
The ruling is mubah (permissible), though sulh is generally recommended.
3
Understand the proofs cited
The passage cites the Quranic verse 'wal-sulhu khayr' (Quran 4:128), a hadith restricting sulh that would make lawful what is unlawful, and the practice of Umar encouraging reconciliation.
The hadith limits sulh: it cannot legitimize the inherently forbidden (e.g., wine) nor restrict what is inherently lawful (e.g., marital rights).
4
Recognize the limits of sulh
The commentary explains that sulh is invalid if it involves an item inherently unlawful (like wine) or seeks to prohibit what is inherently lawful (like sulh not to have intercourse with a co-wife).
This distinction ensures sulh does not contradict Shariah principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصلح
الصلح على ثلاثة أضرب : صلح مع إقرارٍ .
وصلح مع سكوت وهو : أن لا يُقِرَّ المدعى عليه، ولا يُنكِرَه .
وصلح مع إنكار .
وكل ذلك جائز .
وقال عمر رضي الله عنه: رددوا الخصوم؛ لكي يصطلحوا، فإن فَصْلَ القضاء : يُورِثُ الضغائن .
ومعنى قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام إلا صلحاً أحل حراماً»: هو الصلح على الخمر.
وقوله: (أو حرَّم حلالاً) وهو الصلح على عبد على أن لا يبيعه، ولا يستخدمه.
وفي «الهداية»: الحرام المذكور هو الحرام لعينه، كالخمر، والحلال المذكور هو الحلال لعينه كالصلح على أن لا يطأ الضَّرَّة. قال رحمه الله تعالى: (الصلحُ على ثلاثة أضرب: صلح مع إقرار، وصلح مع سكوت، وهو أن لا يُقِرَّ المدعى عليه، ولا يُنكر، وصلح مع إنكار، وكل ذلك جائز).
Book of Sulh. Sulh is of three types: Sulh with acknowledgment, Sulh with silence - meaning the defendant neither acknowledges nor denies, and Sulh with denial. All of that is permissible. 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) said: 'Send the litigants back so that they may reconcile, for the separation of judgment breeds rancor.' And the meaning of his saying, peace be upon him, 'except a sulh that makes lawful what is unlawful' is sulh involving wine. And his saying 'or makes unlawful what is lawful' is sulh concerning a slave on condition that he not be sold or employed. In al-Hidayah: the prohibited mentioned is that which is inherently prohibited, like wine, and the lawful mentioned is that which is inherently lawful, like a sulh not to have intercourse with a co-wife. The author (may Allah have mercy on him) said: Sulh is of three types: Sulh with acknowledgment, Sulh with silence - meaning the defendant neither acknowledges nor denies, and Sulh with denial. All of that is permissible.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage from Kitab al-Sulh opens by defining the three categories of sulh (compromise/settlement) in Hanafi fiqh. The first is sulh ma'a iqrar (with acknowledgment by the defendant), the second is sulh ma'a sukut (with silence, where the defendant neither admits nor denies the claim), and the third is sulh ma'a inkar (with denial). The ruling is that all three are valid (ja'iz). The commentary then provides proofs: the statement of 'Umar encouraging reconciliation, a hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him) clarifying the limits of sulh (it cannot make the unlawful lawful nor vice versa), and a reference to al-Hidayah explaining that the prohibition in the hadith refers to sulh involving inherently unlawful items like wine, and the 'making lawful' refers to sulh that would prohibit what is inherently lawful, such as a sulh not to have intercourse with a co-wife. The author then reiterates the classification, stressing that all three types are permissible in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
صلح (sulh): Compromise or settlement between disputing parties to end litigation.
إقرار (iqrar): Acknowledgment by the defendant of the claim.
سكوت (sukut): Silence by the defendant, neither admitting nor denying the claim.
إنكار (inkar): Denial by the defendant of the claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, sulh is permissible in all three forms — with acknowledgment, silence, or denial — as long as it does not involve what is inherently unlawful or restrict what is inherently lawful.
