Sulh Based on Acknowledgment Treated as a Sale
Explains that sulh over acknowledged claim follows sale rules

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain when sulh is treated as a sale and identify the conditions that validate it.

Core Idea
Sulh over an acknowledged claim with property exchange is governed by sale rules, requiring clarity of consideration and mutual consent.

In Hanafi fiqh, not all settlements (sulh) are alike. When a settlement is based on the defendant's acknowledgment (iqrar) and involves an exchange of property (mal bi mal), it takes on the legal character of a sale. This means all the rules of sale apply, including pre-emption, options, and the prohibition of uncertainty. Understanding when sulh crosses into sale territory is crucial for correct contracting.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basis of sulh
Determine whether the sulh is based on acknowledgment, silence, or denial. The ruling changes accordingly.
Only acknowledgment-based sulh with property exchange triggers sale rules.
2
Check for property exchange
If the sulh involves an exchange of property (mal bi mal), it is treated as a sale. If it is for the exact thing claimed, it is merely taking possession.
Al-Khujandi's categorization helps: exact thing → possession; different thing → sale; less → reduction; more → surplus/riba.
3
Apply sale conditions
Once treated as a sale, all sale conditions apply: the consideration must be known, the seller must be able to deliver, and defects trigger return.
Ignorance of the consideration (jahl al-badal) invalidates the contract.
4
Validate ancillary rights
Pre-emption (shuf'a), option of inspection (khiyar al-ru'ya), and option of condition (khiyar al-shart) are established.
These are the same as in any sale of property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن وقع الصلح عن إقرارٍ : اعتُبر فيه ما يُعتبر في البياعات إن وقع عــن مال بمال .
أما مع الإقرار : فلا خلاف فيه؛ لإطلاق قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَالصُّلْحُ خير. النساء / ١٢٨.
وأما مع السكوت : فهو جائز عندنا ؛ لأن الساكت يجوز أن يكون مقراً، ويجوز أن يكون منكراً ، فإذا صالح حملنا ذلك على الصحة، دون الفساد.
:
وأما مع الإنكار : فهو جائز أيضاً عندنا ؛ لأنه موضوع لقطع الدعوى والمخاصمة، وذلك جائز.
:
* قوله: (فإن وقع الصلحُ على الإقرار اعتُبر فيه ما يُعتبر في البياعات إن وقع عن مال بمال) ؛ لوجود معنى البيع، وهو مبادلة المال بالمال في حق المتعاقدين بتراضيهما.
فتجري فيه الشفعة إذا كان عقاراً، ويُرد بالعيب، ويثبت فيه خيار الرؤية والشرط، وتُفسده جهالة ،البدل، وتُشترط القدرة على تسليم البدل. ـ قال الخُجندي: الصلح على عين ما يدعيه : قبض واستيفاء.
وعلى غير ما يدعيه : بيع وشراء.
وعلى أقل مما يدعيه : حطّ وإبراء.
وعلى أكثر مما يدعيه : فضل ورباً.
ثم الصلح على شيءٍ مجهول عن معلوم، أو مجهول: لا يصح.
If sulh occurs based on acknowledgment, then what is considered in sales is considered in it if it occurs for property in exchange for property. As for with acknowledgment: there is no disagreement on it, due to the general meaning of His statement 'And sulh is better' (4:128). As for with silence: it is permissible according to us, because the silent one could be acknowledging or denying, so if he makes sulh, we carry it to validity, not corruption. As for with denial: it is also permissible according to us, because it is established to cut off claims and disputes, and that is permissible. His statement: 'If sulh occurs based on acknowledgment, what is considered in sales is considered in it if it occurs for property in exchange for property' due to the presence of the meaning of sale, which is exchange of property for property between the parties with their mutual consent. So pre-emption (shuf'a) applies if it is real estate, it can be returned due to defect, the option of inspection and option of condition are established, ignorance of the consideration invalidates it, and ability to deliver the consideration is required. Al-Khujandi said: Sulh for the exact thing claimed is taking possession and receipt. For something other than what is claimed is sale and purchase. For less than what is claimed is reduction and release. For more than what is claimed is surplus and usury. Then sulh for an unknown thing in exchange for something known or unknown is not valid.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage details the legal implications of sulh when it is based on acknowledgment (iqrar). The commentator begins by noting that there is no disagreement among scholars regarding the permissibility of sulh with acknowledgment, citing the Quranic verse 'And sulh is better.' Then he reiterates the Hanafi position on sulh with silence and denial, which are also permissible. The core ruling here is that when sulh is based on acknowledgment and involves exchange of property (mal bil mal), it is treated exactly like a sale (bay'). Therefore, all the rules of sale apply: pre-emption (shuf'a) is established if the property is real estate, the buyer can return the item for a defect, the option of inspection (khiyar al-ru'ya) and option of condition (khiyar al-shart) are valid, ignorance of the consideration (jahl al-badal) invalidates the contract, and the seller must be able to deliver the consideration. Al-Khujandi's categorization further clarifies: if the sulh is for exactly what is claimed, it is merely taking possession and receipt (i.e., a settlement, not a true sale); if it is for something different, it is a sale; if it is for less, it is a reduction and release; if it is for more, it involves surplus and potentially usury, which must be avoided. Finally, the text states that sulh for an unknown item in exchange for a known or unknown item is invalid due to uncertainty.

Key Terms
شفعة (shuf'a): Pre-emption right of a co-owner to buy the share sold to an outsider.
خيار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'ya): Option of inspection: the buyer can return an item not seen at the time of contract.
جهالة البدل (jahl al-badal): Ignorance of the consideration (price or object) which invalidates the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It is treated like a gift
B) It is treated like a sale
C) It is treated like a release
D) It is invalid

Key Takeaway
Sulh based on acknowledgment and property exchange is a sale in effect, requiring known consideration, deliverability, and triggering all sale-related rights and conditions.

The Three Scenarios of Sulh: Acknowledgment, Silence, and Denial
Ruling on sulh with acknowledgment, silence, and denial, and its treatment as sale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on sulh with acknowledgment, silence, and denial, and identify when sulh takes the ruling of a sale.

Core Idea
Sulh with iqrar and property exchange is a sale; with silence or denial it remains valid, ending dispute.

The compromise contract (sulh) is a powerful tool to end litigation. In this lesson, we examine three scenarios based on the defendant's response: acknowledgment (iqrar), silence (sukut), and denial (inkar). We also see how sulh transforms into a sale when property is exchanged, and we explore the conditions that must be met for validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three defendant responses
Recognize iqrar (acknowledgment), sukut (silence), and inkar (denial) as the three possible reactions of the defendant to a claim.
The ruling depends on which response occurs.
2
Determine the ruling for each scenario
For iqrar: sulh is treated as a sale if property is exchanged. For sukut: sulh is valid. For inkar: sulh is valid because it is meant to end disputes.
The Quranic verse 'Wal-sulhu khair' is cited as general proof.
3
Identify when sulh is treated as a sale
When sulh occurs upon acknowledgment and involves property for property, it takes the ruling of a sale (bay'). All rules of sale apply: shuf'a, return for defect, options, etc.
The commentator elaborates on conditions like knowledge of substitute and ability to deliver.
4
Apply Al-Khujandi's categorization
Al-Khujandi classifies sulh: on the very thing claimed (receipt), on something else (sale), for less (reduction), for more (usury). This helps determine the nature of the transaction.
This categorization is critical for identifying riba issues.
5
Recognize additional treatments
Sulh for property in exchange for usufructs is treated as ijarah. Sulh for an unknown thing is invalid. For ribawi items, like-for-like and hand-to-hand exchange are required.
These rules prevent exploitation and ensure fairness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن وقع الصلح عن إقرار : اعتبر فيه ما يعتبر في البياعات إن وقع عن مال بمال.
أما مع الإقرار : فلا خلاف فيه؛ لإطلاق قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَالصُّلْحُ خير ﴾ النساء/128.
وأما مع السكوت : فهو جائز عندنا ؛ لأن الساكت يجوز أن يكون مقراً، ويجوز أن يكون منكراً ، فإذا صالح حملنا ذلك على الصحة، دون الفساد.
وأما مع الإنكار : فهو جائز أيضاً عندنا ؛ لأنه موضوع لقطع الدعوى والمخاصمة، وذلك جائز.
* قوله: (فإن وقع الصلحُ على الإقرار اعتبر فيه ما يعتبر في البياعات إن وقع عن مال بمال) ؛ لوجود معنى البيع، وهو مبادلة المال بالمال في حق المتعاقدين بتراضيهما.
فتجري فيه الشفعة إذا كان عقاراً، ويرد بالعيب، ويثبت فيه خيار الرؤية والشرط، وتفسده جهالة البدل، وتشترط القدرة على تسليم البدل. ـ قال الخجندي: الصلح على عين ما يدعيه : قبض واستيفاء.
وعلى غير ما يدعيه : بيع وشراء.
وعلى أقل مما يدعيه : حطّ وإبراء.
وعلى أكثر مما يدعيه : فضل ورباً.
ثم الصلح على شيء مجهول عن معلوم، أو مجهول: لا يصح.
وإن وقع عن مال بمنافع : فيعتبر بالإجارات.
وعلى شيء معلوم عن معلوم أو مجهول يصح.
وقوله: اعتبر فيه ما يعتبر في البياعات حتى لو كان المدعى ذهباً أو فضة، وبدل الصلح من جنسه: لا يجوز إلا مثلاً بمثل، ويشترط التقابض في المجلس.
If the compromise occurs with acknowledgment, then what is considered in sales is considered in it if it occurs for property in exchange for property. As for with acknowledgment: there is no disagreement about it due to the generality of His statement: 'And compromise is better' (Quran 4:128). As for with silence: it is permissible according to us because the silent one may be acknowledging or denying; if he compromises, we attribute it to validity, not invalidity. As for with denial: it is also permissible according to us because it is established to end litigation and dispute, and that is permissible. * His statement: (If the compromise occurs upon acknowledgment, what is considered in sales is considered in it if it occurs for property in exchange for property) due to the existence of the meaning of sale, which is exchange of property for property between the contracting parties by mutual consent. Thus pre-emption applies if it is real estate, it is returned due to defect, options of inspection and condition are established, ignorance of the substitute invalidates it, and ability to deliver the substitute is required. Al-Khujandi said: Compromise on the very thing claimed: taking possession and receipt. On other than what is claimed: sale and purchase. On less than what is claimed: reduction and release. On more than what is claimed: excess and usury. Then compromise for an unknown thing in exchange for a known or unknown thing: is not valid. And if it occurs for property in exchange for usufructs: it is treated like leases. And for a known thing in exchange for a known or unknown thing: is valid. His statement: What is considered in sales is considered in it even if the claim is gold or silver, and the substitute of the compromise is of its kind: it is not permissible except like for like, and hand-to-hand transfer in the session is required.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the commentary on Kitab al-Sulh. It addresses the validity of compromise (sulh) in three scenarios: with acknowledgment (iqrar), with silence (sukut), and with denial (inkar). First, when the compromise occurs with acknowledgment, it is treated as a sale if it involves exchange of property. The author cites the Quranic verse 'And compromise is better' as a proof for permissibility. When the defendant remains silent, the compromise is also valid because silence could indicate either acknowledgment or denial; we assume validity. When the defendant explicitly denies, the compromise is still valid because its purpose is to end litigation. The commentator then elaborates that when sulh is treated as a sale, all rules of sale apply: shuf'ah, return for defect, options of inspection and condition, and validity depends on knowledge of the substitute and ability to deliver. Al-Khujandi categorizes sulh into types: if on the very thing claimed, it is mere receipt; if on something else, it is a sale; if for less, it is a reduction; if for more, it involves usury. Sulh for an unknown thing is invalid. When sulh is for property in exchange for usufructs, it is treated as ijarah. The passage ends with a note that if the claim is gold or silver and the substitute is of the same type, it must be like for like and exchanged hand-to-hand in the session to avoid riba.

Key Terms
صلح (sulh): A contract to end litigation by mutual agreement, often involving exchange or waiver of claims.
إقرار (iqrar): Acknowledgment by the defendant of the plaintiff's claim.
سكوت (sukut): Silence of the defendant without acknowledgment or denial.
إنكار (inkar): Denial of the claim by the defendant.
الصلح على عين ما يدعيه (al-sulhu 'ala 'ayn ma yad'ihi): Compromise on the very thing claimed, considered receipt and fulfillment.
جهالة البدل (jahl al-badal): Ignorance of the substitute (amount or nature) in a contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a compromise (sulh) occurs with acknowledgment and involves property for property, how is it treated in Hanafi law?
A) As a gift (hibah)
B) As a sale (bay')
C) As a lease (ijarah)
D) As a release (ibra)

Key Takeaway
Sulh is flexible: with iqrar it becomes a sale; with silence or denial it remains valid to settle disputes, always guided by the purpose of ending litigation.

Sulh for Usufructs: Ijarah Treatment and Death Effect
Intra-Hanafi dispute on death's effect on sulh for usufructs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for sulh with usufructs and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on the effect of death, distinguishing between person-specific and non-person-specific usufructs.

Core Idea
Sulh for usufructs is like ijarah: time specification required; death's effect splits Hanafi masters.

This passage addresses a specific type of sulh (compromise) where the subject is usufructs (manafi') rather than tangible property. The commentator explains that such sulh is analogized to ijarah (lease), requiring a fixed time period. Then comes an important intra-Hanafi dispute: Does the death of a party invalidate the sulh? Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani treats it exactly like ijarah, so death terminates the contract. But Imam Abu Yusuf argues that sulh is distinct, and the effect of death depends on the nature of the usufruct.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling on treatment of sulh with usufructs
Note that the text states 'it is treated like leases' (i‘tubira bil-ījārāt). This analogy requires time specification (tawqīt).
This is the foundational principle: sulh for usufructs is not a sale but a lease-like contract.
2
State Muhammad's position on death
Muhammad treats sulh exactly as ijarah: death of either party invalidates it, and the claimant returns to his original claim proportionally.
For Muhammad, the analogy is complete.
3
State Abu Yusuf's contrasting position
Abu Yusuf holds that sulh is different from ijarah; death of the defendant does not invalidate it. For death of the claimant, the sulh remains valid for non-person-specific usufructs (e.g., dwelling, service), but becomes void for person-specific usufructs (e.g., riding, wearing).
Abu Yusuf distinguishes based on transferability of the usufruct to heirs.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The practical difference: under Muhammad, death always ends the sulh and revives the original claim; under Abu Yusuf, the sulh may continue for the benefit of the claimant or heirs, except for person-specific usufructs.
This affects how disputes are settled and the rights of heirs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وإن وقع عن مال بمنافع: اعتبر بالإجارات)؛ لوجود معناها، فيشترط التوقيت فيها.
ويبطل الصلح بموت أحدهما في المدة؛ لأنه إجارة، فإذا كان موته قبل الانتفاع بما وقع عليه الصلح رجع المدعي على دعواه، وإن كان قد انتفع بنصف المدة أو ثلثها: بطل من دعواه بقدر ذلك، ورجع على دعواه فيما بقي، وهذا قول محمد، جعله كالإجارة. وقال أبو يوسف الصلح مخالف للإجارة، فإذا مات المدعى عليه: لا يبطل الصلح وللمدعي أن يستوفي المدة بعد موته.
وكذا إذا مات المدعي لا يبطل الصلح أيضاً في خدمة العبد، وسكنى الدار، وزراعة الأرض، وتقوم ورثته مقامه في الاستيفاء، ويبطل في ركوب الدابة، ولبس الثوب، ولا تقوم ورثته مقامه في الاستيفاء؛ لأن الناس يتفاوتون فيه.
His statement: (And if it occurs for property in exchange for usufructs: it is treated like leases) due to the existence of its meaning, so specification of time is required. And the compromise is invalidated by the death of either party during the period because it is a lease. So if his death occurs before benefiting from what the compromise was made upon, the claimant returns to his claim. And if he had benefited for half the period or a third: his claim is voided by that amount, and he returns to his claim for the remainder. This is the saying of Muhammad, who treats it like a lease. And Abu Yusuf said: The compromise is different from a lease. So if the defendant dies, the compromise is not invalidated, and the claimant may complete the period after his death. Likewise, if the claimant dies, the compromise is also not invalidated in the service of a slave, dwelling in a house, and cultivating land, and his heirs stand in his place for receipt. And it becomes void in riding an animal and wearing a garment, and his heirs do not stand in his place for receipt because people differ in these.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the treatment of sulh when it involves property in exchange for usufructs (manafi'). The commentator states that such sulh is treated like ijarah (lease), hence time specification is required. The dispute arises on the effect of death of a party during the period. Muhammad al-Shaybani treats it exactly as ijarah: the sulh is invalidated by death, and the claimant can return to his original claim proportionally. Abu Yusuf, however, argues that sulh is distinct from ijarah; the death of the defendant does not invalidate it, and the claimant may still use the usufruct for the agreed period after death. Similarly, if the claimant dies, the sulh is not invalidated for usufructs that are not person-specific (like slave service, house dwelling, land cultivation) and the heirs can take his place. But for usufructs that are person-specific (like riding an animal or wearing a garment), the sulh becomes void because people differ in these and it cannot be transferred to heirs. This is the relied-upon position according to the text.

Key Terms
منافع (manafi'): Usufructs or benefits derived from an asset, such as service of a slave, dwelling, riding, etc.
توقيت (tawqit): Specification of a time period for the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Yusuf, what is the effect of the claimant's death on a sulh for the right to dwell in a house?
A) The sulh is invalidated because death ends all contracts.
B) The sulh is not invalidated; the heirs may dwell in the house for the remaining period.
C) The sulh is invalidated because dwelling is person-specific.
D) The sulh becomes a sale and the heirs must pay rent.

Key Takeaway
Sulh for usufructs requires time specification, and the effect of death differs between Muhammad (invalidates) and Abu Yusuf (continues except for person-specific usufructs).

Sulh on Usufruct: Ijarah Analogy and Death's Effect
Ruling and intra-Hanafi dispute on sulh with usufruct

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that sulh on usufruct follows ijarah rules and distinguish the positions of Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf on the effect of death.

Core Idea
Sulh on usufruct is ijarah in meaning; death voids it per Muhammad but not per Abu Yusuf.

This passage addresses a nuanced transaction: a compromise (sulh) where the settlement involves usufruct (manafi'), such as allowing the other party to use a property for a period. The Hanafi school treats such sulh like a lease (ijarah), requiring time specification. However, the effect of death on the contract is contested within the school. We will examine the general ruling and then explore the ikhtilaf between Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and its default classification
Read the matn: when sulh involves usufruct, it is treated according to ijarah. That means the contract must specify duration (tawqit) and follow other ijarah rules.
The passage also mentions that if the claimed property is gold or silver, the exchange must be like-for-like and hand-to-hand (as in sale of ribawi items).
2
Understand the analogy (talil) to ijarah
The commentator explains: 'because its meaning exists' – meaning the essence of ijarah (exchange of usufruct for compensation) is present in such a sulh. Therefore, the same conditions apply.
3
Extract Imam Muhammad's position on death
Imam Muhammad says: since it is ijarah, death of either party void the sulh. The plaintiff returns to his original claim proportionally to the remaining benefit.
If death occurs before any benefit, he reclaims all; if after partial benefit, the claim is reduced accordingly.
4
Extract Abu Yusuf's position and its nuances
Abu Yusuf argues sulh is distinct: it is a settlement to avoid litigation, not a pure ijarah. Therefore, death of either party does not void the contract automatically. The plaintiff (or his heirs) may continue to receive the benefit for the full term, except for personal services like riding or clothing, where heirs cannot substitute.
This difference hinges on whether the contract is fundamentally a transfer of ownership (like ijarah) or a waiver of rights (sulh).
5
Distinguish the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Muhammad: contract ends at death; under Abu Yusuf: contract survives for most usufructs. The student must apply the correct view when giving fatwa, noting that the later preference may lean toward Muhammad's analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصلح
وإن وقع عن مال بمنافع : فيُعتبر بالإجارات.
وعلى شيء معلوم عن معلوم أو مجهول يصح.
وقوله: اعتبر فيه ما يُعتبر فى البياعات حتى لو كان المدعى ذهباً أو فضة، وبدل الصلح من جنسه: لا يجوز إلا مثلاً بمثل، ويُشترط التقابض في المجلس.
قوله: (وإن وقع عن مال بمنافع: اعتبر بالإجارات)؛ لوجود معناها، فيشترط التوقيت فيها.
ويبطل الصلح بموت أحدهما في المدة؛ لأنه إجارة، فإذا كان موته قبل الانتفاع بما وقع عليه الصلح رجع المدعي على دعواه، وإن كان قد انتفع بنصف المدة أو ثلثها: بطل من دعواه بقدر ذلك، ورجع على دعواه فيما بقي، وهذا قول محمد، جعله كالإجارة. وقال أبو يوسف الصلح مخالف للإجارة، فإذا مات المدعى عليه: لا يبطل الصلح وللمدعي أن يستوفي المدة بعد موته.
وكذا إذا مات المدعي لا يبطل الصلح أيضاً في خدمة العبد، وسكنى الدار، وزراعة الأرض، وتقوم ورثته مقامه في الاستيفاء، ويبطل في ركوب الدابة، ولبس الثوب، ولا تقوم ورثته مقامه في الاستيفاء؛ لأن الناس يتفاوتون فيه.
وقال في الصلح على المنافع هلاك رب العين غير قاطع كذاك موتُ المدعي في الدار والعبد لا في الثوب والحمار وإن هلك الشيء الذي وقع الصلح على منفعته، أو استحق: بطل الصلح، بالإجماع.
Book of Sulh. If it occurs concerning property with usufruct, it is treated according to ijarah. And upon something known for something known or unknown, it is valid. His saying: 'Consider in it what is considered in sales, so that if the claimed property is gold or silver, and the substitute in the sulh is of the same type, it is not permissible except like for like, and hand-to-hand payment in the session is required.' His saying: 'And if it occurs concerning property with usufruct, it is treated according to ijarah' because its meaning exists, so timing is required in it. And the sulh becomes void by the death of either party during the term because it is ijarah. If the death occurs before benefitting from what the sulh was made upon, the plaintiff returns to his claim. If he had benefited for half the term or a third, the sulh becomes void to that extent from his claim, and he returns to his claim for the remainder. This is the saying of Muhammad, who made it like ijarah. Abu Yusuf said: 'Sulh is different from ijarah, so if the defendant dies, the sulh is not void and the plaintiff may complete the remaining term after his death.' Likewise, if the plaintiff dies, the sulh is not void also in the service of the slave, residence in the house, and cultivation of the land, and his heirs take his place in receiving the benefit. It becomes void in riding the animal and wearing the garment, and his heirs do not take his place in receiving because people differ in that. And he said regarding sulh on usufruct: the destruction of the owner of the asset is not terminating, similarly the death of the plaintiff concerning the house and the slave—not so for the garment and the donkey. And if the thing whose usufruct was subject to sulh is destroyed or is rightfully claimed, the sulh becomes void by consensus.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage is a ruling and an intra-Hanafi dispute. It begins with the matn: if a sulh (compromise settlement) is made for property that includes usufruct (manafi'), it is governed by the rules of ijarah (lease). This means time specification (tawqit) is required for validity, and the exchange must be like-for-like if the claimed item is gold or silver. The commentary explains that the sulh on usufruct is treated as ijarah because the meaning of ijarah is present. Therefore, death of either party during the term invalidates the contract, as per Imam Muhammad. He analogizes sulh to ijarah: if death occurs before any benefit, the plaintiff can reclaim his original claim; if partial benefit was taken, the claim is reduced proportionally. Abu Yusuf disagrees: sulh is not ijarah; it is a settlement to avoid litigation, so the contract survives death. The plaintiff (or his heirs) may continue to receive the benefit for the full term, except for personal services like riding or clothing, where heirs cannot substitute because enjoyment varies among individuals. The passage concludes by stating that if the subject asset is destroyed or legally claimed by another, the sulh becomes void unanimously.

Key Terms
صلح (sulh): A compromise settlement between disputing parties to avoid litigation.
منافع (manafi'): Usufruct or benefits derived from an asset, such as housing, riding, or cultivation.
إجارة (ijarah): A lease contract that transfers usufruct for a specified period in exchange for rent.
توقيت (tawqit): Specification of time duration, a condition for validity of contracts involving usufruct.
موت (mawt): Death, which can invalidate certain contracts like ijarah in the view of Imam Muhammad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The sulh continues and the heirs may take over.
B) The sulh becomes void and the plaintiff returns to his claim proportionally.
C) The sulh is unaffected and the term continues.
D) The sulh becomes void only if the defendant dies.

Key Takeaway
Sulh on usufruct is governed by ijarah rules, but the effect of death is contested: Imam Muhammad voids the contract, while Abu Yusuf preserves it except for personal usufructs.

The Dual Nature of Sulh in Denial and Silence
Explains dual nature of sulh in denial/silence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the dual legal nature of sulh (settlement) when it occurs after the defendant's denial or silence, distinguishing the defendant's perspective from the plaintiff's.

Core Idea
Sulh after denial or silence is a redemption of oath for the defendant and a sale for the plaintiff.

One of the most common forms of dispute resolution in Islamic law is sulh (amicable settlement). However, the legal nature of sulh changes depending on whether the defendant admits, denies, or remains silent. This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayyira explains that when the defendant denies or remains silent, the sulh has a dual characterization: for the defendant it is a way to avoid taking an oath, while for the plaintiff it is effectively a sale of his claimed right. Understanding this distinction is crucial for determining the correct legal effects, such as whether pre-emption applies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Identify the act being considered: sulh (settlement) made after the defendant's denial or silence. State its ruling according to the Hanafi school: it is valid (sahih).
The ruling is derived from the matn of al-Quduri.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The proof is rational ('aqli), based on the conflicting claims of the parties. The defendant alleges the claimed property is his, so he is not buying it; he is paying to avoid the oath and end litigation. The plaintiff considers the payment as compensation for his claimed right.
No textual (naqli) proof is cited; the reasoning is through istihsan or analysis.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
From the defendant's perspective, the sulh is not a sale because he does not transfer ownership; he merely pays to redeem himself from the oath. From the plaintiff's perspective, the sulh is a sale because he receives a payment in exchange for his alleged right.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
There is no significant dispute within the Hanafi school on this point. The ruling is agreed upon and considered part of the Zahir al-Riwayah.
The cross-madhhab status is muttafaq alayh (agreed upon).
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Consider: Plaintiff claims 1000 dirhams from defendant. Defendant denies, then they settle for 500 dirhams. For defendant, it is fidyat al-yamin; for plaintiff, it is mu'awada.
This distinction affects other rules like return on defect, shuf'a, etc.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والصلح عن السكوت والإنكار في حق المدعى عليه : لافتداء اليمين، وقطع الخصومة، وفي حق المدعي : بمعنى المعاوضة . وإذا صالح عن دار : لم تجب فيها شفعةٌ.
* قوله: (والصلح عن السكوت والإنكار في حق المدعى عليه: لافتداء اليمين، وقطع الخصومة، وفي حق المدعي: بمعنى المعاوضة)؛ لأن المدعى عليه يزعم أن الشيء المدعى على ملكه، فلا يكون المدفوع عوضاً عنه، وقد لزمته الخصومة، فجاز له الافتداء منها.
وأما المدعي: ففي زعمه أن الذي ادعاه: حق، وأن الذي يأخذه عوض حقه.
قوله: (وإذا صالح عن دار لم تجب فيها الشفعة)، يعني إذا كان عن إنكار أو سكوت.
وصورته: ادعى عليه داراً أو عقاراً، فأنكر، أو سكت، ثم صالحه على دراهم لم تجب فيها الشفعة؛ لأن المدعى عليه يزعم أن الدار لم تزل على ملكه، وأنه لم يملكها بالصلح، وإنما دفع العوض لافتداء اليمين، وقطع الخصومة، وله ذلك، وزَعْمُ المدعي: لا يلزمه؛ لأنه لا
And sulh concerning silence and denial, from the defendant's perspective, is to redeem the oath and end litigation; from the plaintiff's perspective, it is a kind of exchange. And if one makes a sulh concerning a house, pre-emption is not obligatory in it. His saying: 'And sulh concerning silence and denial, from the defendant's perspective, is to redeem the oath and end litigation; from the plaintiff's perspective, it is a kind of exchange' because the defendant claims that the claimed thing is his property, so what is paid is not compensation for it, but he is bound by litigation, so he is allowed to redeem himself from it. As for the plaintiff, he claims that what he claimed is his right and what he takes is compensation for his right. His saying: 'And if one makes a sulh concerning a house, pre-emption is not obligatory in it' means if it is concerning denial or silence. Its scenario: someone claims a house or real estate from him, he denies or remains silent, then he makes a sulh with him for dirhams; pre-emption is not obligatory in it because the defendant claims that the house has always been his property and that he did not own it by the sulh, but rather he paid the compensation to redeem the oath and end litigatio, and he has that right, and the plaintiff's claim is not binding on him because it is not...
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage provides a ruling and its rational proof. It explains the nature of sulh when it occurs after the defendant's denial (inkar) or silence (sukut), i.e., when he does not admit the claim. For the defendant, sulh is a means to avoid taking an oath (fidyat al-yamin) and to end the litigation (qat' al-khusuma). For the plaintiff, however, sulh is considered a sale (mu'awada) because he receives something in exchange for his claimed right. The commentary reinforces this: the defendant believes the claimed property is his, so he is not buying it back; he is simply paying to avoid the oath. The plaintiff believes his claim is valid, so he takes the sulh payment as compensation. The second part states that if such a sulh is made over a house (or real estate), the right of pre-emption (shuf'a) does not apply. The reason is that the defendant is not selling the house; he is paying to end litigation. The scenario is: the plaintiff claims a house, the defendant denies or remains silent, then they settle for a payment in dirhams. Since the defendant never admitted ownership transfer, shuf'a is not triggered. The text cuts off while explaining that the plaintiff's claim does not oblige the defendant.

Key Terms
سكوت (sukut): Silence by the defendant, neither admitting nor denying the claim.
إنكار (inkar): Denial by the defendant of the plaintiff's claim.
افتداء اليمين (iftida' al-yamin): Redemption from taking an oath by paying compensation.
قطع الخصومة (qat' al-khusuma): Ending the litigation or dispute.

Key Takeaway
Sulh after denial or silence is not a uniform transaction; it is redemption for the defendant and sale for the plaintiff.

Why Shuf'a Does Not Apply to Sulh in Denial or Silence
Explains why shuf'a does not apply in sulh over denial/silence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that shuf'a (pre-emption) does not apply when a sulh is made over real estate after the defendant's denial or silence, and explain the reason based on the absence of a sale.

Core Idea
Shuf'a does not apply to sulh after denial/silence because no sale occurs from the defendant.

The second part of the passage addresses a practical question: does the right of pre-emption (shuf'a) arise when a settlement (sulh) is made over a house after the defendant's denial or silence? The answer is no. The reason is that the defendant, in his view, is not selling the house; he is paying to avoid the oath. Since shuf'a requires a valid sale of property, and no sale has taken place from the defendant's side, the right of pre-emption is not triggered. This lesson examines the reasoning and its implications.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling
The ruling is clear: if a sulh is made over a house (or real estate) after denial or silence, shuf'a is not obligatory (lā tajibu fīhā shuf‘a).
This is part of the same passage, but a distinct ruling.
2
Describe the scenario
The scenario: Plaintiff claims a house from defendant. Defendant denies or remains silent. Then they settle for a payment in dirhams. The plaintiff now has the house and wants to keep it.
The plaintiff is the one who receives the house in the sulh? Actually careful: the passage says 'if one makes sulh concerning a house' - the owner is the defendant, but he is not selling. The plaintiff gets the house? Actually it's ambiguous: typically in sulh over denial, the plaintiff gets the property and pays something to the defendant? No, the passage: 'someone claims a house from him, he denies or remains silent, then he makes sulh with him for dirhams' - so the defendant pays dirhams to the plaintiff to settle. Then the plaintiff drops the claim. The house remains with defendant. So the sulh does not transfer ownership. Shuf'a would apply if the defendant sold the house, but he didn't. So the plaintiff's claim is dropped, but no sale. So shuf'a does not apply.
3
Identify the dalil (proof)
The proof is again rational: because the defendant claims the house has always been his property, and he did not transfer it via the sulh. He paid only to redeem the oath and end litigation. Therefore, there is no sale contract (bay‘) to which shuf'a could attach.
Shuf'a only arises from a sale of real estate.
4
Note the condition for shuf'a
Shuf'a requires a valid sale (bay‘ sahīh) of the property. Since the defendant is not selling (in his and the law's view), the condition is not met.
Even if the plaintiff considers it a sale, the defendant's perspective is legally binding because he is the owner and does not admit the claim.
5
Apply to a variation
If the defendant had admitted the claim and then settled, it would be a sale and shuf'a would apply. This contrast highlights the importance of the defendant's position.
The ruling is specific to denial/silence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والصلح عن السكوت والإنكار في حق المدعى عليه : لافتداء اليمين، وقطع الخصومة، وفي حق المدعي : بمعنى المعاوضة . وإذا صالح عن دار : لم تجب فيها شفعةٌ.
* قوله: (والصلح عن السكوت والإنكار في حق المدعى عليه: لافتداء اليمين، وقطع الخصومة، وفي حق المدعي: بمعنى المعاوضة)؛ لأن المدعى عليه يزعم أن الشيء المدعى على ملكه، فلا يكون المدفوع عوضاً عنه، وقد لزمته الخصومة، فجاز له الافتداء منها.
وأما المدعي: ففي زعمه أن الذي ادعاه: حق، وأن الذي يأخذه عوض حقه.

And sulh concerning silence and denial, from the defendant's perspective, is to redeem the oath and end litigation; from the plaintiff's perspective, it is a kind of exchange. And if one makes a sulh concerning a house, pre-emption is not obligatory in it. His saying: 'And sulh concerning silence and denial, from the defendant's perspective, is to redeem the oath and end litigation; from the plaintiff's perspective, it is a kind of exchange' because the defendant claims that the claimed thing is his property, so what is paid is not compensation for it, but he is bound by litigation, so he is allowed to redeem himself from it. As for the plaintiff, he claims that what he claimed is his right and what he takes is compensation for his right. His saying: 'And if one makes a sulh concerning a house, pre-emption is not obligatory in it' means if it is concerning denial or silence. Its scenario: someone claims a house or real estate from him, he denies or remains silent, then he makes a sulh with him for dirhams; pre-emption is not obligatory in it because the defendant claims that the house has always been his property and that he did not own it by the sulh, but rather he paid the compensation to redeem the oath and end litigatio, and he has that right, and the plaintiff's claim is not binding on him because it is not...
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage provides a ruling and its rational proof. It explains the nature of sulh when it occurs after the defendant's denial (inkar) or silence (sukut), i.e., when he does not admit the claim. For the defendant, sulh is a means to avoid taking an oath (fidyat al-yamin) and to end the litigation (qat' al-khusuma). For the plaintiff, however, sulh is considered a sale (mu'awada) because he receives something in exchange for his claimed right. The commentary reinforces this: the defendant believes the claimed property is his, so he is not buying it back; he is simply paying to avoid the oath. The plaintiff believes his claim is valid, so he takes the sulh payment as compensation. The second part states that if such a sulh is made over a house (or real estate), the right of pre-emption (shuf'a) does not apply. The reason is that the defendant is not selling the house; he is paying to end litigation. The scenario is: the plaintiff claims a house, the defendant denies or remains silent, then they settle for a payment in dirhams. Since the defendant never admitted ownership transfer, shuf'a is not triggered. The text cuts off while explaining that the plaintiff's claim does not oblige the defendant.

Key Terms
شفعة (shuf'a): Pre-emption, the right of a co-owner to buy out a sold share.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does shuf'a not apply when a sulh is made over a house after the defendant's denial?
A) Because the house is not owned by the plaintiff.
B) Because the defendant is not selling; he is paying to avoid the oath.
C) Because shuf'a only applies to movable property.
D) Because the plaintiff has no right to claim.

Key Takeaway
Shuf'a (pre-emption) does not arise from a sulh after denial/silence because there is no sale from the defendant.

Sulh: Acknowledgment vs. Denial — Effects on Pre-emption and Recovery
Explaining the legal effects of sulh based on acknowledgment vs. denial/silence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish sulh on acknowledgment from sulh on denial/silence and apply the correct rulings on pre-emption (shuf'a) and recovery upon loss of the subject matter.

Core Idea
Sulh on acknowledgment is a sale; sulh on denial/silence is a ransom — different nature, different legal effects.

This lesson dives into the Hanafi rules on compromise (sulh). The key is whether the defendant acknowledges the claim or denies/remains silent. This distinction determines if the settlement is treated as a sale (with pre-emption rights and proportional recovery) or as a procedural ransom (with no pre-emption and revival of the original claim upon loss). Understanding this logic is essential for correctly applying the rulings in property disputes.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the bases of sulh
Identify whether the sulh is based on acknowledgment (iqrar) or denial/silence (inkar/sukut). This classification is the foundation for all subsequent rulings.
2
Understand the legal nature from each party's perspective
For the defendant in denial/silence, the payment is a ransom to avoid the oath and end litigation; he never concedes ownership. For the plaintiff, he treats the payment as an exchange for his claim. In acknowledgment, both parties treat it as a sale.
The defendant's view is legally decisive because the plaintiff's claim is not proven.
3
Apply the distinction to pre-emption (shuf'a)
Since sulh on denial/silence is not a sale, no pre-emption right arises. If the defendant later acknowledges, it retroactively becomes a sale and pre-emption applies.
Shuf'a only attaches to sales, not to ransoms.
4
Apply the distinction to loss of subject matter
If the subject matter is lost or claimed by a third party: for acknowledgment sulh (sale), the defendant recovers a proportional part of the consideration. For denial/silence sulh (ransom), the plaintiff's original claim revives and he must return the compensation.
The rationale: in a sale, loss of part calls for proportional return; in a ransom, the whole transaction fails and parties revert to original positions.
5
Synthesize the underlying principle
The nature of the transaction (exchange vs. procedural settlement) determines the legal consequences. Always ask: is this a real transfer of ownership or a payment to avoid litigation?
This principle is a clear example of Hanafi istihsan based on the parties' implicit intentions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصلح
والصلح عن السكوت والإنكار في حق المدعى عليه : لافتداء اليمين، وقطع الخصومة، وفي حق المدعي : بمعنى المعاوضة . وإذا صالح عن دار : لم تجب فيها شفعةٌ. وقال في الصلح على المنافع هلاك رب العين غير قاطع كذاك موتُ المدعي في الدار والعبد لا في الثوب والحمار وإن هلك الشيء الذي وقع الصلح على منفعته، أو استحق: بطل الصلح، بالإجماع.
* قوله: (والصلح عن السكوت والإنكار في حق المدعى عليه: لافتداء اليمين، وقطع الخصومة، وفي حق المدعي: بمعنى المعاوضة)؛ لأن المدعى عليه يزعم أن الشيء المدعى على ملكه، فلا يكون المدفوع عوضاً عنه، وقد لزمته الخصومة، فجاز له الافتداء منها.
وأما المدعي: ففي زعمه أن الذي ادعاه: حق، وأن الذي يأخذه عوض حقه.
قوله: (وإذا صالح عن دار لم تجب فيها الشفعة)، يعني إذا كان عن إنكار أو سكوت.
وصورته: ادعى عليه داراً أو عقاراً، فأنكر، أو سكت، ثم صالحه على دراهم لم تجب فيها الشفعة؛ لأن المدعى عليه يزعم أن الدار لم تزل على ملكه، وأنه لم يملكها بالصلح، وإنما دفع العوض لافتداء اليمين، وقطع الخصومة، وله ذلك، وزَعْمُ المدعي: لا يلزمه؛ لأنه لا يُصدق عليه، فلهذا لم تجب الشفعة.
ولهذا لو ظهر بالدار عيب لا يرجع بأرشه، ولا يردُّها؛ لأن في زعمه أنه لم يملكها من جهته.
* قوله: (وإذا صالح على دار وجبت فيها الشفعة)؛ لأن المدعي يأخذها عوضاً عن حقه.
- ومَن مَلَكَ داراً على وجه المعاوضة وجبت فيها الشفعة، ويأخذها الشفيع بقيمة الحق المدعى به ؛ لأن المصالح أخذها عوضاً عن ذلك الحق. ولو أقر المدعى عليه والمسألة بحالها وجبت الشفعة فيهما جميعاً، ويأخذ الشفيع كل واحدة منهما بقيمة الأخرى.
عنه :
قوله: (وإذا كان الصلح عن إقرار، فاستُحِق بعض المصالح رَجَعَ المدعى عليه بحصة ذلك من العوض؛ لأن الصلح إذا كان عن إقرار: كان معاوضةً، كالبيع.
* قوله: (وإن وقع الصلح عن سكوت أو إنكار، فاستحق المتنازَعُ فيه: رجع المدعي بالخصومة: أي مع المستحق، (ورد العوض)؛ لأن
Book of Sulh. Sulh based on silence and denial, from the perspective of the defendant: is to avoid the oath and to end the litigation; from the perspective of the plaintiff: is in the sense of an exchange. And if he makes a sulh regarding a house, there is no pre-emption right in it. And he said regarding sulh on benefits: the destruction of the owner of the asset is not cutting off, likewise the death of the plaintiff in the house and slave, not in the garment and donkey. And if the thing on whose benefit the sulh was made perishes or is claimed by another, the sulh becomes void by consensus. * His statement: (Sulh based on silence and denial, from the perspective of the defendant: is to avoid the oath and to end the litigation; from the perspective of the plaintiff: is in the sense of an exchange) because the defendant claims that the claimed thing is his property, so what is paid is not a compensation for it, and litigation has become incumbent on him, so it is permissible for him to ransom himself from it. As for the plaintiff, in his claim that what he claimed is a right, and what he takes is compensation for his right. His statement: (And if he makes a sulh regarding a house, no pre-emption is due in it) meaning if it is based on denial or silence. Its description: He claimed a house or real estate against him, and he denied or remained silent, then he made a sulh with him for dirhams, no pre-emption is due in it because the defendant claims that the house has always been his property, and that he did not own it by the sulh; rather, he paid the compensation to avoid the oath and to end the litigation, and he has that right, and the claim of the plaintiff is not binding on him because he is not believed against him, therefore no pre-emption was due. And for this reason, if a defect appears in the house, he does not recourse for the reduction in price nor does he return it, because in his claim he did not own it from him. * His statement: (And if he makes a sulh on a house, pre-emption is due in it) because the plaintiff takes it as compensation for his right. And whoever owns a house on the basis of an exchange, pre-emption is due in it, and the pre-emptor takes it for the value of the claimed right, because the settling party took it as compensation for that right. And if the defendant acknowledges while the situation remains, pre-emption is due in both of them together, and the pre-emptor takes each one of them for the value of the other. His statement: (And if the sulh is based on acknowledgment, and some of the subject matter of the sulh is claimed by another, the defendant recovers the corresponding share of the compensation) because if the sulh is based on acknowledgment, it is an exchange, like a sale. * His statement: (And if the sulh occurs based on silence or denial, and the disputed property is claimed by another, the plaintiff returns to the lawsuit: meaning with the rightful claimant, and returns the compensation) because
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage from the Book of Sulh in al-Jawhara al-Nayyira explains key rulings on compromise (sulh) based on whether it is founded on acknowledgment (iqrar) or denial/silence (inkar/sukut). The commentator expands on the matn of al-Quduri, clarifying the legal nature of each type and its consequences.

The first ruling establishes the general principle: For the defendant, sulh on denial or silence serves to avoid taking an oath and to end litigation. For the plaintiff, it is treated as an exchange (mu'awada) where he gives up his claim in return for compensation. The commentary explains that the defendant considers the claimed property as his own, so the payment is not a price but a ransom. The plaintiff, however, views it as compensation for his right.

A major consequence is the applicability of pre-emption (shuf'a). If sulh is made regarding a house based on denial or silence, no pre-emption right arises. The reason is that the defendant did not sell the house; he merely paid to avoid the oath. In his view, he never parted with ownership. Even if a defect appears, the defendant cannot claim an abatement because he never considered himself having sold the property. Conversely, if sulh is made on a house based on acknowledgment, pre-emption is due because it is a true exchange akin to a sale. The pre-emptor takes the house for the value of the claimed right.

Another ruling covers partial loss of the subject matter. If sulh is based on acknowledgment and part of the property is lost, the defendant recovers a proportional part of the consideration, just as in a sale. If sulh is based on denial or silence and the entire disputed property is lost or claimed by a third party, the plaintiff returns to his original lawsuit (i.e., his claim revives) and must return the consideration he received. The commentary begins explaining the reason but is cut off; the implied reasoning is that since the sulh was not a sale, the loss of the property invalidates the compromise and the parties revert to their original positions.

Throughout, the commentator uses logical reasoning (talil) to derive these rules from the fundamental distinction between sulh as a true exchange (when based on acknowledgment) and as a procedural settlement (when based on denial or silence).

Key Terms
صلح (sulh): A compromise or settlement of a dispute, often involving payment in exchange for dropping a claim.
شفعة (shuf'a): The right of pre-emption allowing a co-owner or neighbor to purchase property sold to a third party.
معاوضة (mu'awada): An exchange or commutative contract where each party gives something for the other's countervalue.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a defendant denies a claim and then settles by paying the plaintiff, does the plaintiff acquire a right of pre-emption (shuf'a) over the property in dispute?
A) Yes, because the plaintiff received payment in exchange for his claim.
B) No, because the defendant never conceded ownership and the payment is to avoid the oath.
C) Yes, because the settlement is a sale by nature.
D) No, only if the defendant acknowledges the claim.

Key Takeaway
The legal nature of sulh — exchange or procedural ransom — determines the applicability of pre-emption and the consequences of loss of the subject matter.

Full Recovery in Settlement Upon Denial: Restitution and Revival of Litigation
Ruling on full recovery of disputed property in settlement upon denial or silence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when the entire disputed property is recovered from a settlement made upon denial or silence, and explain the underlying reasoning (ta'lil) that distinguishes this case from settlements upon acknowledgment.

Core Idea
When settlement is on denial, recovery of the property voids the consideration – the right to sue revives.

In this passage, the jurist explains the legal consequence of a settlement made upon denial or silence when the entire disputed property is later recovered by a rightful claimant. Unlike a settlement upon acknowledgment (which is treated as a binding exchange), here the defendant paid only to avoid litigation, so if the property is reclaimed, the payment was without cause. Consequently, the plaintiff must return the consideration and regains the right to initiate litigation against the defendant. This distinction reflects the Hanafi school's nuanced approach to the nature of the settlement contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios of settlement
The text begins by stating the case: settlement occurs upon silence or denial. Contrast this with settlement upon acknowledgment, which has different rules discussed in earlier lessons.
Silence (sukut) is legally equivalent to denial (inkar) in this context.
2
Determine what triggers the ruling
The condition is 'the disputed property is recovered' – meaning it is taken from the plaintiff by a rightful owner through legal proof.
This is the istihqaq (recovery by rightful claimant) event.
3
State the dual obligation on the plaintiff
The plaintiff must both return the consideration (al-'iwad) received from the defendant and gets back the right to sue (khusumah). These two consequences occur together.
The return of consideration is wajib (obligatory) because the settlement is void ab initio regarding the recovered portion.
4
Understand the legal reasoning (ta'lil)
The passage provides the ratio: 'The defendant only paid the consideration to push away litigation from himself.' Since the property is taken away, it becomes clear there was no valid claim against the defendant, so the payment was without legal cause.
This ta'lil is the foundation for the rule and explains why preemption (shuf'ah) does not apply – the settlement is not a sale.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن وقع الصلحُ عن سكوت، أو إنكار ، فاستحق المتنازَعُ فيه : رجع المدعي بالخصومة، وردَّ العوض يُصدق عليه، فلهذا لم تجب الشفعة.
وإن استُحقَّ بعض ذلك : ردَّ حصته ، ورَجَعَ بالخصومة فيه.
المدعى عليه ما بذل العوض إلا لدفع خصومته عنه، فإذا ظهر الاستحقاق تبيَّن أنه لا خصومة له، فقد أخذ عوضاً عن غير شيء.  قوله: (وإن استحق بعض ذلك ردَّ حصته، ورجع بالخصومة فيه): أي في ذلك القدر.
If the settlement occurs upon silence or denial, and the disputed property is recovered, the plaintiff gets back the right to sue and returns the consideration; he is believed about it, so for this, preemption is not due. And if some of that is recovered, he returns its share and gets back the right to sue in it. The defendant only paid the consideration to push away litigation from himself, so when the recovery appears, it becomes clear that there is no litigation against him, so he took compensation for nothing. He said: (And if some of that is recovered, he returns its share and gets back the right to sue in it): meaning in that amount.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage deals with settlements made upon silence (sukut) or denial (inkar) of the claim. The rule is different from acknowledgment. Here, if the entire disputed property (mutanaza' fihi) is recovered by a rightful claimant, the plaintiff (who received the property via settlement) gets back the right to sue (khusumah) against the defendant, and returns the consideration (al-'iwad) he received. The commentary explains that this is because the defendant only paid to avoid litigation (to 'buy his peace'). If the property is reclaimed, it proves there was no valid claim against the defendant, so he should not have paid, and the plaintiff must return what he took. Preemption does not apply because the settlement is not an exchange. If only part is recovered, a proportional rule applies: the plaintiff returns the share of the consideration for that part and gets back the right to sue only in that part (i.e., the claim revives proportionally).

Key Terms
سكوت (sukut): Silence: the defendant does not respond to the claim.
خصومة (khusumah): Litigation: the right to pursue the claim in court.
استحقاق (istihqaq): Recovery: the rightful owner takes the disputed property through legal proof.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a settlement upon denial, what happens if the entire disputed property is recovered by a rightful owner?
A) The plaintiff keeps the consideration and loses the right to sue.
B) The plaintiff returns the consideration and loses the right to sue.
C) The plaintiff returns the consideration and regains the right to sue.
D) The plaintiff keeps the consideration and regains the right to sue.

Key Takeaway
In settlements upon denial or silence, full recovery of the disputed property obligates the plaintiff to return the consideration and revives the right to sue, because the defendant's payment was only to avoid litigation.

Partial Recovery in Settlement Upon Denial: Proportional Restitution
Ruling on partial recovery of disputed property in settlement upon denial or silence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for partial recovery of disputed property in a settlement made upon denial or silence, and calculate the proportional return of consideration and revival of litigation rights.

Core Idea
If part of the property is recovered, the settlement splits – proportional return and partial revival of suit.

The passage extends the ruling to a scenario where only a portion of the disputed property is recovered by a rightful claimant. In this case, the settlement is not wholly void; rather, it is void only to the extent of the recovered part. The plaintiff must return a proportional share of the consideration and regains the right to sue only for that portion. This nuanced rule maintains fairness and avoids unjust enrichment, consistent with the principle that the defendant's payment was only to avoid litigation on the claim.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the partial recovery condition
The text says: 'if some of that is recovered.' This means only a portion of the property that was the subject of settlement is taken by a rightful owner.
The ruling differs from full recovery – it is not all-or-nothing.
2
State the proportional obligation
The plaintiff returns 'its share' – that is, the portion of the consideration corresponding to the recovered part.
This is a proportional return (bi-hisab al-mustahaqq).
3
State the proportional revival of litigation right
The plaintiff regains the right to sue only 'in that amount' – i.e., only for the recovered portion.
The settlement stands for the unrecovered portion.
4
Connect to the ta'lil
The underlying reason applies partially: the defendant paid to avoid litigation on the whole claim. To the extent the claim is void, the payment for that part was without cause.
This ta'lil justifies the proportional rule.

وإن استُحقَّ بعض ذلك : ردَّ حصته ، ورَجَعَ بالخصومة فيه.
المدعى عليه ما بذل العوض إلا لدفع خصومته عنه، فإذا ظهر الاستحقاق تبيَّن أنه لا خصومة له، فقد أخذ عوضاً عن غير شيء.  قوله: (وإن استحق بعض ذلك ردَّ حصته، ورجع بالخصومة فيه): أي في ذلك القدر.

And if some of that is recovered, he returns its share and gets back the right to sue in it. Unspecified Claim in a House: Effect of Partial Recovery
Ruling with proof (ta'lil) on sulh and istihqaq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the return of consideration when an unspecified claim on a house is settled and part or all of the house is subsequently recovered, and explain the underlying reasoning.

Core Idea
For an unspecified claim, partial recovery does not trigger refund because the claim may still be satisfied by the remaining property.

This passage from the Book of Sulh (Settlement) addresses a nuanced scenario: a claimant makes an ambiguous claim on a house, reaches a settlement for some consideration, and later a third party recovers part of the house. The key question is whether the claimant must return any of the consideration. The Hanafi ruling, grounded in the possibility that the claim was in the remaining portion, provides a clear answer that distinguishes between partial and total recovery.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case and conditions
The case involves a claimant who asserts a 'right' in a house without specifying its nature or extent (e.g., not stating half or a third, nor a specific side). He then settles with the defendant for something. The settlement is valid under Hanafi law.
The right must be in the house itself, not a right of preemption (shuf'ah), as settlement on shuf'ah is invalid.
2
Understand the partial recovery scenario
If later some of the house is recovered by a third party (istihqaq), the claimant does not return any of the consideration he received. The reason: his claim could have been limited to the remaining portion, so the settlement stands for that part.
The recovery does not affect the settlement because the claim was unspecified; it is possible that the claim was satisfied by the property that remains.
3
Contrast with total recovery
If the entire house is recovered, the consideration becomes empty of any corresponding benefit. In that case, the claimant must return all the consideration he took.
This is because the basis of the settlement – the house – is entirely gone, leaving the consideration unsupported.
4
Analyze the reasoning (ta'lil)
The ruling is based on probability (ihtimal): since the claim was unspecified, it may have been satisfied by the portion not recovered. Thus, the settlement remains valid for that portion. When all is recovered, the probability is eliminated.
This is a rational argument (ta'lil) and not based on a specific textual proof from Quran or Sunnah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصلح
وإن ادعى حقاً في دار ولم يبينه ، فصولح من ذلك على شيء، ثم استحق بعض الدار : لم يَردَّ شيئاً من العوض؛ لأن دعواه يجوز أن تكون فيما بقي .
ولو استحق الكل : يرجع بما أخذه .
* قوله: (وإن ادعى حقاً في دارٍ ولم يُبيِّنه ، فصولح عنه على شيء، ثم استحق بعض الدار : لم يَردَّ شيئاً من العوض؛ لأن دعواه يجوز أن تكون فيما بقي).
بخلاف ما إذا استحق كلُّه ؛ لأنه يعرى العوض عند ذلك عن شيء يقابله، (فيرجع بكله).
وقوله: حقاً في دار: يعني حقاً في عين الدار، لا حقاً له بسبب الشفعة ؛ لأن الصلح على الشفعة لا يجوز.
وقوله : لم يبينه : أي لم ينسبه إلى جزء معلوم، كالنصف أو الثلث، ولا إلى جانب معلوم، كالشرقي أو الغربي أو القبلي.
فإِن نَسبه إلى جزء شائع، ثم استحق بعض الدار : نظر:
Book of Settlement. If he claims a right in a house and does not specify it, then he settles upon something, then some of the house is recovered: he does not return anything from the consideration, because his claim could be in what remains. If all is recovered: he gets back what he took. * He said: (If he claims a right in a house and does not specify it, then he settles upon something, then some of the house is recovered: he does not return anything from the consideration; because his claim could be in what remains). Contrary to if all is recovered; because then the consideration becomes empty of anything to oppose it, so he gets back all of it. And his saying: 'a right in a house' means a right in the house itself, not a right due to preemption, because settlement on preemption is not valid. And his saying: 'did not specify it' means he did not attribute it to a known part, like half or third, nor to a known side, like Eastern or Western or Qibli. So if he attributed it to a common share, then some of the house is recovered: consider:
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This is a continuation of the Book of Settlement. The matn addresses the scenario where a claimant claims a 'right' in a house without specifying its nature or extent (e.g., he says 'I have a right' but does not say half or a third). The defendant settles with him by giving him something (likely a sum of money or other property). Later, a third party recovers (istihaqq) part of the house. The ruling: the plaintiff does not have to return anything from the consideration he received. The reasoning: his original claim could have been in the remaining part of the house, so the settlement stands for that part. However, if the entire house is recovered, then the consideration is without any corresponding benefit (since the claim is completely gone), so the plaintiff must return all the consideration. The commentary clarifies two points: 1) The 'right' claimed must be a right in the house itself (like ownership, servitude, etc.), not a right of preemption, because settlement for shuf'ah is invalid. 2) 'Not specifying' means not attributing to a known fraction or direction. If he does specify a common share (e.g., half), then different rules apply, and the text hints at further discussion (نظر:).

Key Terms
مبهم (mubham): Ambiguous: a right not specified as to quantum or location.
استحقاق (istihqaq): Recovery by a third party who proves ownership or right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a claim on a house is unspecified and part of the house is recovered, what happens to the consideration?
A) The plaintiff returns a proportional part
B) The plaintiff returns the entire consideration
C) The plaintiff does not return anything
D) The lawsuit is reopened

Key Takeaway
For an unspecified claim in a house, partial recovery leaves the settlement intact; only total recovery triggers a refund of the entire consideration.

Ruling on Partial Recovery in Settlement (Sulh)
Explains consequence of partial recovery of settled property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when part of a settled property is reclaimed by a third party and identify the buyer's rights and obligations.

Core Idea
Partial recovery voids only the reclaimed portion; the buyer returns it and retains litigation rights.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from the Hanafi school on what happens when a third party successfully reclaims part of a property that was the subject of a prior settlement (sulh). The text, from a commentary on al-Quduri's Mukhtasar, directly addresses the buyer's obligation and right. Understanding this ruling is crucial for correctly handling title defects in transactions and avoiding confusion about the scope of voidness in settlements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its context
The act is a settlement (sulh) over property, followed by a third party's successful claim (istihqaq) to part of that property. Recognize that the passage assumes a valid settlement existed before the claim.
2
State the buyer's obligation
The text commands: 'he returns its portion' (ردَّ حصته). The buyer must return only the reclaimed portion to the seller, not the whole property.
This is a wajib (obligatory) act because the seller's delivery was defective regarding that portion.
3
Identify the buyer's right
The buyer 'goes back to litigation in it' (رجع بالخصومة فيه). This means the buyer retains the right to sue the seller for compensation regarding the reclaimed part, such as returning a proportional part of the settlement amount or damages.
The litigation right is limited to the reclaimed portion, not the entire settlement.
4
Distinguish from full recovery
Although not explicitly stated, the implication is that if the entire property is reclaimed, the whole settlement is void. The partial case is an exception where only the reclaimed part is affected.
This distinction prevents over-generalization.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن استُحقَّ بعض ذلك : ردَّ حصته ، ورَجَعَ بالخصومة فيه. قوله: (وإن استحق بعض ذلك ردَّ حصته، ورجع بالخصومة فيه): أي في ذلك القدر.
And if part of that is reclaimed: he returns its portion, and he goes back to litigation in it. His statement: (And if part of that is reclaimed, he returns its portion, and he goes back to litigation in it): meaning in that amount.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This is a commentary on a matn ruling. The ruling states that when a buyer receives property that is later partly reclaimed by a third party, the buyer must return the portion corresponding to the reclaimed part to the seller and retains the right to litigate regarding that part. The commentator explains that 'in it' refers to that specific reclaimed amount. This is based on the principle that the seller is only responsible for delivering what was sold, and if part fails, the buyer can seek recourse.

Key Terms
استحقاق (istihqaq): The act of being reclaimed or appropriated by a third party, indicating a defect in title.
حصته (hissatuhu): His portion, i.e., the specific part of the property that was reclaimed.
الخصومة (al-khusumah): Litigation, lawsuit, or legal proceeding to claim compensation or enforce rights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, if part of the settled property is reclaimed, the buyer must:
A) Return the whole property and get full consideration back.
B) Return only the reclaimed portion and retains the right to litigate regarding it.
C) Keep the property and sue the third party directly.
D) Return the reclaimed portion but waives any right to litigate.

Key Takeaway
When part of a property subject to a settlement is reclaimed, the buyer returns only that part and retains the right to litigate regarding it.

Settlement (Sulh) for Unknown Claims and Injuries
Introduces Book of Sulh with rulings on valid subjects and conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on sulh for an unknown claim and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument, and distinguish the rules for sulh in qisas vs diyah.

Core Idea
Sulh over an unknown right is valid; qisas is not property, allowing increase over diyah.

This lesson opens the Book of Sulh from al-Quduri's Mukhtasar with commentary. It clarifies when a settlement is valid despite an unspecified claim, and the different rules for settling injuries. Understanding these principles is key to resolving disputes without full litigation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Imagine a claimant asserts an unspecified right in a house and settles for a known sum. Later, a third party reclaims part of the house. The key issue is whether the defendant can get back some of the settlement amount.
2
Apply the Hanafi Rule
If part of the house is reclaimed, no return is made because the claim could have been in the remaining portion. If all is reclaimed, the consideration must be returned since it has no counterpart.
This reasoning is based on the fact that the defendant gave the consideration to end litigation; when the whole claim disappears, the consideration becomes baseless.
3
Note the Inter-Madhhab Dispute
The text states that settlement for an unknown right in exchange for a known thing is permissible in the Hanafi school, contrary to al-Shafi'i who requires the claim to be specified to avoid gharar.
The Hanafi school relies on practical need and the fact that the settlement itself resolves uncertainty.
4
Distinguish Between Types of Injuries
For unintentional injuries (khatā'), the settlement cannot exceed the fixed diyah because it is legally determined. For intentional injuries (qisas), increase over diyah is allowed because qisas is not property but a personal right evaluated by contract.
If the settlement is for something other than the fixed diyah amounts, increase is allowed but possession must be taken in the session to avoid debt-for-debt exchange.
5
Extract the Thamarah al-Khilaf
Practically, Hanafis allow settling an unspecified claim for a known amount, while Shafi'is require specification. Also, in qisas cases, Hanafis allow the victim to accept more than diyah, whereas in khatā' the amount is capped.
This affects how disputes are resolved extra-judicially.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصلح
وإن ادعى حقاً في دار ولم يبينه ، فصولح من ذلك على شيء، ثم استحق بعض الدار : لم يَردَّ شيئاً من العوض؛ لأن دعواه يجوز أن تكون فيما بقي .
ولو استحق الكل : يرجع بما أخذه .
المدعى عليه ما بذل العوض إلا لدفع خصومته عنه، فإذا ظهر الاستحقاق تبيَّن أنه لا خصومة له، فقد أخذ عوضاً عن غير شيء. قوله: (وإن استحق بعض ذلك ردَّ حصته، ورجع بالخصومة فيه): أي في ذلك القدر.

* قوله: (وإن ادعى حقاً في دارٍ ولم يُبيِّنه ، فصولح عنه على شيء، ثم استحق بعض الدار : لم يَردَّ شيئاً من العوض؛ لأن دعواه يجوز أن تكون فيما بقي).
بخلاف ما إذا استحق كلُّه ؛ لأنه يعرى العوض عند ذلك عن شيء يقابله، (فيرجع بكله).

إن بقي من الدار مقدارُ المُشاع أو أكثر : فلا رجوع للمدعى عليه بشيء من العوض، وإن بقي أقل.
منه :

وقوله : لم يبينه : فيه إشارة ودليل على أن الصلح عن المجهول على معلوم : جائز عندنا، خلافاً للشافعي.

قوله: (وجناية العمد والخطأ، إلا أنه لا تصح الزيادة على قدر الدية في الخطأ؛ لأنها مقدرة شرعاً، فلا يجوز إبطال ذلك.
بخلاف الصلح عن القصاص، حيث تجوز الزيادة على قدر الدية؛ لأن القصاص ليس بمال، وإنما يتقوم بالعقد.

Book of Sulh: And if someone claims a right in a house and does not specify it, and then settles regarding that for something, then part of the house is reclaimed: he does not return anything from the consideration, because his claim could be in what remains. And if all is reclaimed: he takes back what he took. The defendant only gave consideration to repel litigation against him; so when the recovery appears, it becomes clear that he has no litigation, so he took consideration for nothing. His statement: (And if part of that is reclaimed, he returns its portion, and he goes back to litigation in it): meaning in that amount. 
: 
* His statement: (And if someone claims a right in a house and does not specify it, and settles regarding that for something, then part of the house is reclaimed: he does not return anything from the consideration, because his claim could be in what remains). Contrary to when all is reclaimed; because then the consideration becomes devoid of a counterpart, so he returns it all. And his statement: 'right in a house' means a right in the house itself, not a right due to pre-emption, because settlement for pre-emption is not valid. If he attributed it to a common (undivided) part, then part of the house is reclaimed: we consider: if what remains of the house is the amount of the share or more: then there is no recourse for the defendant for anything of the consideration; if less remains: then the consideration is divided over all the disputed matter, and what corresponds to the reclaimed part is returned to the defendant, and what remains is his. His statement: (And intentional and unintentional injury, except that increase over the amount of the blood-wit (diyah) is not valid in unintentional injury because it is fixed by law, so its nullification is not permissible. Contrary to settlement for retribution (qisas), where increase over the amount of diyah is permissible because retribution is not wealth, but rather it is evaluated by contract. And this is if he settles for one of the fixed amounts of diyah; but if he settles for other than that: increase over the amount of diyah is permissible because it is an exchange for it, except that it is required to take possession in the session so that it does not become a separation of debt for debt.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage opens the Book of Sulh (Settlement) and is a commentary on the matn of al-Quduri. It first discusses a scenario where a claimant asserts an unspecified right in a house and then agrees to a settlement for a known sum. If later part of the house is reclaimed by a third party, the defendant (the original owner/settlor) does not get any return of the consideration because the claimant's unspecified right could have been in the remaining portion. However, if the entire house is reclaimed, the consideration must be returned because it is then without any counterpart. The commentator then explains that 'right in a house' means a right in the property itself, not a pre-emption right, as settlement for pre-emption is invalid. If the claimant specifies a known fraction (e.g., half) and part is reclaimed, the ruling depends on whether the remaining portion equals or exceeds that fraction; if it does, no return is made; if less, the consideration is apportioned proportionally. A key point is that settlement for an unknown right in exchange for a known sum is valid according to the Hanafi school, contrary to al-Shafi'i. Next, the text states that sulh is permissible for claims of money, benefits (e.g., right to service of a slave), and both intentional and unintentional injuries. However, in unintentional injuries, the settlement cannot exceed the fixed diyah because it is legally determined; any increase would invalidate the settlement. For intentional injuries (where qisas is involved), increase over diyah is allowed because qisas is not property but is valued by contract. If the settlement is for something other than the fixed diyah amounts, increase is allowed as an exchange, but the consideration must be handed over in the session to avoid a debt-for-debt exchange.

Key Terms
صلح (sulh): A settlement or compromise ending a dispute; a contract that resolves a claim without full litigation.
مجهول (majhool): Unknown or unspecified; here referring to the right claimed in the house that was not particularized.
قصاص (qisas): Retributive justice in cases of intentional homicide or injury, giving the victim or heirs the right to demand equivalent harm or accept monetary compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because qisas is a fixed amount like diyah.
B) Because qisas is not wealth but a personal right evaluated by contract, whereas diyah is legally fixed.
C) Because qisas requires court approval.
D) Because diyah is less than qisas.

Key Takeaway
Sulh is permissible for unspecified claims and intentional injuries, but for unintentional injuries the settlement cannot exceed the fixed diyah.
