General Permissibility of Sulh and Ruling on Unknown to Known
States the general ruling on sulh and a specific dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on sulh (compromise) from an unknown claim (majhul) to a known settlement (ma'lum) and identify the Shafi'i disagreement.

Core Idea
Sulh from unknown to known is valid in the Hanafi school, despite Shafi'i objection.

This passage from al-Quduri establishes the general permissibility of sulh in claims involving property, benefits, and injuries. It then addresses a critical point of cross-madhhab disagreement: whether a settlement where the claim is unspecified (majhul) but the compensation is specified (ma'lum) is valid. The Hanafi school, relying on istihsan, affirms its validity, while Imam al-Shafi'i invalidates it due to gharar (uncertainty).

Guided Path
1
Identify the scope of sulh
Note that sulh is permissible (ja'iz) for claims of wealth (amwal), benefits (manafi'), and both intentional and unintentional injury (jinayah). This establishes its broad applicability.
Sulh is a contract of compromise used to settle disputes.
2
Understand the partial acknowledgment scenario
When a defendant acknowledges part of a claim (e.g., shares in a house), the compensation is divided proportionally based on what remains after the acknowledged share.
This scenario illustrates how sulh is applied when the claim is partially known.
3
Grasp the unknown-to-known rule
The text explicitly states that sulh from an unknown (majhul) claim for a known (ma'lum) compensation is valid in the Hanafi school. This is the key ruling.
The unknown here refers to the specific claim; the compensation must be known.
4
Recognize the cross-madhhab dispute
This ruling is contested: Imam al-Shafi'i holds that such sulh is invalid due to gharar (excessive uncertainty). Understand the practical difference in application.
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi (relied upon for fatwa).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And compromise is permissible from claims of wealth, benefits, and intentional and unintentional injury. If there remains from the house the amount of the shared portion or more: then the defendant has no recourse for any of the compensation, and if less remains: then he divides the compensation on all the disputed property, so what falls to the person entitled: he returns it to the defendant, and what remains: it is for him. And his saying: he did not clarify it: in it is an indication and evidence that compromise for the unknown for a known: is permissible according to us, contrary to al-Shafi'i.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage states the general ruling that compromise (sulh) is permissible in claims involving property, benefits, and both intentional and unintentional injury. It then discusses a specific scenario concerning a disputed house where if the amount left after acknowledging the share of the third party is equal to or more than the disputed share, the defendant gets no compensation; otherwise, the compensation is divided proportionally. The author then highlights an important principle: sulh from an unknown (majhul) for a known (ma'lum) is valid in the Hanafi school, contrary to Imam al-Shafi'i. This is a key point of ikhtilaf between the schools.

Key Terms
الصّلح (al-sulh): A contract of compromise to settle a dispute by mutual agreement, often involving an exchange.
المجهول (al-majhul): That which is unknown in specification or quantity; here, the subject of a claim that is not precisely determined.
المعلوم (al-ma'lum): That which is known in specification and quantity; the known consideration in a sulh contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates sulh (compromise) even when the claim is unknown, as long as the settlement is known, prioritizing dispute resolution over strict avoidance of uncertainty.

Settlement on Claim of Slavery Is Valid as Manumission for Money
Explaining the ruling on settling a claim of slavery with payment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on settlement (sulh) in a claim of slavery, explain the legal construction from each party's perspective, and identify when wala' is established.

Core Idea
A settlement on a disputed claim of slavery is valid because each party's perspective makes it lawful.

This lesson unpacks a nuanced Hanafi ruling: when a claimant says 'you are my slave' and they settle for payment, the settlement is valid. It does not constitute a sale of a free person because each party's belief gives it a lawful basis. The claimant sees it as buying out his right (like manumission for money), while the defendant, believing himself free, sees it as ending a lawsuit. We will explore the conditions, the construction, and the implications for wala' (clientage).

Guided Path
1
State the ruling and its scope
Identify that the settlement is valid when the defendant is of unknown lineage. This condition prevents abuse against known free persons.
The text specifies 'majhul al-nasab' (unknown lineage) from al-Yanabi'.
2
Analyze the claimant's perspective
Understand that for the claimant, the settlement is like manumission for money ('itq 'ala mal). He takes payment to drop his claim of ownership, which is permissible.
This construction avoids treating the defendant as a commodity.
3
Analyze the defendant's perspective
For the defendant, who believes himself free, the payment is to end litigation. This is also permissible from his side.
Both perspectives are considered independently to validate the contract.
4
Identify the status of wala'
Note that wala' (clientage) is not established automatically because the defendant denies being a slave. Wala' only follows if the claimant proves slavery with evidence.
Wala' gives inheritance rights; its absence is a key practical consequence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن ادعى رجل على رجل أنه عبده، فصالحه على مال أعطاه : جاز، وكان في حق المدعي : في معنى العتق على مال . قوله : (وإذا ادعى على رجل أنه عبده فصالحه على مال أعطاه إياه: جاز)، يعني إذا كان المدعى عليه مجهول النسب. كذا في «الينابيع». * قوله: (وكان في حق المدعي في معنى العتق على مال)؛ لأنه أمكن تصحيحه على هذا الوجه في حقه؛ لأن في زعمه أنه يأخذ المالَ لإسقاط حقه من الرق، وذلك جائز. وفي زعم المدعى عليه أنه يُسقط عن نفسه الخصومة، وذلك جائز؛ لأنه يزعم أنه حر الأصل. قال في «الهداية» (۱): يكون في حق المدعي بمنزلة الإعتاق على مال، ولهذا يصح على حيوان في الذمة إلى أجل وفي حق المدعى عليه: يكون لدفع الخصومة، إلا أنه لا ولاء عليه؛ لإنكار العبد، إلا أن يقيم البينة أنه عبده، فتقبل، ويثبت الولاء.
If a man claims against another that he is his slave, and he settles with him for money he gives him: it is permissible, and in the right of the claimant, it is in the meaning of manumission for money. (Commentary:) His saying: 'If he claims against a man that he is his slave and settles with him for money he gives him: it is permissible' means if the defendant is of unknown lineage. Thus in 'al-Yanabi'. His saying: 'And in the right of the claimant it is in the meaning of manumission for money' is because it is possible to correct it in this way regarding him; because in his belief he takes the money to drop his right of ownership, and that is permissible. And in the belief of the defendant, he drops the litigation from himself, and that is permissible; because he believes himself to be free-born. He said in al-Hidayah: It is in the right of the claimant like manumission for money, and therefore it is valid on an animal in the dhimmah for a term, and in the right of the defendant, it is for repelling litigation, except that there is no wala' upon him due to the slave's denial, unless he establishes evidence that he is his slave, then it is accepted and wala' is established.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on settlement (sulh) in a dispute over ownership of a person. The claimant asserts that the defendant is his slave, and they agree on a payment to resolve the claim. The ruling states that such a settlement is valid. The key point is that each party's perspective makes the transaction correct: the claimant sees it as buying out his right to enslave (similar to manumission for money), while the defendant, who believes himself free, sees it as paying to end the lawsuit. The commentary from al-Hidayah notes that for the claimant it is like manumission for money, hence it is valid even for a future obligation on an animal; for the defendant it is purely to end litigation. However, wala' (the bond of clientage from manumission) does not automatically follow because the slave denies the claim, unless the claimant proves the slave's status, in which case wala' is established.

Key Terms
عتق على مال ('itq 'alā māl): Manumission of a slave in exchange for payment, valid as a contract between master and slave.
ولاء (walā'): The bond of clientage that establishes inheritance rights between the manumittor and the freed slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is wala' (clientage) not established in the settlement on a claim of slavery?
A) Because the defendant denies being a slave
B) Because the claimant did not intend to free him
C) Because the settlement is invalid
D) Because wala' is only for actual manumission

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates a settlement on a disputed claim of slavery by constructing it as manumission for money for the claimant and ending litigation for the defendant, with wala' only established if slavery is proven.

Settlement on a Debt: Partial Payment and Forgiveness, Not a Sale
Explaining that settlement on a debt is considered partial collection and release, not a commutative contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on settling a debt of the same genus for a lesser amount and explain why it is treated as partial payment and forgiveness, not a sale.

Core Idea
Settling a debt of the same genus for less is valid because it is partial collection and forgiveness, not a prohibited sale.

This lesson clarifies a key Hanafi principle: when a creditor agrees to accept less than the full debt of the same type (e.g., dirhams for dirhams), the transaction is not a sale subject to riba rules. Instead, it is legally recharacterized as the creditor collecting part of his right and forgiving the remainder. This avoids the prohibition of riba al-fadl. The distinction between same-genus and cross-genus settlements is critical, as cross-genus settlements are treated as commutative contracts (mu'awada).

Guided Path
1
Identify the Type of Debt and Settlement
Determine if the original debt and the settlement amount are of the same genus (e.g., both dirhams, both dinars, both wheat). The ruling depends on this.
Same genus means identical in type, even if quality differs (good vs. defective).
2
Apply the Hanafi Recharacterization for Same Genus
If the settlement involves the same genus, the transaction is not treated as a sale. Instead, it is as if the creditor received part of his due and forgave the rest.
This recharacterization avoids riba al-fadl, which would occur if it were a sale of unequal quantities.
3
Distinguish Cross-Genus Settlements
If the settlement involves a different genus (e.g., dirhams settled with a specific item), it is treated as a commutative contract (mu'awada) because no riba concern exists.
In such cases, the rules of sale apply, including the need for possession before separation (qabd) if required.
4
Recognize the Key Terminology
Note that 'mudayana' refers to a debt arising from a credit sale or loan. The ruling also applies to other debts like usurpation (ghasb), but the example uses mudayana because it is legitimate.
The term 'zuyuf' means counterfeit or defective dirhams – quality differences are ignored for the genus ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكلُّ شيءٍ وَقَعَ عليه الصلح، وهو مستحق بعقد المداينة : لم يُحمـل على المعاوضة، وإنما يُحمل على أنه استوفى بعض حقه، وأسقط باقيه . كمن له على رجل ألف درهم جياد ، فصالحه على خمسمائة زيــوف : جاز، وصار كأنه أبرأه عن بعض حقه . قوله: ( وكلُّ شيءٍ وَقَعَ عليه عقد الصلح، وهو مستحق بعقد المداينة : لم يُحمل على المعاوضة)؛ لأنه إذا ادعى على رجل دراهم، فصالحه على أقل منها : لم يُحمل على المعاوضة؛ لما فيه من الربا، (وإنما يُحمل على أنه استوفى بعض حقه، وأسقط باقيه). وإن صالحه على عين من الأعيان أو ادعى عيناً، فصالحه منها على دراهم جاز، ويُحمل على المعاوضة؛ لأنه لا يؤدي إلى الربا. وقوله: بعقد المداينة : يعني أن بدل الصلح إذا كان من جنس ما يستحقه المدعي على المدعى عليه بالعقد الذي جرى بينهما: فإن الصلح لا يُحمل على المعاوضة. والمداينة: البيع بالدين. - وإنما وَضَعَ المسألة في المداينة وإن كان الحكم في الغصب كذلك ؛ لأن الغصب غير مشروع. قوله: (كَمَن له على رجل ألف درهم جياد، فضالحه على خمسمائة زيوف: جاز ، وصار كأنه أبرأه من بعض حقه). وقبض الخمسمائة التي وقع عليها الصلح قبل التفرق : ليس بشرط.
And everything upon which a settlement is made while it is owed by a loan contract: it is not carried upon commutative exchange, but it is carried upon that he has collected part of his right and dropped the rest. Like one who has against a man one thousand good dirhams and settles with him for five hundred fake dirhams: it is permissible, and it becomes as if he released him from part of his right. (Commentary:) His saying: 'Everything upon which a settlement contract is made while it is owed by a loan contract: it is not carried upon commutative exchange' because if he claims dirhams against a man and settles for less than them, it is not carried upon commutative exchange due to what is in it of riba, and rather it is carried upon that he has collected part of his right and dropped the rest. And if he settles with him for a specific item, or claims a specific item and settles from it for dirhams, it is permissible and is carried upon commutative exchange because it does not lead to riba. His saying: 'by a loan contract' means that if the compensation of the settlement is of the same genus as what the claimant is owed by the contract that occurred between them, then the settlement is not carried upon commutative exchange. And al-mudayana: sale on credit. And he only placed the case in loan contract although the ruling is the same in usurpation because usurpation is not legitimate. His saying: 'Like one who has against a man one thousand good dirhams and settles with him for five hundred fake dirhams: it is permissible, and it becomes as if he released him from part of his right.' And taking possession of the five hundred that the settlement was made upon before separation is not a condition.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage explains a key principle in Hanafi law of settlement: when the subject of the settlement is a debt arising from a loan contract (mudayana) or any obligation of the same genus, the settlement is not treated as a sale or exchange (mu'awada) but rather as a partial collection of the original debt and forgiveness of the remainder. This is to avoid riba, since exchanging the same genus (e.g., dirhams for dirhams) at a different quantity would be riba al-fadl. The Hanafi school solves this by re-characterizing the transaction as a partial payment and forgiveness. If, however, the settlement involves a different genus (e.g., a claim for dirhams settled with a specific item like a garment), it is treated as a commutative contract (a sale) because no riba concern exists. The commentary clarifies that 'al-mudayana' means a sale on credit (debt). The ruling also applies to usurpation (ghasb) but is illustrated here with loans because usurpation is not a legitimate basis. The requirement of qabd (taking possession) before separation, which is necessary in a sale of different types, is not required here because it is not a sale; it is deemed as collection of part of the debt.

Key Terms
مداينة (mudāyana): A sale on credit that creates a debt; also refers to the resulting debt itself.
معاوضة (muʿāwaḍa): A commutative contract where one thing is exchanged for another, such as a sale.
زيوف (zuyūf): Counterfeit or defective dirhams of lesser quality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is a settlement treated as a commutative contract (mu'awada) rather than partial payment?
A) When the settlement is on a debt of the same genus
B) When the settlement involves a specific item (ayn) in exchange for dirhams
C) When the settlement is on a debt from a loan
D) When the settlement involves borrowing

Key Takeaway
Settlement on a debt of the same genus is valid as partial payment and forgiveness, not as a sale, to avoid riba.

Liability of the Agent in Sulh: Pure Remission vs. Exchange
Ruling on agent's liability in two types of sulh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between sulh over pure remission (blood/debt) and sulh over property exchange, and state the agent's liability in each case, including the effect of guarantee and recovery from the principal.

Core Idea
Agent's liability in sulh depends on whether it is a pure remission or an exchange; in an exchange, the agent is personally liable and recovers from the principal.

The liability of an agent (wakil) in a settlement (sulh) is not uniform; it hinges on the nature of the settlement. When the sulh is a pure remission—such as over blood money or a debt—the agent acts merely as a messenger and bears no financial responsibility unless he voluntarily guarantees payment. Conversely, if the sulh involves an exchange of property for property, the agent becomes the party to the contract and is personally liable for the payment, though he can later recover the amount from the principal (muwakkil). This lesson parses the key distinction and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of sulh
From the matn and commentary, extract the two scenarios: (1) sulh over blood of premeditated murder (dam 'amd) or over part of a debt claim—these are pure remissions; (2) sulh over property in exchange for property (mal bi mal).
Remember: 'Isqat mahd' (pure remission) vs. 'mu'awada' (exchange).
2
State the default liability rule for each type
For pure remission: the agent is not liable unless he guarantees. For exchange: the agent is liable because the rights of the contract transfer to him.
The matn's exception 'illa an yadmanah' applies to both, but in exchange the agent is already liable by default.
3
Understand the role of guarantee (daman)
If the agent guarantees the payment, he becomes personally bound to the other party. In pure remission, this creates liability where none existed. In exchange, it confirms his existing liability.
Guarantee is a separate act; it does not change the nature of the sulh.
4
Apply Al-Khujandi's extension
Al-Khujandi rules that if the agent guarantees, he recovers from the principal regardless of prior authorization. The order for sulh is treated as an order for guarantee. Similarly, in khul' (divorce by compensation), the agent recovers from the wife.
Compare with marriage agency where recovery requires explicit authorization to guarantee.
5
Distinguish from marriage agency
In marriage agency, the agent is not liable for the dower unless he explicitly guarantees it and is authorized. The difference is that khul' is valid without the wife's order (fuduli can do it), so the agent's recovery is easier.
This illustrates how the fiqh rules are tailored to the nature of each contract.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومَن وكل رجلاً بالصلح عنه ، فصالحه : لم يلزم الوكيـل مـا صـالحه عليه، إلا أن يَضمَنَه ، والمال لازم للموكل .
قوله : ومَن وكل رجلاً ليصالح عنه : لم يلزم الوكيل ما صالح عليه، إلا أن يضمنه، والمال لازم للموكل). يريد به إذا كان الصلح عن دم العمد أو كان الصلح عن بعض ما يدعيه من الدين؛ لأنه إسقاط محض، فكان الوكيل فيه سفيراً عن الموكل ومعبراً، فلا ضمان عليه، كالوكيل بالنكاح لا يلزمه المهر.
أما إذا وكله بالصلح عن مال بمال، بأن ادعى رجل عليه عروضاً أو عقاراً أو نحوهما ، فوكله بالصلح عنه على مال: فإن المال لازم للوكيل؛ لأن حقوق العقد هنا على الوكيل، دون الموكل، ويرجع بما ضمن على الموكل.
- قال الخجندي: الوكيل بالصلح إذا ضمن المال: يرجع على الموكل، سواء أمره بالضمان أو لم يأمره، وجَعَلَ الأمر بالصلح أمراً بالضمان. وكذا إذا أمرته المرأة بأن يخالعها من زوجها، ففعل: يعود عليها، ويكون الأمر بالخلع أمراً بالضمان.
بخلاف الوكيل بالنكاح إذا ضمن المهر للمرأة: فإنه لا يرجع به على الزوج إلا أن يكون أمره بالضمان.
والفرق: أن الخلع يجوز عليها بغير أمرها ألا ترى أن فضولياً لو قال للزوج : اخلع امرأتك على مائة من مالي فخلعها : جاز، فلما كان يجوز.
And whoever appoints a man to make sulh on his behalf, and he makes sulh: the agent is not liable for what he settled upon, unless he guarantees it, and the property is binding on the principal. His saying: (And whoever appoints a man to make sulh on his behalf: the agent is not liable for what he settled upon, unless he guarantees it, and the property is binding on the principal). He intends by this when the sulh is over blood of premeditated murder or over some of what he claims of debt; because it is a pure remission, so the agent is a messenger and expresser for the principal, so there is no liability on him, like the agent in marriage is not liable for the dower. But if he appoints him for sulh over property in exchange for property, such as a man claims goods or real estate or the like against him, and he appoints him for sulh over it for money: then the money is binding on the agent; because the rights of the contract here are on the agent, not the principal, and he recovers what he guaranteed from the principal. Al-Khujandi said: The agent for sulh, if he guarantees the money, he recovers from the principal, whether he ordered him to guarantee or not, and he considers the order for sulh as an order for guarantee. Likewise, if a woman orders him to make khul' (divorce by compensation) from her husband, and he does so, he recovers from her, and the order for khul' is an order for guarantee. Unlike the agent in marriage if he guarantees the dower for the woman: he does not recover it from the husband unless he ordered him to guarantee. And the difference: that khul' is valid without her order; do you not see that an unauthorized person (fuduli) if he says to the husband: divorce your wife on a hundred from my wealth, and he divorces her, it is valid, so since it is valid...
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage states the ruling on the liability of an agent (wakil) appointed for sulh. The matn says the agent is not liable for the settlement amount unless he personally guarantees it; the property is binding on the principal (muwakkil). The commentator explains the distinction between two types of sulh: (1) Suhh over blood money (dam 'amd) or a debt claim — here the agent is just a messenger, no liability, like a marriage agent not liable for dower. (2) Suhh over property in exchange for property — here the agent is liable for the payment because the rights of the contract transfer to him, and he can recover from the principal. Al-Khujandi adds that if the agent guarantees, he recovers from the principal regardless of prior authorization, treating the order as an implicit guarantee order. This is similar to khul', where the agent recovers from the wife if she orders him, unlike marriage agency where the agent must be specifically authorized to guarantee.

Key Terms
وكيل (wakil): An agent appointed to act on behalf of another (muwakkil) in a legal matter.
صلح عن دم (sulh 'an dam): Settlement for blood money in cases of premeditated murder, where the heirs agree to accept compensation instead of retaliation.
ضمان (damān): Guarantee or liability for payment; here, the agent's personal guarantee of the settlement amount.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which type of sulh is the agent automatically liable for the payment, even without a personal guarantee?
A) Sulh over blood money (dam 'amd).
B) Sulh over a debt claim.
C) Sulh over property in exchange for property.
D) Sulh over a marriage dower.

Key Takeaway
An agent's liability in sulh hinges on whether the settlement is a pure remission (no liability unless guarantee) or an exchange (automatic liability), with recovery rights from the principal.

Agent Liability in Settlement – When Guarantee Creates Recourse
Explaining when settlement agent is liable and his right of recourse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on agent liability in settlement (sulh) and distinguish between cases where the agent is liable and where he is not, based on the presence of guarantee (daman).

Core Idea
An agent is not liable in settlement unless he guarantees – the order to settle implies an order to guarantee.

Settlement (sulh) is a contract that can be either a pure relinquishment of a claim or an exchange of property. When a principal appoints an agent to settle on his behalf, the agent's liability depends critically on whether and how he guarantees the payment. This lesson unpacks that distinction and traces the agent's right of recourse against the principal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of settlement
First, determine whether the settlement is a pure relinquishment (isqat mahd), such as settling blood money or a debt claim, or an exchange (mu‘awadah), where property is given for property. This classification affects the agent's role.
Pure relinquishment: agent acts as mere messenger. Exchange: agent acts as principal in the contract.
2
Determine the agent's role
In pure relinquishment, the agent is a safir (intermediary) transmitting the principal's relinquishment. In exchange, the agent becomes the contracting party and assumes contractual rights and duties.
3
Apply the rule on guarantee
The agent is not liable for what he settles unless he guarantees (daman) the payment. If he guarantees, the property is binding on him and he may recover from the principal. Without guarantee, the principal remains liable.
Al-Khujandi adds: the order to settle itself is an order to guarantee.
4
Trace the right of recourse
When the agent guarantees and pays, he can recover from the principal regardless of whether the principal explicitly ordered the guarantee. This is analogous to khul‘ (divorce by compensation) where the wife's order to effect khul‘ implies a guarantee.
5
Compare with agency in marriage
Contrast with an agent in marriage: if the agent guarantees the dowry, he cannot recover from the husband unless the husband ordered the guarantee. The difference: khul‘ is valid without the wife's permission, but marriage is not valid without the husband's permission.
In marriage, the order only makes the contract valid, not create a guarantee.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصلح
ومَن وكل رجلاً بالصلح عنه ، فصالحه : لم يلزم الوكيل ما صالحه عليه، إلا أن يَضمَنَه ، والمال لازم للموكل .
قوله : ومَن وكل رجلاً ليصالح عنه : لم يلزم الوكيل ما صالح عليه، إلا أن يضمنه، والمال لازم للموكل). يريد به إذا كان الصلح عن دم العمد أو كان الصلح عن بعض ما يدعيه من الدين؛ لأنه إسقاط محض، فكان الوكيل فيه سفيراً عن الموكل ومعبراً، فلا ضمان عليه، كالوكيل بالنكاح لا يلزمه المهر.
أما إذا وكله بالصلح عن مال بمال، بأن ادعى رجل عليه عروضاً أو عقاراً أو نحوهما ، فوكله بالصلح عنه على مال: فإن المال لازم للوكيل؛ لأن حقوق العقد هنا على الوكيل، دون الموكل، ويرجع بما ضمن على الموكل.
- قال الخجندي: الوكيل بالصلح إذا ضمن المال: يرجع على الموكل، سواء أمره بالضمان أو لم يأمره، وجَعَلَ الأمر بالصلح أمراً بالضمان. وكذا إذا أمرته المرأة بأن يخالعها من زوجها، ففعل: يعود عليها،
ويكون الأمر بالخلع أمراً بالضمان.
بخلاف الوكيل بالنكاح إذا ضمن المهر للمرأة: فإنه لا يرجع به على الزوج إلا أن يكون أمره بالضمان.
والفرق: أن الخلع يجوز عليها بغير أمرها ألا ترى أن فضولياً لو قال للزوج : اخلع امرأتك على مائة من مالي فخلعها : جاز، فلما كان يجوز:
ففائدة أمرها الرجوع عليها بالضمان.
وكذا الأمر بالصلح أمر بالضمان ؛ لهذا المعنى، والنكاح لا يجوز على الرجل بغير أمره، ففائدة أمره جواز النكاح، لا ثبوت الرجوع، فلذلك افترقا.
الصرف.
Book of Settlement. Whoever appoints a man as agent to settle on his behalf, and he settles with him: the agent is not liable for what he settled upon, unless he guarantees it, and the property is binding on the principal. His statement: 'Whoever appoints a man to settle on his behalf...' means that if the settlement is for intentional blood money or for part of what he claims of debt, because it is a pure relinquishment, so the agent therein is an intermediary and transmitter for the principal, so there is no liability on him, like the agent in marriage is not liable for the dowry. But if he appoints him to settle property for property, such as a man claiming goods or real estate or the like from him, and he appoints him to settle for it for property: then the property is binding on the agent, because the rights of the contract here are on the agent, not the principal, and he recovers what he guaranteed from the principal. Al-Khujandi said: The agent in settlement, if he guarantees the property, recovers from the principal, whether he ordered the guarantee or not, and he made the order to settle an order to guarantee. Similarly, if a woman orders him to effect a khul' (divorce by compensation) from her husband, and he does so, it reverts upon her, and the order to effect khul' is an order to guarantee. Unlike the agent in marriage if he guarantees the dowry to the woman: he does not recover it from the husband unless he ordered the guarantee. The difference: khul' is permissible upon her without her order—do you not see that if an unauthorized person said to the husband: 'Divorce your wife for one hundred from my wealth' and he divorced her, it is valid. Since it is valid, the benefit of her order is recourse upon her for the guarantee. Similarly, the order to settle is an order to guarantee, for this reason. Marriage is not permissible upon a man without his order, so the benefit of his order is the validity of the marriage, not the establishment of recourse. Therefore they differ. Sarf.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage from Kitab al-Sulh (Book of Settlement) discusses the liability of an agent appointed to settle a claim. The author first gives the ruling: if someone appoints an agent to settle on his behalf, the agent is not liable for what he settles unless he guarantees the payment. The property is binding on the principal. The commentary explains that this applies when the settlement is for blood money (in intentional killing) or for a debt claim, because these are pure relinquishments (isqat mahd). In such cases, the agent acts merely as a messenger (safir) and transmitter, so he bears no liability, similar to an agent in marriage who is not liable for the dowry. However, if the agent is appointed to settle one property claim for another (e.g., goods or real estate) and the agent guarantees the payment, then the property becomes binding on the agent because the rights of the contract are on him. He can then recover what he paid from the principal. Al-Khujandi adds that the agent recovers from the principal whether or not the principal ordered the guarantee; the order to settle is itself an order to guarantee. This is analogous to khul' (divorce by compensation): if a wife orders an agent to effect khul' from her husband, the agent can recover from her. In contrast, an agent in marriage who guarantees the dowry cannot recover from the husband unless the husband ordered the guarantee. The difference is that khul' is valid even without the wife's permission (a third party can pay and effect khul'), so her order merely gives recourse. Marriage cannot occur without the man's permission, so his order only makes the marriage valid, not guarantee recourse.

Key Terms
وكيل (wakil): Agent appointed by principal (muwakkil) to perform a legal act on his behalf.
صلح (sulh): Settlement of a claim or dispute, often by relinquishment or exchange.
ضمان (daman): Guarantee; the agent's personal commitment to pay.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Al-Khujandi's opinion in the text, when can an agent in settlement recover from the principal?
A) Only if the principal explicitly ordered the guarantee
B) Always, because the order to settle implies an order to guarantee
C) Only if the settlement is for blood money
D) Only if the agent paid from his own pocket

Key Takeaway
An agent in settlement is not liable unless he guarantees, but the order to settle itself constitutes an order to guarantee in exchange settlements, giving the agent automatic recourse.

Unauthorized Settlement: The First Two Forms
Listing first two forms of unauthorized settlement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the first two forms of unauthorized settlement (sulh by fuduli) and state the ruling and liability for each form.

Core Idea
An unauthorized settler becomes personally liable if he guarantees the payment or specifies his own property.

This lesson examines the first two scenarios of settlement (sulh) carried out by a person without the principal's permission. The text presents two forms where the unauthorized agent explicitly guarantees the settlement amount or earmarks his own property. Understanding these forms clarifies when the settlement is valid and who bears the obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the context
The passage follows a discussion on sulh but now focuses on an unauthorized agent (fuduli) acting without the principal's consent. The agent is a volunteer (mutabarri').
The four forms are introduced; only the first two are detailed in this window.
2
Distinguish the two forms
Form 1: The agent says 'I settle for X on condition I am guarantor' or 'from my wealth' or 'upon me'. Form 2: The agent says 'I settle for this specific property of mine' (e.g., 'this thousand of mine' or 'this slave of mine').
Both forms involve the agent taking personal responsibility.
3
Analyze the first form: guarantee
The agent's statement implies a guarantee (daman). The settlement is valid and the money is binding on the agent. He becomes a debtor for that amount.
The agent cannot recover from the claimant; he only gets what he holds (subject of the claim? Context: likely he has no recourse).
4
Analyze the second form: specific property
The agent attributes the settlement to a specific item of his own property (e.g., 'this thousand of mine'). The settlement is valid and the agent must deliver that specific item.
Attribution to his own wealth makes delivery obligatory.
5
Note the legal consequence
In both forms, the agent is a volunteer (mutabarri'). He bears the liability without right to claim from the principal or the claimant.
This distinguishes from cases where the agent acts with permission.

Source Text

وقوله : (وإن صالح عنه على شيء بغير أمره: فهو على أربعة أوجه: إن صالح بمال، وضمنه : تم الصلح، ولزمه المال). يريد به أن يقول : صالحني من دعواك مع فلان على ألف على أني ضامن بها، أو قال: بألف من مالي، أو بألف علي، أو على ألفي هذه، فإذا فعل: فالمال لازم للوكيل؛ لأنه متبرع، ولا يكون له شيء من المدعي، وإنما له الذي هو في يده.
* قوله: (وكذلك إذا قال: صالحتك على ألفي هذه)، أو على عبدي هذا: (تم الصلح، ولزمه تسليمها؛ لأنه لما أضافه إلى مال نفسه: فقد التزم تسليمه، وهذا وجه ثان.
And His statement: 'If he settles on his behalf for something without his order, it is on four forms: if he settles with property and guarantees it, the settlement is valid and the property is binding on him.' He means that he says: 'Settle with me from your claim with so-and-so for one thousand on condition that I am guarantor for it,' or he says 'for one thousand from my wealth,' or 'for one thousand upon me,' or 'for this thousand of mine.' When he does so, the property is binding on the agent because he is a volunteer, and he has nothing from the claimant; rather he has what is in his hand. His statement: 'Similarly, if he says: I settle with you for this thousand of mine,' or 'for this slave of mine,' then the settlement is valid and he is bound to deliver it, because when he attributed it to his own wealth, he committed to deliver it. This is the second form.

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of settlement (sulh) but now focuses on an unauthorized agent (fuduli) who settles a claim without the principal's permission. The matn states that such a settlement falls into four forms. The commentary explains the first form: the unauthorized person settles for a sum of money and guarantees it (e.g., 'I settle for one thousand on the condition that I am guarantor' or 'from my wealth' or 'upon me' or 'this thousand of mine'). In this case, the settlement is valid, and the money is binding on the unauthorized person because he is a volunteer (mutabarri'). He has no right to claim from the claimant; he only gets what is in his hand (i.e., the subject of the claim? Actually the text says 'إنما له الذي هو في يده' meaning he only gets what he holds, but context unclear; likely he cannot recover from claimant). The second form: if he says 'I settle with you for this thousand of mine' or 'for this slave of mine,' the settlement is valid and he must deliver that specific item. The attribution to his own wealth makes him committed to delivery. The remaining two forms are not detailed in this window.

Key Terms
فضولي (fuduli): An unauthorized person who acts on behalf of another without permission.
متبرع (mutabarri'): Volunteer; one who undertakes an obligation without right to reimbursement.

Fourth Scenario of Third-Party Settlement Without Authorization
Explains four rulings on settlement by third party without authorization

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four scenarios of settlement (sulh) by a third party without the defendant's order and determine whether the settlement is immediately binding or suspended, using the conditions given by al-Quduri.

Core Idea
A settlement by a third party without authorization is either immediately binding if he guarantees payment, or suspended awaiting the defendant's ratification.

Settlement (sulh) is a common way to resolve disputes, but what happens when someone else steps in to settle on behalf of a defendant without being asked? This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayyira unpacks four distinct scenarios from al-Quduri's Mukhtasar, each with a different legal outcome. Understanding these scenarios helps you correctly advise clients on liability when a volunteer intervenes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four scenarios
Read the matn and Sharh to list the four scenarios described: (1) third party guarantees payment (daman), (2) third party attributes payment to his own specific property, (3) third party delivers payment immediately, (4) third party mentions payment but does not deliver or attribute.
Each scenario has a different legal consequence.
2
Determine the binding condition
For each scenario, note whether the settlement is immediately binding (complete) or suspended. The key factor is whether the third party has made himself personally liable or has secured the payment.
Scenarios 1-3 are complete; scenario 4 is suspended.
3
Analyze the suspended scenario
In scenario 4, the contract is mawquf (suspended) because the third party volunteered the contract but not the payment. The defendant's ratification makes it binding on the defendant; rejection voids it.
This parallels other mawquf contracts in Hanafi fiqh.
4
Note the fifth view
Al-Khujandi adds a fifth scenario where the third party attributes the settlement to himself personally, which is also valid. This shows that the list is not exhaustive.
This view is mentioned in the Sharh but not in the matn.
5
Connect to authorization
Understand that the right of the third party to recover from the defendant depends on whether he acted with the defendant's order. Without order, he is a volunteer; with order, he can recover.
This is explained at the end of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن صالح عنه على شيءٍ بغير أمره : فهو على أربعة أوجه : إن صالح بمال، وضَمِنَه : تم الصلح . وكذلك إن قال : صالحتك على الفي هذه : تم الصلح، ولزمه تسليمها . وكذلك لو قال : صالحتك على ألف، وسلمها إليه . وإن قال : صالحتك على ألف، ولم يسلمها إليه : فالعقد موقوف، فإن أجازه المدعى عليه : جاز، ولزمته الألف، وإن لم يُجِزْهُ : بَطَلَ . قوله: (وإن صالح عنه على شيء بغير أمره: فهو على أربعة أوجه: إن صالح بمال، وضمنه : تم الصلح، ولزمه المال). يريد به أن يقول : صالحني من دعواك مع فلان على ألف على أني ضامن بها، أو قال: بألف من مالي، أو بألف علي، أو على ألفي هذه، فإذا فعل: فالمال لازم للوكيل؛ لأنه متبرع، ولا يكون له شيء من المدعي، وإنما له الذي هو في يده. * قوله: (وكذلك إذا قال: صالحتك على ألفي هذه)، أو على عبدي هذا: (تم الصلح، ولزمه تسليمها؛ لأنه لما أضافه إلى مال نفسه: فقد التزم تسليمه، وهذا وجه ثان. * قوله: (وكذلك لو قال : صالحتك على ألف، وسلَّمها إليه)، وهذا وجه ثالث؛ لأن التسليم يوجب سلامة العوض له، فيتم العقد. * قوله : (وإن قال : صالحتك على ألف وسكت : فالعقد موقوف، فإن أجازه المدعى عليه : جاز، ولزمته الألف، وإن لم يجزه: بطل)، وهذا وجه رابع. وإنما وقف؛ لأن العاقد تبرع بالعقد، ولم يتبرع بالمال؛ لأنه لم يُضف المال إلى نفسه، فلم يلزمه، فإن أجازه المطلوب: لزمه المال، وإن لم يُجزه : بطل. وذكر الخجندي وجهاً خامساً، وهو أن يقول: صالحني من دعواك على فلان، بإضافة الصلح إلى نفسه، كما لو أضافه إلى المال: فيجوز. وبدل الصلح على المصالح، سواء كان بأمر المدعى عليه أو بغير أمره، وليس للمدعي على المدعى عليه سبيل، ويرجع المصالح بما ضمن على المدعى عليه إن كان الصلح بأمره، سواء أمره بالضمان أو لم يأمره، وإن كان بغير أمره: فإنه متبرع، فلا يرجع عليه.
If he settles on his behalf for something without his order: then it is on four aspects: If he settles for wealth and guarantees it, the settlement is complete. Likewise if he says: I settle with you on this thousand, the settlement is complete and delivery of it is binding. Likewise if he says: I settle with you on a thousand and delivers it to him. And if he says: I settle with you on a thousand and does not deliver it to him: then the contract is suspended; if the defendant ratifies it, it becomes valid and the thousand is binding on him; if he does not ratify it, it becomes void. His statement: (If he settles on his behalf for something without his order: it is on four aspects: if he settles for wealth and guarantees it, the settlement is complete and the wealth is binding on him). He means by it that he says: Settle with me for your claim against so-and-so for a thousand on the condition that I am guarantor for it, or he says: for a thousand from my wealth, or for a thousand owed by me, or on this thousand of mine; when he does so, the wealth is binding on the agent because he is a volunteer, and nothing is due to him from the claimant; rather what he has in his hand is his. * His statement: (Likewise if he says: I settle with you on this thousand of mine), or on this slave of mine: (the settlement is complete and delivery of it is binding on him; because when he attributes it to his own wealth, he has committed to deliver it; this is the second aspect. * His statement: (Likewise if he says: I settle with you on a thousand and delivers it to him), this is the third aspect; because delivery secures the countervalue for him, so the contract becomes complete. * His statement: (And if he says: I settle with you on a thousand and is silent: the contract is suspended; if the defendant ratifies it, it becomes valid and the thousand is binding, and if he does not ratify it, it becomes void), this is the fourth aspect. It is suspended because the contracting party volunteered the contract but did not volunteer the wealth; since he did not attribute the wealth to himself, it is not binding on him. If the defendant ratifies it, the wealth becomes binding; if he does not ratify, it becomes void. Al-Khujandi mentioned a fifth aspect: that he says: Settle with me for your claim against so-and-so, attributing the settlement to himself, just as if he attributed it to wealth: it is permissible. The settlement amount is on the settler, whether it was with the defendant's order or without his order; the claimant has no recourse against the defendant. The settler recovers what he guaranteed from the defendant if the settlement was with his order, whether he ordered him to guarantee or not. If it was without his order, then he is a volunteer and does not recover from him.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayyira explains a ruling from al-Quduri's Mukhtasar regarding settlement (sulh) made by a third party on behalf of a defendant without the defendant's prior authorization. The matn states four scenarios, and the sharh elaborates each. The first scenario: the third party settles by paying money and explicitly guarantees payment (e.g., says 'I am a guarantor' or 'from my wealth'). Here the settlement is immediately complete, and the payment is binding on the third party because he volunteered to guarantee; the claimant has no recourse against the defendant. The second scenario: the third party attributes the settlement payment to his own specific property (e.g., 'on this thousand of mine' or 'on this slave of mine'), making delivery of that specific property binding on him; the settlement is also complete. The third scenario: the third party says 'I settle on a thousand' and immediately hands over the money; the settlement is complete because actual delivery secures the countervalue. The fourth scenario: the third party says 'I settle on a thousand' but does not deliver and does not attribute the payment to himself; the contract is suspended pending the defendant's ratification. If the defendant ratifies, it becomes binding on the defendant; if not, it becomes void. The sharh then mentions a fifth view from al-Khujandi: if the third party attributes the settlement to himself personally (without mentioning wealth), it is valid, and the payment is on himself. Finally, the passage clarifies that whether the settlement amount is on the settler or on the defendant depends on authorization: if the settler acted with the defendant's order, he can recover from the defendant; if without order, he is a volunteer and cannot recover.

Key Terms
صلح (sulh): A contract of compromise/settlement between disputing parties, often involving payment to end a claim.
ضمان (daman): A guarantee or suretyship; here it means the third party takes personal responsibility for the payment.
موقوف (mawquf): Suspended; the contract is incomplete and awaits ratification by the defendant.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In scenario 4 of the passage, what is the status of the settlement if the third party says 'I settle with you on a thousand' but does not deliver or specify payment from his own property?
A) The settlement is immediately void.
B) The settlement is complete, and the third party must pay.
C) The settlement is suspended pending the defendant's ratification.
D) The settlement is valid if the claimant accepts.

Key Takeaway
A third party can validly settle a dispute without authorization if he guarantees payment, delivers payment, or attributes payment to his own property; otherwise, the settlement is suspended until the defendant ratifies.

Definition and Legal Evidence of Hibah (Gift)
Defining hibah and citing its primary evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of hibah and identify the two proofs from Quran and Sunnah that establish its permissibility.

Core Idea
Hibah is the transfer of ownership without compensation, validated by offer and acceptance alone.

This lesson introduces the Book of Hibah from the commentary on al-Quduri. You will learn the linguistic and legal definition of hibah, the primary evidence from the Quran and Sunnah, and why the text uses 'valid' instead of 'concluded' to distinguish it from sale. Understanding this foundation is essential for the subsequent rules on gifts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic and legal definitions
Read the first sentence: 'الهِبَةُ فِي اللُّغَةِ هِيَ: التَّبَرُّعُ. وَفِي الشَّرْعِ: عِبَارَةٌ عَنْ تَمْلِيكِ الْأَعْيَانِ بِغَيْرِ عِوَضٍ.' Note that the legal definition specifies 'tamlik al-a'yān' (transfer of tangible assets) and 'bi-ghayr 'iwad' (without compensation).
The term 'tamlik' means giving ownership.
2
Understand the Quranic evidence
The author cites Surah An-Nisa 4:4: 'فَإِن طِبْنَ لَكُمْ عَن شَيْءٍ مِّنْهُ نَفْسًا فَكُلُوهُ هَنِيئًا مَّرِيئًا'. The verse refers to a wife voluntarily giving up part of her dowry, implying that taking a gift is permissible. The commentary explains 'hanī'an' (pleasant, no sin) and 'marī'an' (wholesome outcome).
The verse is used as evidence for permissibility (jawāz) of accepting gifts.
3
Examine the Sunnah evidence
The author then mentions the hadith: 'تَهَادَوْا تَحَابُّوا' (Exchange gifts, you will love each other). This command indicates recommendation and permissibility of gifting.
The hadith is narrated with a good chain as per the footnote.
4
Analyze the phrasing 'sahha' vs 'in'aqada'
The matn states: 'الهِبَةُ تَصِحُّ بِالْإِيجَابِ وَالْقَبُولِ'. The commentator notes that for sale the term 'yan'aqidu' (is concluded) is used, but for hibah 'tasihhu' (is valid) is used. This is because hibah is completed by mere offer and acceptance, without requiring a formal contract structure like sale.
This highlights that hibah is a gratuitous transfer, not a commutative exchange.
5
Synthesize the legal implication
Conclude that hibah is a valid contract upon offer and acceptance, and it is permissible based on clear textual evidence. The definition excludes compensation, distinguishing it from sale.
Later rules may address delivery (qabd) for revocation, but validity is immediate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الهبة الهبة تصح بالإيجاب والقبول، ٥٩ الهبة في اللغة هي: هي: التبرع. وفي الشرع : عبارة عن تمليك الأعيان بغير عوض. وهي جائزة بالكتاب، وهو قوله تعالى: ﴿فَإِن طِبْنَ لَكُمْ عَن شَيْءٍ مِّنْهُ نَفْسًا فَكُلُوهُ هَنت امريا .. النساء / ٤ ، أي هنيئاً ، لا إثم فيه، مريئاً، لا ملامة فيه. وقيل: الهنيء: الطيِّبُ المَسَاغ، الذي لا ينقصه شيء، والمريء: المحمود العاقبة، الذي لا يَضُرُّ ولا يؤذي. وبالسنة : وهو قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: «تهادوا تحابوا»(۱). قال رحمه الله: (الهبة تصح بالإيجاب والقبول). إنما قال: تصح، وقال في البيع ينعقد : لأن الهبة تتم بالإيجاب (۱) قال الحافظ ابن حجر في التلخيص الحبير :٦٩/٣ : رواه البخاري في الأدب المفرد (٦٠٧) ، والبيهقي ١٦٩/٦ ، وأورده ابن طاهر في مسند الشهاب (٦٥٩)، اهـ وإسناده حسن.
Book of Gift (Hibah): The gift is valid by offer and acceptance. Hibah in language is: it is a donation. In Islamic law: it is the transfer of ownership of tangible assets without compensation. And it is permissible by the Book, which is His statement, the Exalted: "But if they willingly give up from it something of their own accord, then eat it with enjoyment and wholesome pleasure" (An-Nisa 4:4) i.e., pleasant, no sin in it, wholesome, no blame. And it is said: al-hanī' is the good and easy to swallow, that lacks nothing, and al-marī' is the praiseworthy outcome that does not harm or hurt. And by the Sunnah: which is his statement, peace be upon him: "Exchange gifts, you will love each other." He, may Allah have mercy on him, said: (The gift is valid by offer and acceptance). He only said "is valid" while in sale he said "is concluded" because the gift is completed by the offer... (footnote: al-Hafiz Ibn Hajar in al-Talkhis al-Habir 3/69: narrated by al-Bukhari in al-Adab al-Mufrad (607) and al-Bayhaqi 6/169, and Ibn Tahir mentioned it in Musnad al-Shihab (659), its chain is good.)
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage introduces the Book of Gift (Kitab al-Hibah) from the commentary on al-Quduri. It begins by defining hibah linguistically (a donation) and legally (transfer of ownership of tangible assets without compensation). The author then provides evidence from the Quran (an-Nisa 4:4, which refers to a wife giving up her dowry voluntarily) and the Sunnah (the hadith 'Exchange gifts, you will love each other'). The matn (original text) states: 'The gift is valid by offer and acceptance.' The commentator explains why the word 'sahha' (valid) is used instead of 'in'aqada' (concluded) as used for sale: because hibah is completed solely by offer and acceptance (without requiring a separate contract of sale). This distinction highlights that hibah is a gratuitous transfer, not a commutative contract.

Key Terms
الهبة (al-hibah): The transfer of ownership of tangible assets without compensation.
الإيجاب والقبول (al-ījāb wa al-qubūl): Offer and acceptance; the two components of a contract. For hibah, these are sufficient to conclude the gift.
تمليك الأعيان (tamlik al-a'yān): Transfer of ownership of physical, tangible assets.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the legal definition of hibah according to this passage?
A) A donation with compensation.
B) The transfer of ownership of tangible assets without compensation.
C) A loan without interest.
D) A sale with deferred payment.

Key Takeaway
Hibah is valid by offer and acceptance alone, as established by Quran and Sunnah, and is defined as transfer of ownership without compensation.

The Validity and Completion of Gift (Hibah): Offer, Possession, and Gift of Debt Dispute
Stating the ruling on gift contract formation and completion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of hibah, explain the role of possession in ownership transfer, and distinguish the gift of debt dispute between Abu Hanifa and Zufar.

Core Idea
A gift is valid by offer and acceptance, but ownership transfers only upon possession; forgiving a debt needs no acceptance from the debtor.

In this lesson, we explore the Hanafi contract of gift (hibah) as explained by al-Quduri and expanded by al-Jawharah al-Nayyirah. You will see how a gift differs from a sale and why possession is crucial for ownership. We also examine a special case: forgiving a debt and the difference between Abu Hanifa and Zufar.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi definition and ruling of hibah
From the passage, identify the linguistic and legal definitions of hibah, and the basic ruling that it is valid by offer and acceptance.
The matn uses 'tasihh' (valid) for hibah, not 'yan'iqid' (concluded) as for sale.
2
Identify the proofs from Quran and Sunnah
Locate the verse (Quran 4:4) and the hadith ('Give gifts, you will love each other') that establish the permissibility of hibah.
These proofs indicate desirability and validity, not obligation.
3
Explain the role of possession (qabd) in completion
Understand why the matn says hibah is completed by possession: ownership does not transfer until the donee takes possession, as possession substitutes for acceptance.
Al-Hidayah states possession is necessary to avoid imposing delivery on the donor without his full consent.
4
Analyze the practical implications of the ruling
Consider the example of an oath: if someone swears not to give a gift and makes an offer without acceptance, the oath is not broken because the gift is incomplete.
This distinguishes hibah from sale, where both offer and acceptance are needed for conclusion.
5
Evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute on gift of debt
Compare Abu Hanifa's view (no acceptance required for ibra') with Zufar's view (acceptance required) and note the practical consequence: the debtor's silence makes the ibra' effective unless rejected.
The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view, as indicated by the text's primacy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الهبة
الهبة في اللغة هي: التبرع.
وفي الشرع : عبارة عن تمليك الأعيان بغير عوض.
وهي جائزة بالكتاب، وهو قوله تعالى: ﴿فَإِن طِبْنَ لَكُمْ عَن شَيْءٍ مِّنْهُ نَفْسًا فَكُلُوهُ هَنِيئًا مَرِيئًا﴾ [النساء/٤]، أي هنيئاً، لا إثم فيه، مريئاً، لا ملامة فيه. وقيل: الهنيء: الطيّب المَسَاغ، الذي لا ينقصه شيء، والمريء: المحمود العاقبة، الذي لا يَضُرُّ ولا يؤذي.
وبالسنة : وهو قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: «تهادوا تحابوا»(١).
قال رحمه الله: (الهبة تصح بالإيجاب والقبول).
إنما قال: تصح، وقال في البيع ينعقد : لأن الهبة تتم بالإيجاب وتتم بالقبض.
وحده، ولهذا لو حلف لا يهب، فوهب، ولم يقبل الموهوب له: . أما البيع : فلا يتم إلا بهما جميعاً، حتى لو حلف: لا يبيع، فباع، ولم يقبل الآخرُ : لا يحنث، فلهذا استعمل لفظ: ينعقد في البيع.
* قوله: (وتتم بالقبض).
قال في «الهداية ) : القبض لا بدَّ منه لثبوت الملك؛ لأن الهبة عقد تبرع، وفي إثبات الملك قبل القبض : إلزام المتبرع شيئاً لم يتبرع به، وهو التسليم، فلا يصح. قال في «الينابيع»: القبضُ يقوم مقام القبول، حتى إنه إذا قال له: وهبت لك عبدي هذا ، والعبدُ حاضر ، فقَبَضَه : جاز وإن لم يقل : قَبِلتُ. وكذا لو كان العبد غائباً، فقال: وهبته منك، فاذهب فاقبضه، ولم يقل : قَبِلتُ، فذهب وقبضه : جاز.
ولو وهب الدين من الغريم أو أبرأه منه : لم يفتقر إلى القبول عند أبي حنيفة، ويرتد بالرد.
وقال زفر : يقف على القبول
فإن وهب لرجل ديناً على آخر ، وأذن له في قبضه منه، فقبضه : جاز؛ استحساناً.
The Book of Gift
Linguistically, gift is: voluntary donation.
And in sacred law: it is the transfer of ownership of tangible things without compensation.
It is permissible by the Book, i.e., the saying of Allah Most High: 'But if they give up willingly to you anything of it, then consume it with enjoyment and pleasure' [al-Nisa' 4:4], meaning with enjoyment, no sin in it; with pleasure, no blame in it. It is said: al-hani' is the good and palatable which lacks nothing, and al-mari' is the commendable consequence which neither harms nor hurts.
And by the Sunnah: i.e., his saying, peace be upon him: 'Give gifts, you will love each other.'
The author, may Allah have mercy on him, said: 'The gift is valid by offer and acceptance.'
He said 'valid' while he said for sale 'is concluded' because the gift is completed by the offer and completed by possession.
And this is its limit, so if someone swears not to make a gift, then he makes a gift and the donee does not accept, then... As for sale, it is only completed by both, so if someone swears not to sell, then he sells and the other does not accept, he does not break his oath. For this reason the term 'is concluded' is used for sale.
* His saying: 'And it is completed by possession.'
He said in al-Hidayah: Possession is necessary for confirmation of ownership because the gift is a gratuitous contract, and confirming ownership before possession would impose on the donor something he did not donate, i.e., delivery, so it is not valid.
He said in al-Yanabi': Possession stands in place of acceptance, such that if he says to him: 'I have given you this slave' and the slave is present, and he takes possession, it is permissible even if he did not say 'I accept.' Similarly, if the slave was absent and he said: 'I have given him to you, go and take possession' and he did not say 'I accept' and he goes and takes possession, it is permissible.
And if he gives a debt to the debtor or grants him immunity from it, it does not require acceptance according to Abu Hanifah, but it is nullified by rejection.
Zufar said: It depends on acceptance.
And if he gives a debt owed by another to a third party, and permits him to collect it, and he collects it, it is permissible by istihsan.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the ruling of gift (hibah) from the Matn of al-Quduri. The commentator first defines hibah both linguistically (as voluntary donation) and technically (as transfer of ownership of a tangible thing without compensation). He then provides proofs: Quranic verse 4:4, which is interpreted as permission to consume what is given willingly, and the hadith 'Give gifts, you will love each other,' which encourages the practice.
The author notes that the matn uses 'valid' (tasihh) for hibah while it uses 'concluded' (yan'iqid) for sale. The reason is that in a gift, the contract is completed by offer and possession, not acceptance alone, whereas sale requires both offer and acceptance. This has practical consequences: if someone swears not to give a gift and makes an offer without the donee's acceptance, the oath is not broken because the gift is not yet complete. In contrast, if someone swears not to sell and makes an offer without acceptance, he also does not break the oath. But the point is that the term 'concluded' is appropriate for sale because both parties must agree.
The commentary then expands on the role of possession (qabd). Citing al-Hidayah, possession is necessary for ownership to be established because a gift is a gratuitous contract; otherwise, it would impose delivery on the donor without his full consent. Al-Yanabi' adds that possession can substitute for acceptance: if the donor offers and the donee takes possession without verbal acceptance, the gift is valid. This applies whether the object is present or absent.
Finally, the passage discusses a special case: a gift of a debt from the debtor (i.e., forgiving the debt). According to Abu Hanifah, this does not require acceptance from the debtor; the gift is effective unless the debtor rejects it. Zufar disagrees, holding that acceptance is required. The text also mentions that if a gift of a debt is made to a third party with permission to collect, and he collects, it is valid by istihsan (juristic preference).

Key Terms
الهبة (al-hibah): Transfer of ownership of a tangible thing without compensation.
الإيجاب والقبول (al-ijab wa al-qabul): Offer and acceptance; the two declarations forming a contract.
القبض (al-qabd): Taking physical possession of the gifted item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does ownership of a gifted item transfer to the donee?
A) At the moment of the donor's offer (ijab)
B) At the moment of the donee's acceptance (qabul)
C) At the moment of possession (qabd)
D) Only after a court order

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a gift is valid by offer and acceptance, but ownership only transfers upon possession; forgiving a debt does not require acceptance.

Gift Completed by Possession: The Ruling and Its Rationale
Explanation of why possession is necessary for gift completion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a gift requires possession for ownership transfer and explain the rational proof from al-Hidayah.

Core Idea
A gift's ownership does not transfer until possession is taken; sale transfers by offer and acceptance alone.

This passage from the matn declares that a gift is completed only by possession. The commentator then provides a rational proof from al-Hidayah, distinguishing a gift from a sale. Understanding why possession is necessary helps grasp the nature of gratuitous contracts in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The passage states 'وتتم بالقبض' (it is completed by possession). Determine what 'completion' means: transfer of ownership, not validity of the contract.
The contract itself is valid upon offer and acceptance; qabd is a condition for milk (ownership).
2
Compare with sale
Note the contrast: sale is completed by offer and acceptance alone; gift requires possession. This is shown through the oath example.
If someone swears not to sell and sells, but the buyer does not accept, the oath is broken. For a gift, the oath is only broken if possession occurs.
3
Locate the proof
The commentator cites al-Hidayah's rational argument (dalil aqli): a gift is a gratuitous contract; imposing delivery before possession is an extra obligation not consented to.
The proof is based on the nature of tabarru' (gratuitous act).
4
Understand the wajh al-dalalah
The proof links the nature of tabarru' to the condition of qabd: since the donor only intends to give the item, not to oblige himself to deliver, ownership before possession would be an imposition.
The donor's consent does not extend to assuming the liability of delivery.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
The Hanafi view contrasts with Imam al-Shafi'i's, where qabd is required for the validity of the gift contract itself.
In Hanafi, the contract is valid without qabd; ownership simply does not transfer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الهبة
وتتم بالقبض .
وحده، ولهذا لو حلف لا يهب، فوهب، ولم يقبل الموهوب له : . أما البيع : فلا يتم إلا بهما جميعاً، حتى لو حلف: لا يبيع، فباع، ولم يقبل الآخرُ : لا يحنث ، فلهذا استعمل لفظ: ينعقد في البيع.
* قوله: (وتتم بالقبض).
قال في «الهداية ) : القبض لا بدَّ منه لثبوت الملك؛ لأن الهبة عقد تبرع، وفي إثبات الملك قبل القبض : إلزام المتبرع شيئاً لم يتبرع به، وهو التسليم، فلا يصح.
The Book of Gift. And it is completed by possession. It alone, and for this, if he swore not to give, and he gave, and the donee did not accept: As for sale, it is not completed except by both of them, so that if he swore: not to sell, and he sold, and the other did not accept, he is not guilty of perjury, therefore the word 'concluded' is used in sale. * His statement: (And it is completed by possession). He said in al-Hidayah: Possession is necessary for the establishment of ownership, because the gift is a gratuitous contract, and in establishing ownership before possession there is an imposition of something the donor did not donate, namely delivery, so it is not valid.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. It establishes that a gift (hibah) is not completed until possession (qabd) is taken by the donee. The author begins by stating the ruling: 'It is completed by possession.' Then he explains the practical implication: if someone swears not to make a gift and then makes an offer without possession, he does not break his oath because the gift is not complete. In contrast, in a sale, the contract is completed by offer and acceptance alone, so if someone swears not to sell and he sells (with offer and acceptance), he breaks his oath even if the buyer has not taken possession. The commentator then cites al-Hidayah, which provides the rational proof (dalil aqli): a gift is a gratuitous contract (tabarru'); therefore, the donor only intends to give the item itself, not to assume the obligation of delivery. If ownership were transferred before possession, it would impose an extra obligation (delivery) that the donor did not undertake, which is invalid. Thus, possession is a condition for the transfer of ownership.

Key Terms
القبض (al-qabd): Taking possession, either physically or constructively, of the gifted item.
الهبة (al-hibah): A voluntary transfer of ownership without compensation.
التبرع (al-tabarr'u): A gratuitous act or donation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does a gift require possession for the transfer of ownership?
A) Because it is a contract of exchange.
B) Because it is a gratuitous contract, and imposing delivery before possession would be an additional obligation not consented to.
C) Because the donor can change his mind.
D) Because the donee must accept.

Key Takeaway
A gift is completed by possession because it is a gratuitous contract; ownership does not transfer until the donee takes possession.

Possession Replaces Verbal Acceptance in Gift
Illustrating that possession can replace verbal acceptance in gift

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that possession can substitute for verbal acceptance in a gift and apply it to scenarios where the gifted item is present or absent.

Core Idea
In a gift, taking possession can replace saying 'I accept'.

In most contracts, acceptance must be expressed verbally in the same session. However, the gift contract (hiba) has a unique feature: the act of taking possession can serve as acceptance. This lesson examines the rule from al-Yanabee' that possession stands in place of acceptance, with two clear examples involving a slave—presence and absence of the gift item.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
Read the first sentence: possession takes the place of acceptance. This is the legal principle.
This is specific to gift; sale requires verbal acceptance.
2
Apply to the first example
The donor says 'I give you this slave' while the slave is present. The donee takes possession without saying 'I accept'. Why is it valid? Because the donor's ijab (offer) authorizes possession, and qabd (possession) acts as qabul (acceptance).
Presence means the item is physically available for immediate possession.
3
Apply to the second example
The slave is absent. The donor says 'I have given him to you, go and take possession'. The donee goes and takes possession without verbal acceptance. Again, it is valid because the donor's instruction serves as authorization.
The donor must clearly instruct the donee to take possession; mere offer without instruction may not suffice.
4
Distinguish from sale contract
In gift, possession can replace acceptance; in sale, both offer and acceptance must be verbal in the same session (majlis al-aqd). This is because gift is a gratuitous contract where intent is demonstrated by action.
This rule is a concession to facilitate gifts, especially when the donee is present.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال في «الينابيع»: القبضُ يقوم مقام القبول، حتى إنه إذا قال له: وهبت لك عبدي هذا ، والعبدُ حاضر ، فقَبَضَه : جاز وإن لم يقل : قَبِلتُ. وكذا لو كان العبد غائباً، فقال: وهبته منك، فاذهب فاقبضه، ولم يقل : قَبِلتُ، فذهب وقبضه : جاز.
He said in al-Yanabee': Possession takes the place of acceptance, such that if he said to him: I have given you this slave of mine, and the slave is present, and he took possession of it, it is permissible even if he did not say: I accept. Likewise, if the slave was absent, and he said: I have given him to you, so go and take possession of him, and he did not say: I accept, and he went and took possession, it is permissible.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the sharh. It cites al-Yanabee' to explain that possession (qabd) in a gift can serve as acceptance (qabul). The rule is illustrated with two examples. In the first, the donor says 'I have given you this slave' while the slave is present, and the donee takes possession without verbal acceptance—this is valid. In the second, the slave is absent; the donor tells the donee to go and take possession, and the donee does so without saying 'I accept'—this is also valid. The principle is that in a gift, the donor's offer (ijab) is an authorization for the donee to take possession, and the act of possession itself constitutes acceptance. This differs from sale, where verbal acceptance is generally required in the session.

Key Terms
القبض (al-qabd): Physical possession of the gifted item.
القبول (al-qubul): Verbal acceptance of an offer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if the gift item is absent and the donor says 'I have given him to you, so go and take possession,' and the donee takes possession without saying 'I accept', the gift is:
A) Invalid because acceptance was not verbal
B) Valid only if the donee later says 'I accept'
C) Valid because possession takes the place of acceptance
D) Valid only if the donor is present at the time of possession

Key Takeaway
In a gift contract, the donee's possession (qabd) with the donor's authorization serves as valid acceptance (qabul), eliminating the need for verbal acceptance.

Gift of Debt: Acceptance and Rejection
Intra-Hanafi dispute on gift of debt acceptance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on whether acceptance is required for a gift of debt and explain the consequence of rejection.

Core Idea
A gift of debt from the debtor is a unilateral discharge; acceptance is not required, but rejection annuls it.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the gift of debt. According to Abu Hanifa, such a gift is effective upon offer and does not require acceptance, while Zufar requires acceptance. Understanding this distinction is crucial for properly executing charitable debt forgiveness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
What is being ruled upon? It is the gift of a debt from the debtor (i.e., forgiving a debt). The dispute is about whether acceptance is required for this gift to be effective.
The act is a gift of debt (hibat al-dayn) which functions as a discharge (ibra').
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa holds that acceptance is not required. The gift is effective immediately upon offer. However, if the debtor rejects it, the gift is annulled and the debt returns.
The text says: 'لم يفتقر إلى القبول عند أبي حنيفة، ويرتد بالرد'.
3
State Zufar's position
Zufar holds that the gift is suspended pending acceptance. If the debtor does not accept, the gift is void from the start.
Zufar's view: 'يقف على القبول'.
4
Determine the thamarah (practical consequence)
Under Abu Hanifa, if the debtor rejects, the debt is reinstated. Under Zufar, the gift never took effect, so the debt remains as if nothing happened.
The difference is in the timing of the gift's validity and the effect of rejection.
5
Note the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position for fatwa is Abu Hanifa's view, as the commentary presents it first and does not contradict it.
Zufar's view is a minority within the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو وهب الدين من الغريم أو أبرأه منه : لم يفتقر إلى القبول عند أبي حنيفة، ويرتد بالرد.
وقال زفر : يقف على القبول
فإن وهب لرجل ديناً على آخر ، وأذن له في قبضه منه، فقبضه : جاز؛
استحساناً.
فإن قبض الموهوب له في المجلس بغير أمر الواهب : جاز . وإن قبض بعد الافتراق : لم تصح إلا أن يأذن له الواهب في القبض .
وفي «شرحه»: إذا كان له على رجل دين، فوهبه له: لم يكن له أن يرجع فيه؛ لأن هبة الدين ممن هو عليه : إسقاط له، وبراءة منه، فلم يبق هناك عين يُمكن الرجوع فيها.
للهبة.
وإن قال الموهوب له مُجيباً له لا :أقبلُها : فالدين بحاله؛ لأنه رد
وإن كان الموهوب له غائباً، فلم يعلم بالهبة حتى مات: جازت الهبة، وبرى عما كان عليه؛ لأن الهبة تنفرد بالواحد، فتتم بالإيجاب، وإنما تبطل بالرد، وقد فات الرد، فبقيت.
قوله: (فإن قَبَضَ الموهوب له في المجلس بغير أمر الواهب: جاز)، وهذا استحسان؛ لأن تمام الهبة بالقبض، كما أن تمام البيع بالقبول، والقبولُ لا يحتاج إلى إذن الموجب بعد الإيجاب، فكذا الهبة. قوله: (وإن قَبَضَ بعد الافتراق لم يصح، إلا أن يأذن له الواهب في القبض).
أما إذا لم يأذن له فلأنَّ القبض في الهبة كالقبول، وذلك يختص بالمجلس، لا بعده، فإذا قبض بعد ذلك لم يجز، كما لو قبل بعد المجلس.
And if he gifted the debt from the debtor or absolved him of it, it does not require acceptance according to Abu Hanifa, and it is annulled by rejection. And Zufar said: It depends on acceptance. If he gifted to a man a debt owed by another, and he permitted him to collect it from him, and he collected it, it is valid, by istihsan. If the donee takes possession in the assembly without the donor's command, it is valid. And if he takes possession after separation, it is not valid unless the donor permits him to take possession. And in its commentary: If he has a debt owed by a man, and he gifted it to him, he cannot retract it, because the gift of a debt from the debtor is a discharge and absolution, so there remains no specific thing to retract. For the gift. And if the donee responds to him saying 'No, I accept it', then the debt remains as it was, because it is a rejection. And if the donee is absent and did not know of the gift until he died, the gift is valid and the debtor is released from what he owed, because the gift is completed by the offer alone, and it is only invalidated by rejection, and rejection was not possible, so it stands. His statement: (If the donee takes possession in the assembly without the donor's command, it is valid), and this is istihsan, because the completion of the gift is by possession, just as the completion of sale is by acceptance, and acceptance does not require permission from the offeror after the offer, so similarly the gift. His statement: (And if he takes possession after separation, it is not valid, unless the donor permits him to take possession). As for when he does not permit him, because possession in a gift is like acceptance, and that is specific to the assembly, not after it, so if he takes possession after that, it is not valid, just as if he accepted after the assembly.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage contains several interrelated rulings on the gift of debt (hibat al-dayn) and the role of acceptance and possession. It begins with an intra-Hanafi dispute: According to Abu Hanifa, if someone gifts a debt from the debtor (i.e., forgives it) or absolves him (ibra'), acceptance is not required; the gift is effective immediately upon offer, but if the debtor rejects it, the gift is annulled. Zufar holds that the gift is suspended pending acceptance. The relied-upon position is Abu Hanifa's. The text then addresses the case where someone gifts a debt owed by a third party to the donee; if the donee collects the debt with the donor's permission, it is valid by istihsan. Next, it covers possession by the donee: if the donee takes possession of the gifted item in the same session (majlis) without the donor's explicit permission, it is valid, by istihsan, because possession in a gift functions like acceptance in a sale, and acceptance does not require the offeror's permission after the offer. However, if possession is taken after the session, it is invalid unless the donor permits it, because possession is analogous to acceptance, which must occur in the session. The commentary also discusses the impossibility of retraction in a gift of debt from the debtor, since it is a discharge. If the donee rejects the gift, the debt remains. If the donee dies without knowledge of the gift, the gift is valid because it is perfected by the offer alone, and rejection was not possible.

Key Terms
الدين (al-dayn): A debt owed, a liability.
الإبراء (al-ibra'): Absolution or release from a debt.
الرد (al-radd): Rejection; the act of refusing a gift.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if someone gifts a debt from the debtor (i.e., forgives it), what is the effect of the debtor's rejection?
A) The gift is void from the start
B) The gift is annulled (the debt returns)
C) The gift remains valid despite rejection
D) The donor may retract the gift

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a gift of debt from the debtor (ibra') is a unilateral discharge that does not require acceptance, but is annulled by rejection.
