Possession of a Gift: Session vs Post-Session
Rulings on possession of a gift: session vs post-session

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that possession in a gift (qabd) is analogous to acceptance in a sale and thus must occur within the assembly or with the donor's permission.

Core Idea
Possession in a gift substitutes for acceptance and is only valid within the session unless the donor permits later possession.

This lesson covers the rules for taking possession of a gifted item. The Hanafi school treats possession (qabd) in a gift like acceptance (qabul) in a sale, so it must occur in the same assembly (majlis) or with the donor's permission. The text provides rulings based on istihsan.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios of possession
The text discusses: (1) possession with the donor's permission, (2) possession in the majlis without permission, (3) possession after separation without permission.
Each scenario has a different ruling.
2
Understand the analogy between gift possession and sale acceptance
In completion of a gift, possession (qabd) serves the same function as acceptance (qabul) in a sale. Therefore, the rules of acceptance in a sale are applied to possession in a gift.
This analogy is used to derive the ruling by istihsan.
3
Apply the rule: possession without permission only valid in the session
If the donee takes possession within the same gathering (majlis) without the donor's explicit permission, the gift is valid. This is because possession functions like acceptance, which does not require separate permission after the offer.
The text says: 'فإن قبض الموهوب له في المجلس بغير أمر الواهب: جاز'.
4
Recognize the exception: donor's permission makes post-session possession valid
If the donee takes possession after the session, it is invalid unless the donor explicitly permits it. Permission can be given before or after the session? The text implies permission given after the session is effective? Actually it says 'إلا أن يأذن له الواهب في القبض' which could be before or after. Typically, permission after the session is required.
Without permission, possession after separation is like acceptance after the session, which is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو وهب الدين من الغريم أو أبرأه منه : لم يفتقر إلى القبول عند أبي حنيفة، ويرتد بالرد.
وقال زفر : يقف على القبول
فإن وهب لرجل ديناً على آخر ، وأذن له في قبضه منه، فقبضه : جاز؛
استحساناً.
فإن قبض الموهوب له في المجلس بغير أمر الواهب : جاز . وإن قبض بعد الافتراق : لم تصح إلا أن يأذن له الواهب في القبض .
وفي «شرحه»: إذا كان له على رجل دين، فوهبه له: لم يكن له أن يرجع فيه؛ لأن هبة الدين ممن هو عليه : إسقاط له، وبراءة منه، فلم يبق هناك عين يُمكن الرجوع فيها.
للهبة.
وإن قال الموهوب له مُجيباً له لا :أقبلُها : فالدين بحاله؛ لأنه رد
وإن كان الموهوب له غائباً، فلم يعلم بالهبة حتى مات: جازت الهبة، وبرى عما كان عليه؛ لأن الهبة تنفرد بالواحد، فتتم بالإيجاب، وإنما تبطل بالرد، وقد فات الرد، فبقيت.
قوله: (فإن قَبَضَ الموهوب له في المجلس بغير أمر الواهب: جاز)، وهذا استحسان؛ لأن تمام الهبة بالقبض، كما أن تمام البيع بالقبول، والقبولُ لا يحتاج إلى إذن الموجب بعد الإيجاب، فكذا الهبة. قوله: (وإن قَبَضَ بعد الافتراق لم يصح، إلا أن يأذن له الواهب في القبض).
أما إذا لم يأذن له فلأنَّ القبض في الهبة كالقبول، وذلك يختص بالمجلس، لا بعده، فإذا قبض بعد ذلك لم يجز، كما لو قبل بعد المجلس.
And if he gifted the debt from the debtor or absolved him of it, it does not require acceptance according to Abu Hanifa, and it is annulled by rejection. And Zufar said: It depends on acceptance. If he gifted to a man a debt owed by another, and he permitted him to collect it from him, and he collected it, it is valid, by istihsan. If the donee takes possession in the assembly without the donor's command, it is valid. And if he takes possession after separation, it is not valid unless the donor permits him to take possession. And in its commentary: If he has a debt owed by a man, and he gifted it to him, he cannot retract it, because the gift of a debt from the debtor is a discharge and absolution, so there remains no specific thing to retract. For the gift. And if the donee responds to him saying 'No, I accept it', then the debt remains as it was, because it is a rejection. And if the donee is absent and did not know of the gift until he died, the gift is valid and the debtor is released from what he owed, because the gift is completed by the offer alone, and it is only invalidated by rejection, and rejection was not possible, so it stands. His statement: (If the donee takes possession in the assembly without the donor's command, it is valid), and this is istihsan, because the completion of the gift is by possession, just as the completion of sale is by acceptance, and acceptance does not require permission from the offeror after the offer, so similarly the gift. His statement: (And if he takes possession after separation, it is not valid, unless the donor permits him to take possession). As for when he does not permit him, because possession in a gift is like acceptance, and that is specific to the assembly, not after it, so if he takes possession after that, it is not valid, just as if he accepted after the assembly.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage contains several interrelated rulings on the gift of debt (hibat al-dayn) and the role of acceptance and possession. It begins with an intra-Hanafi dispute: According to Abu Hanifa, if someone gifts a debt from the debtor (i.e., forgives it) or absolves him (ibra'), acceptance is not required; the gift is effective immediately upon offer, but if the debtor rejects it, the gift is annulled. Zufar holds that the gift is suspended pending acceptance. The relied-upon position is Abu Hanifa's. The text then addresses the case where someone gifts a debt owed by a third party to the donee; if the donee collects the debt with the donor's permission, it is valid by istihsan. Next, it covers possession by the donee: if the donee takes possession of the gifted item in the same session (majlis) without the donor's explicit permission, it is valid, by istihsan, because possession in a gift functions like acceptance in a sale, and acceptance does not require the offeror's permission after the offer. However, if possession is taken after the session, it is invalid unless the donor permits it, because possession is analogous to acceptance, which must occur in the session. The commentary also discusses the impossibility of retraction in a gift of debt from the debtor, since it is a discharge. If the donee rejects the gift, the debt remains. If the donee dies without knowledge of the gift, the gift is valid because it is perfected by the offer alone, and rejection was not possible.

Key Terms
المجلس (al-majlis): The session in which the contract of gift is made.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference; a method of reasoning that allows deviation from an analogy for a stronger reason.
القبض (al-qabd): Possession; taking physical control of the gifted item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a donee takes possession of a gift after the session without the donor's permission, what is the ruling?
A) The gift is valid because possession is sufficient.
B) The gift is invalid unless the donor permits it.
C) The gift is valid only if the donee tells the donor later.
D) The gift remains valid if the donor does not object.

Key Takeaway
Possession of a gift is like acceptance in a sale; it must occur in the session unless the donor grants permission for later possession.

Rules of Permission for Possession After Council and Effect of Death
Explains rulings on permission for qabd after council and effect of death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the effect of donor's permission for possession after the gift council and apply it to scenarios involving revocation and death of parties.

Core Idea
Permission for possession is a delegation that outlasts the council; death of either party before possession voids the gift.

In this passage, the author explains that a donor's permission to take possession of a gifted item is considered a delegation (taslit) that remains effective even after the gift council ends, analogous to agency (wakala). However, if the donor revokes permission before possession, or if either the donor or donee dies before possession, the gift becomes invalid. Understanding these rulings is crucial because the gift transfer of ownership is not complete without actual possession (qabd).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the donor's permission (idhn) for the donee to take possession after the gift council. The ruling is that this permission is valid and effective as a delegation (taslit) that continues beyond the council.
Permission is not a condition for validity of the gift itself but a delegation to enable possession.
2
Locate the rational proof (dalil aqli)
The author compares taslit to wakala (agency), which remains effective after the session ends. This rational analogy (qiyas) justifies the continuity of permission.
No explicit textual dalil; the proof is by analogy to a known ruling.
3
Analyze the specific scenarios
a) If donor leaves and donee takes possession later, it's valid because delegation persists. b) If donor revokes permission before possession, possession is invalid. c) If donor dies before possession, gift void because ownership ends. d) If donee dies before possession, gift void because he never became owner.
Each scenario tests the limits of the delegation principle.
4
Derive the underlying principle
Possession (qabd) is a condition for complete transfer of ownership in gifts. Without it, the gift remains revocable and does not pass to heirs.
This principle distinguishes Hanafi law from schools that allow gift completion by offer and acceptance alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إذا أذن له : فالإذن تسليط منه على القبض، والتسليط يبقى بعد المجلس، كالتوكيل. فإن كان الموهوب موجوداً في المجلس، فقال له: قد خلّيتُ بينك وبينه، فاقبض، وانصرف الواهِبُ وقَبَضَه بعده: جاز؛ لأن التسليط لا يبطل بعد الافتراق. وإن أذن له في قبضه بعد الافتراق، فلم يقبضه حتى عزله: لم يصح قبضه بعد ذلك. فإن مات الواهب قبل القبض : بطلت الهبة. وكذا إذا مات الموهوب له. أما إذا مات الواهب : فلان بموته زال ملكه وفات تسليطه كالموكل. وأما إذا مات الموهوب له فلأنه لما مات قبل قبضه : لم يكن مالكاً له، فلم يكن موروثاً عنه.
As for when he gives him permission: then permission is a delegation from him over possession, and the delegation remains after the council, like agency. If the gifted item is present in the council, and he says to him: 'I have vacated it between you and it, so take possession,' and the donor leaves and he takes possession after him: it is valid; because the delegation is not invalidated after separation. And if he gave him permission to take possession after separation, but he did not take possession until he revoked him: his possession after that is not valid. If the donor dies before possession: the gift is void. Similarly if the donee dies. As for when the donor dies: because with his death his ownership ceases and his delegation fails, like the principal. As for when the donee dies: because when he dies before possession, he was not an owner of it, so it is not inherited from him.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayra explains the effect of permission (idhn) given by the donor to the donee for possession (qabd) after the gift council (majlis al-hibah). The Hanafi position is that permission is a delegation (taslit) that survives the council, analogous to wakala (agency). If the item is present and the donor says 'I have vacated it between you and it, take possession,' then the donor leaves and the donee takes possession later, it is valid because the delegation is not nullified by separation. However, if the permission was specifically for after separation and the donor revokes it before possession, the possession becomes invalid. The passage also addresses two cases of death: if the donor dies before possession, the gift is void because his ownership ceases and his delegation cannot continue; if the donee dies before possession, the gift is also void because he never became owner, so it cannot pass to his heirs. This ruling is based on the principle that a gift is not complete without possession, and possession is a condition for transfer of ownership.

Key Terms
الإذن (al-idhn): Permission given by donor to donee to take possession of the gifted item after the gift council.
القبض (al-qabd): Physical or constructive taking of possession of the gifted item.
التسليط (al-taslit): Delegation or empowerment given by the donor to the donee to take possession.
المجلس (al-majlis): The gathering or session where the gift offer is made and accepted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if the donor dies before the donee takes possession?
A) The gift becomes the property of the donee's heirs
B) The gift becomes void
C) The gift transfers to the donor's heirs
D) The gift is completed upon intention

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a gift is not complete without possession; permission for possession is a delegation that lasts beyond the council, but death of either party before actual possession voids the gift.

Gift Ownership Before Possession and Gift to a Slave
States that gift remains in donor's ownership until qabd, and rules on gift to a slave.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that a gift remains in the donor's ownership until possession (qabd) and explain the special rules for accepting a gift on behalf of a slave.

Core Idea
A gift is revocable until possession; a slave must accept his own gift.

This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayra establishes two key Hanafi rules on gifts. First, the donor retains ownership of the gifted item until the donee takes physical possession (qabd), making the gift revocable before that point. Second, if a gift is given to a slave, the slave himself must accept it—his master cannot act on his behalf—but the master ultimately owns the property, and the donor can still revoke.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
The passage states two distinct cases: (a) gift before possession, and (b) gift to a slave. Recognize that each has its own rule.
2
Analyze the first ruling: gift remains in donor's ownership until qabd
Note that the donor can revoke the gift at any time before the donee takes possession. This is because ownership hasn't transferred.
3
Analyze the second ruling: gift to a slave
The slave must accept the gift himself; the master cannot accept or take possession on his behalf. The slave accepts, then the master ultimately owns the property.
4
Examine the consequence: donor's right to revoke
Even after the slave accepts, the donor can still revoke because the slave's ownership is not stable—he is like an agent (wakil) for the master.
5
Compare with manumission (khuruj)
The passage distinguishes this from the 'option of manumission' (khiyar al-khuruj). The slave's ownership here is not fixed, unlike manumission which would make ownership stable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولهذا قالوا: إن الهبة ما لم تُقبض فهي على ملك الواهب، حتى إنه لو رجع فيها قبل قبضها: صح رجوعه. ولو وهب للعبد هبة: فالقبول إلى العبد، ولا يجوز قبول المولى له، ولا قبضه له، ثم بعد ذلك يملكه المولى. وللواهب أن يرجع ، ولا يكون هذا كالخروج؛ لأن الملك للعبد لا يستقر، فصار كالوكيل.
And for this reason they said: Indeed, a gift as long as it has not been possessed is in the ownership of the donor, such that if he revokes it before its possession, his revocation is valid. And if one gives a gift to a slave: then acceptance is for the slave, and it is not permissible for the master to accept for him, nor to take possession for him; then after that the master owns it. And the donor has the right to revoke, and this is not like manumission; because ownership for the slave is not stable, so it becomes like an agent.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayra states a foundational Hanafi principle: a gift remains the property of the donor until possession (qabd) occurs. Therefore, the donor can revoke the gift at any time before possession is taken. This is a distinct feature of Hanafi law. The passage then addresses a gift to a slave (mamluk). The slave can accept the gift himself; the master cannot accept or take possession on behalf of the slave. However, after the slave accepts and possesses, the master ultimately owns the property, as the slave is not a full owner. The donor can revoke this gift because the slave's ownership is not stable—the slave is like an agent (wakil) for the master in acceptance. The ruling distinguishes this from the 'option of manumission' (khuruj) where the slave could become free; here, the gift is revocable because the slave's ownership is conditional.

Key Terms
العبد (al-ʿabd): A male slave who can own property but whose ownership is not independent.
القبول (al-qabul): Acceptance of the gift by the donee.
الخروج (al-khuruj): Manumission or liberation of a slave; here used as a comparison for the stability of ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is a gift considered to be in the ownership of the donee?
A) Immediately upon offer
B) Upon acceptance
C) Upon possession (qabd)
D) Upon donor's declaration

Key Takeaway
A gift does not transfer ownership until possession, and a slave must accept a gift himself though the donor can still revoke.

Valid Expressions for a Gift (Hibah) in Hanafi Law
Lists the valid oral formulae for a gift contract and distinguishes from loan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which verbal formulae constitute a valid hibah and correctly classify ambiguous words like at'am and hamala according to intention.

Core Idea
A gift is concluded by specific indicative words; not all giving words transfer ownership—some imply only a loan.

In Hanafi law, a gift (hibah) is a contract transferring ownership without compensation. The verbal formula (sighah) is key. This lesson catalogues the words that create a valid gift, clarifies when intention matters, and distinguishes gift from loan (ʿariyah). Special rules for gifts to slaves are also covered.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core gift words
From the passage, list the explicit expressions that automatically create a gift: wahaba, nahala, aʿta, atʿama (with food), jaʿala, aʿmara, hamala (with intention).
These words, when uttered with intent, transfer ownership.
2
Distinguish ambiguous expressions
Some words like 'aʿmara' (lifelong grant) and 'hamala' (mounting) require intention: if the donor intends gift, it's hibah; if loan, it's ʿariyah.
The word 'manaha' always indicates a loan, not a gift.
3
Apply the food/land distinction
The expression 'atʿamtu' (I fed) when used with an edible item transfers ownership of that item. When used with land, it is a loan because land is not eaten.
This is from al-Hidayah's commentary.
4
Recognize the Quranic basis for 'clothing'
The word 'kasawtuka' (I clothed you) is a gift, supported by Quran 5:89 (أَوْ كِسْوَتُهُمْ).
The verse shows clothing is a transferable property.
5
Apply the rule for gifts to a slave
If a slave accepts a gift, it is valid even without the master's acceptance. But if the slave rejects and the master accepts, it is invalid. The master cannot take possession or accept on behalf of the slave.
The slave's independent acceptance is sufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتنعقد الهبةُ بقوله: وهبتُكَ، و: نَحَلْتُكَ، و: أعطيتُكَ، و: أطعمتك هذا الطعام، و: جعلت هذا الثوب لك، و: أعمرتـك هـذا الشيء، و: حملتك على هذه الدابة، إذا نوى بالحملان الهبة. ولو قبل العبد الهبة، ولم يقبلها المولى: صحت. ولو ردّها العبد، وقبلها المولى: لم تصح. ولا يجوز قبض المولى ، ولا قبوله لما وهب للعبد، سواء كان على العبد دين، أو لم يكن. * قوله: (وتنعقد الهبة بقوله: وهبتك، نحلتك، و: أعطيتك، و: أطعمتك هذا الطعام، و: جعلتُ هذا الشيء لك). قال في الهداية ) : الإطعام إذا أضيف إلى ما يُطعم عينه : فإنه يراد به تمليك العين، بخلاف ما إذا قال : أطعمتك هذه الأرض، حيث تكون عارية؛ لأن عينها لا تطعم. * قوله: (و: أعمرتُك هذا الشيء، و حملتك على هذه الدابة إذا نوى بالحملان الهبة، وإن نوى العارية كانت عارية؛ لأنها تحتملها. وإن قال : كسوتُك هذا الثوب كان هبةً ؛ لأنه يُراد به التمليك، قال الله تعالى: أَوْ كِسْوَتُهُمْ ) . المائدة / ٨٩ ولو قال : منحتك هذه الجارية: كانت عارية.
And the gift is concluded by his saying: 'I give you', and 'I bestow upon you', and 'I give you', and 'I fed you this food', and 'I made this garment for you', and 'I gave you this thing for lifelong use', and 'I mounted you upon this riding animal' if by mounting he intends a gift. And if the slave accepts the gift, and the master does not accept it, it is valid. And if the slave rejects it, and the master accepts it, it is not valid. And it is not permissible for the master to take possession nor acceptance for what is given to the slave, whether the slave has debt or not. * His statement: (And the gift is concluded by his saying: 'I give you', 'I bestow upon you', 'I give you', 'I fed you this food', 'I made this thing for you.') It is said in al-Hidayah: 'The feeding when added to what is eaten in its substance then it is intended to mean ownership of the substance, contrary to when he says: I fed you this land, where it is a loan because its substance is not eaten.' * His statement: (And I gave you this thing for lifelong use, and I mounted you upon this riding animal) if he intends by mounting a gift, and if he intends a loan then it is a loan because it can bear that. And if he says: I clothed you this garment, it is a gift because it is intended to mean ownership. Allah Almighty said: 'or their clothing' (Surah al-Ma'idah: 89). And if he says: I granted you this slave-girl, it is a loan.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayra lists the specific verbal formulae (sighah) that create a valid gift (hibah) contract in Hanafi law. The gift is concluded by words like wahaba (I give), nahala (I bestow), a'ta (I give), at'ama (I feed) when attached to an edible substance, ja'ala (I make), a'mara (I give for lifelong use), hamala (I mount) if intending gift. The commentary from al-Hidayah clarifies that 'at'am' when applied to food means ownership of the substance, but if applied to land it is a loan ('ariyah) because land is not eaten. Similarly, 'kasawa' (clothing) is a gift by Quranic usage (Surah al-Ma'idah: 89). 'Manaha' (gave as a grant) is a loan, not a gift. The passage also reiterates the slave rule: acceptance by the slave is sufficient; master's acceptance is not required. The master cannot take possession or accept on behalf of the slave.

Key Terms
الهبة (al-hibah): A contract transferring ownership of a property without compensation, concluded by specific verbal formulae.
الصيغة (al-sighah): The verbal formula used to conclude a contract; here the specific words that create a gift.
العارية (al-ʿariyah): A loan of an object for use without transfer of ownership; distinguished from hibah.
الإطعام (al-itʿam): The act of feeding; when used with a specific food item, it conveys ownership of that item.
المنحة (al-minhah): Giving a thing for use; considered a loan ('ariyah) not a gift in Hanafi law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A valid gift in Hanafi law requires using one of the recognized verbal formulae; ambiguous words like 'manaha' and 'at'am' with inedibles result in a loan.

Expressions Creating a Valid Gift and the Role of Acceptance
Ruling on verbal formulations creating a valid gift contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which verbal expressions constitute a valid gift contract in the Hanafi school and apply the rules regarding the slave's acceptance.

Core Idea
Not every giving expression creates a gift; intention and the nature of the object determine ownership transfer vs. usufruct.

Gift contracts in Hanafi fiqh depend on precise wording. This lesson examines the seven key expressions that establish a valid gift, how ambiguous phrases like 'feeding' or 'mounting' are interpreted based on context, and the special rule that a slave's own acceptance is necessary—the master cannot substitute his acceptance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the valid gift expressions
List the seven expressions mentioned in the text that constitute a valid offer (ijab) of a gift: wahaba, nahala, a'ta, at'ama, ja'ala, a'mara, hamala.
Note that 'hamala' requires an explicit intention of gift; otherwise it is a loan.
2
Distinguish gift from loan based on object
For 'at'ama' (feeding), if the object is something that can be eaten (e.g., food), it is a gift; if it cannot be eaten (e.g., land), it is a loan (ariyah). Similarly, 'a'mara' and 'hamala' depend on intention.
The text quotes al-Hidayah to clarify the 'at'ama' distinction.
3
Analyze the slave acceptance rule
The slave's own acceptance is required for the gift to be valid; the master cannot accept on his behalf. If the slave rejects, the master's acceptance is void.
This rule applies regardless of the slave's debt status.
4
Recognize the default classification of 'kasa' and 'manaha'
'Kasa' (clothing) is always a gift because it implies ownership transfer, supported by Quran 5:89. 'Manaha' (grant) with a slave-girl is always a loan.
Surah al-Ma'idah verse 89 provides the dalil for 'kasa' as tamlik.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The gift is contracted by his saying: 'I gifted you', and 'I bestowed upon you', and 'I gave you', and 'I fed you this food', and 'I made this garment for you', and 'I granted you lifetime use of this thing', and 'I mounted you on this animal' if he intends by mounting a gift. And if the slave accepts the gift and the master does not accept it, it is valid. And if the slave rejects it and the master accepts it, it is not valid. And it is not permissible for the master to take possession nor to accept what is gifted to the slave, whether the slave has debt or not. He said in al-Hidayah: 'Feeding when it is added to what is actually eaten intends transfer of ownership, unlike if he says: I fed you this land, then it is a loan because its substance is not eaten.' And if he says: 'I granted you lifetime use of this thing' and 'I mounted you on this animal' if he intends by mounting a gift, but if he intends a loan then it is a loan because it can bear that. And if he says: 'I clothed you this garment' it is a gift because it intends transfer of ownership. Allah said: 'or clothe them' (al-Ma'idah 89). And if he says: 'I gave you as a grant this slave-girl' it is a loan.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Al-Quduri's Mukhtasar explains the verbal formulations that constitute a valid gift contract in the Hanafi school. The matn lists seven expressions: 'wahaba' (I gifted), 'nahala' (I bestowed), 'a'ta' (I gave), 'at'ama' (I fed), 'ja'ala' (I made for you), 'a'mara' (I granted lifetime use), and 'hamala' (I mounted) with the condition that if 'hamala' is used, the intention must be gift; otherwise it is a loan. The commentator then quotes al-Hidayah to clarify that 'feeding' (it'am) when applied to food items denotes transfer of ownership, but if applied to land, it is a loan because land is not eaten. The ruling on a slave's acceptance is important: a slave can accept a gift without the master's consent, and the gift is valid. However, if the slave rejects and the master tries to accept, it is invalid. The master has no right to accept or take possession on behalf of the slave, regardless of the slave's debts. The commentary also addresses 'kasa' (clothing), citing the Quranic verse (5:89) to confirm it implies ownership transfer. Finally, the phrase 'manaha' (bestowed) with a slave-girl is explicitly a loan ('ariyah), not a gift, in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
الهبة (al-hiba): A contract that transfers ownership of a specific thing without compensation.
العارية (al-ariyah): A contract that grants usufruct of a thing without compensation, not ownership.
تمليك (tamlik): Transfer of ownership; the intended effect of a gift contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, if a slave rejects a gift but the master accepts it, what is the ruling?
A) The gift is valid because the master accepted.
B) The gift is invalid because the slave rejected.
C) The gift is suspended until the slave changes his mind.
D) The gift becomes a loan.

Key Takeaway
A gift contract's validity depends on precise wording and the recipient's acceptance; ambiguous expressions are interpreted by object type and intention, and a slave's own acceptance is required.

Al-Karkhi's Principle: Distinguishing Loan of Use from Gift or Loan of Consumption
Intra-Hanafi dispute on classification of grants based on object's use

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply al-Karkhi's principle to classify a gratuitous transfer as ariyah, hiba, or qard, and state the two Hanafi transmissions for consumable items.

Core Idea
The nature of the object determines the contract: non-consumable = ariyah; consumable = dispute between hiba and qard.

In Hanafi law, not every gratuitous transfer is a gift. The key is how the object is used. If you benefit from using the object without consuming it (like riding a camel or wearing a garment), it is a loan for usufruct (ariyah). But if you can only benefit by consuming it (like food or milk), the early Hanafi masters had two opinions: one considers it a gift (hiba), the other a loan (qard). This passage from al-Karkhi gives the principle and shows an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the grant and its object
Look at what is given: a camel, sheep, garment, house (non-consumable) or food, milk, dirhams (consumable). Note the term 'manihah' (grant).
The text uses 'manihah' which can mean ariyah or hiba.
2
Classify the benefit
Determine whether the primary benefit requires consumption (eating, spending) or mere use (riding, wearing, dwelling).
This is the central illa (effective cause).
3
Apply the principle
For non-consumable benefits: it is ariyah. For consumable benefits: there are two transmitted opinions (riwayatan) – one that it is hiba, another that it is qard.
No preference given in this source; it is an unresolved ikhtilaf.
4
Consider the legal consequences
If it is ariyah, the recipient has ownership of the usufruct only and must return the same object. If it is hiba, the recipient owns the object itself immediately. If it is qard, the recipient owns the object but must return similar items (like a debt).
This is the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference).
5
Recognize the intra-madhhab nature
Understand that both opinions are within the Hanafi school; no tarjih is offered in this passage. Later scholars may have preferred one.
This shows the flexibility and internal ijtihad within the madhhab.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال في «الكرخي»: إذا منحه بعيراً أو شاةً أو ثوباً أو داراً: فهي عارية. وإن منحه طعاماً أو لبناً أو دراهم ففيه روايتان: إحداهما: هبة، والأخرى: قرض. والأصل فيه: أن كلَّ ما يُنتفع به كالسكنى، أو اللبس، أو الركوب: فهو عارية وكل ما لا يُنتفع به إلا بأكله، أو استهلاكه ففيه روايتان.
He said in al-Karkhi: If he gives him as a grant a camel, sheep, garment, or house, it is a loan. And if he gives him as a grant food, milk, or dirhams, there are two transmissions: one that it is a gift, and the other that it is a loan. The principle in this is: everything that is benefited from like dwelling, wearing, or riding, it is a loan; and everything that cannot be benefited from except by eating or consuming it, there are two transmissions regarding it.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Al-Quduri's Mukhtasar quotes the work known as 'al-Karkhi', likely a reference to Usool al-Karkhi or a similar text. It addresses a key distinction in Hanafi law: when someone gives something to another without compensation, is it a gift (hiba) or a loan (ariyah) or a loan (qard)? The rule is based on how the object is used. If the benefit is derived by using the object without consuming it (like riding an animal, wearing a garment, or living in a house), it is a loan (ariyah). However, if the item can only be benefited from by consuming it (like food or milk), there are two transmitted opinions from the early Hanafi masters: one considers it a gift (hiba), and the other considers it a loan (qard). This ikhtilaf is significant because the legal effects differ: a gift requires immediate transfer of ownership, while a loan (qard) creates a debt. Note that the term 'ariyah' here is used for non-consumable usufruct, while 'qard' is for consumable items. The text does not explicitly resolve which transmission is preferred.

Key Terms
العارية (al-ariyah): A loan of a non-consumable thing for its usufruct without compensation.
القرض (al-qard): A loan of a consumable thing that is to be returned in kind.
المنيحة (al-manihah): A grant of something for temporary benefit; generally treated as ariyah in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Karkhi, if someone grants another a camel, what is the ruling?
A) It is a gift (hiba).
B) It is a loan (ariyah).
C) It is a loan (qard).
D) It is uncertain.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi classification of gratuitous transfers hinges on the object's benefit: non-consumable use yields ariyah; consumable use yields a dispute between hiba and qard.

Gifts of Divisible vs. Indivisible Property: The Condition of Physical Division
Ruling on gift of undivided shares in divisible vs. indivisible property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between gifts of divisible and indivisible property and state the condition of physical division and delivery for ownership transfer.

Core Idea
A gift of divisible property transfers ownership only after division and delivery; a gift of an undivided share in indivisible property is valid immediately.

This lesson from the Hanafi commentary on Al-Quduri's Mukhtasar clarifies a critical distinction in gift law: when you gift a share of a house (divisible), you must physically divide and hand over the specific portion for ownership to transfer. But if you gift a share of something indivisible like a garment, the gift is valid without division. Let's unpack the reasoning and conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the property type
First, determine whether the gifted property is divisible (qabili li al-qisma) or indivisible. Divisible means it can be partitioned without loss of value (like land, house). Indivisible includes slaves, garments, etc.
2
Apply the ruling for divisible property
If divisible, the gift is contractually valid (ja'iz) but ownership (milk) does not transfer until the property is physically divided and the donee takes possession (hawz) of their specific portion. The text says 'لا تجوز إلا محوزةً مقسومة'.
The word 'la tajuz' here means ownership is not established, not that the contract is invalid.
3
Apply the ruling for indivisible property
If indivisible, a gift of an undivided share (musha') is valid and ownership transfers immediately, because division is impossible and does not affect the gift.
Examples: gifting half of a garment or a slave.
4
Consider the effect on partners
The same rule applies even if the donee is a co-owner. A gift of an undivided share in divisible property to a partner still requires division and delivery.
5
Note the same rule for charity
The ruling applies equally to voluntary charity (sadaqah) – it must be divided and delivered if the property is divisible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا تجوز الهبة فيما يُقسَم إلا مَحُورَةً مقسومةً . وهبةُ المُشَاع فيما لا يُقْسَم جائزة. قوله: (ولا تجوز الهبة فيما يُقسم إلا محوزةً مقسومة). - وكذا الصدقة. وتجوز فيما لا يُقسم. ولا فرق في ذلك بين الشريك وغيره يعني إذا وهب من شريكه : لا ومعنى قوله : لا تجوز أي لا يثبت الملك فيها؛ لأنها في نفسها وقعت جائزة، لكن غير مثبتة للملك قبل تسليمها محوزةً، فإنه لو قَسَمَها، وسلّمها مقسومة: صحت. قوله: (وهبةُ المُشَاع فيما لا يُقسَم جائزة)، كالعبد والثوب، وأشباه ذلك؛ لأن الإشاعة فيما لا يحتمل القسمة غير مؤثرة في الهبة.
And the gift is not valid in what can be divided except by being seized (mahuzatan) divided. And the gift of an undivided share (musha') in what cannot be divided is valid. His statement: (And the gift is not valid in what can be divided except by being seized divided). - And similarly for charity. And it is valid in what cannot be divided. And there is no difference in that between a partner and others, meaning if he gifts to his partner: no. And the meaning of his statement 'it is not valid' is that ownership is not established; because it itself occurred as permissible, but not establishing ownership before delivery as seized. So if he divides it and delivers it divided, it is valid. His statement: (And the gift of an undivided share in what cannot be divided is valid), like a slave and a garment, and the like; because the undivided share in what cannot be divided has no effect on the gift.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Al-Quduri's Mukhtasar addresses a key rule in the Hanafi law of gifts: a gift of property that is divisible (i.e., can be partitioned without loss of value) is not effective to transfer ownership unless the property is physically divided and delivered (seized) in its divided form. This is called 'mahuzatan maqsumatan'. The reason is that a gift of an undivided share in divisible property is not considered a complete gift until the property is divided and each party takes possession of their specific portion. This applies even between co-owners: a partner cannot gift an undivided share in jointly owned divisible property without dividing it first. However, if the property is indivisible (like a slave or a garment), a gift of an undivided share is valid because it cannot be physically divided. The commentary clarifies that the word 'la tajuz' does not mean the gift is invalid contractually; rather, it means ownership is not transferred until the conditions are met. Once the gift is divided and delivered, it becomes valid and ownership transfers. The same rule applies to voluntary charity (sadaqah).

Key Terms
المشاع (al-musha'): An undivided share in joint property.
الحوزة (al-hawzah): The act of taking possession and seizing the gifted property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi law, a gift of an undivided share (musha') in a house (divisible property) is:
A) Valid and ownership transfers immediately.
B) Valid but ownership transfers only after division and delivery.
C) Invalid entirely.
D) Valid only if the donor and donee are partners.

Key Takeaway
A gift of divisible property requires physical division and delivery for ownership transfer; a gift of indivisible property does not.

Gift of Divisible vs Indivisible Property
Ruling on gift conditions for divisible vs indivisible property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition for a valid gift of divisible property and classify property as divisible or indivisible for the purpose of gift transfer.

Core Idea
A gift of divisible property only transfers ownership upon possession and physical division; a gift of an undivided share in indivisible property is immediately valid.

This lesson examines a key Hanafi ruling on gifts: the requirement of possession and division (mahuza maqsūma) for divisible property. Unlike other schools, Hanafis hold that the contract itself is valid (sahih) but ownership is not transferred until the item is physically separated and delivered. For indivisible items like a slave or a garment, an undivided share gift is fully effective.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two property categories
Read the opening lines: 'And the gift is not valid in what is divisible except possessed and divided. And the gift of an undivided share in what is not divisible is permissible.' Note that property is divided into two types: divisible (mā yuqsamu) and indivisible (mā lā yuqsamu).
Divisible means the item can be split without ruining its utility, e.g., land. Indivisible means splitting would destroy its function, e.g., a garment or a slave.
2
Understand the condition 'mahuza maqsūma'
The phrase 'mahūratan maqsūmatan' means 'possessed and divided'. For a gift of divisible property to transfer ownership, two things are required: (a) actual delivery (qabd) and (b) physical separation (qisma) so the gifted portion is distinct from the rest.
The commentary clarifies that 'la tajūz' here means ownership is not established, not that the contract is invalid. The gift is valid (jā'iza) but incomplete.
3
Compare divisible vs indivisible property
Gift of an undivided share (mushā') in divisible property is not valid without division, but in indivisible property it is valid. Reason: In indivisible property, the undivided share does not harm the gift, whereas in divisible property, the shares are not separated.
This applies to both gifts and charity (sadaqa).
4
Apply to examples
Given a scenario: A gifts B a plot of land that can be split. Without dividing and handing over, ownership does not transfer. If A gifts B a house (indivisible), the undivided share gift is valid even if the house is not divided.
The text also mentions that the same rule applies if the donee is a co-owner of the divisible property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الهبة عارية.
ولا تجوز الهبة فيما يُقسَم إلا مَحُورَةً مقسومةً . وهبةُ المُشَاع فيما لا يُقْسَم جائزة.
قال في «الكرخي»: إذا منحه بعيراً أو شاةً أو ثوباً أو داراً: فهي عارية. وإن منحه طعاماً أو لبناً أو دراهم ففيه روايتان: إحداهما: هبة، والأخرى: قرض.
والأصل فيه: أن كلَّ ما يُنتفع به كالسكنى، أو اللبس، أو الركوب: فهو عارية وكل ما لا يُنتفع به إلا بأكله، أو استهلاكه ففيه روايتان.
قوله: (ولا تجوز الهبة فيما يُقسَم إلا محوزةً مقسومة). - وكذا الصدقة. وتجوز فيما لا يُقسم. ولا فرق في ذلك بين الشريك وغيره يعني إذا وهب من شريكه : لا ومعنى قوله : لا تجوز أي لا يثبت الملك فيها؛ لأنها في نفسها وقعت جائزة، لكن غير مثبتة للملك قبل تسليمها محوزةً، فإنه لو قَسَمَها، وسلّمها مقسومة: صحت.

The book of gifts. 'Ariyah (loan). And the gift is not valid in what is divisible except [when] possessed and divided. And the gift of an undivided share in what is not divisible is permissible. He said in al-Karkhi: If he grants him a camel, or a sheep, or a garment, or a house: it is 'ariyah. And if he grants him food, or milk, or dirhams, then there are two narrations: one: it is a gift, and the other: it is a loan. And the principle in this: that everything that is used for benefit like dwelling, or wearing, or riding: it is 'ariyah. And everything that is not benefited from except by eating it or consuming it: there are two narrations. He said: (And the gift is not valid in what is divisible except [when] possessed [and] divided). – And likewise charity. And it is permissible in what is not divisible. And there is no difference in that between the partner and other. Meaning: if he gives a gift to his partner: [it is] not. And the meaning of his saying: it is not valid: i.e., ownership is not established in it; because in itself it occurred validly, but not establishing ownership before its delivery as possessed. For if he divides it and delivers it divided: it is valid. He said: (And the gift of an undivided share in what is not divisible is permissible), like a slave and a garment and similar to that; because the undivided share in what does not bear division has no effect on the gift.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage from Al-Jawhara al-Nayyirah begins the chapter on gifts (hibah). It presents a foundational ruling: a gift of divisible property (like land or a house that can be split) is only valid if the item is actually possessed and physically separated (mahuza maqsūma). If it is not yet divided, the gift does not transfer ownership until possession is taken. The author notes that the same rule applies to charity (sadaqa). Gifts of indivisible property (like a slave or a garment) are valid even without physical division because dividing them would destroy their utility. The text then quotes Imam Al-Karkhi clarifying the distinction between a loan for use ('āriya) and a gift or loan for consumption (qarḍ). If the item is given for non-consumptive use (e.g., a camel to ride or a house to live in), it is 'āriya. If the item is consumed by use (e.g., food eaten or money spent), there are two transmitted opinions: one considers it a gift (hiba), the other a loan (qarḍ). The underlying principle is based on the nature of benefit: non-consumptive use yields 'āriya; consumptive use yields a dispute. The commentary then explains the matn phrases: 'la tajūz' here means that ownership is not established in the gift before delivery and division, not that the contract itself is invalid. The gift is valid (jā'iza) but incomplete until possession. For undivided shares (mushā') in indivisible property, the gift is fully valid because co-ownership does not harm the gift's effect.

Key Terms
هبة (hiba): A gift that transfers ownership without compensation, but for divisible property ownership is contingent on possession and division.
مشاع (mushā'): An undivided share in common property (co-ownership).
محوزة (mahuza): The condition of being possessed/taken delivery of (qabd) in a way that separates the gifted item from the giver's control.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) It is valid because the house is not divisible without damage.
C) It is valid only if the house is divided.
D) It is invalid because a gift of an undivided share is never valid.

Key Takeaway
A gift of divisible property requires possession and physical division to transfer ownership; a gift of an undivided share in indivisible property is immediately valid.

Distinguishing 'Ariyah from Hibah and Qard
Distinguishing 'ariyah from hibah and qard based on consumptive vs non-consumptive use

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply Al-Karkhi's principle to classify a transaction as 'ariyah, hibah, or qard based on whether the item is consumed by use.

Core Idea
Non-consumptive use yields 'ariyah; consumptive use is disputed between hibah and qard.

This lesson unpacks Al-Karkhi's explanation distinguishing a loan for use ('ariyah) from a gift (hibah) and a loan for consumption (qard). The underlying principle is the nature of benefit: if the item is used without being consumed (e.g., wearing a garment, riding an animal), it is 'ariyah. If the item is consumed (e.g., food, money), there are two narrations: one considers it a gift, the other a loan. This distinction is critical for determining ownership and liability.

Guided Path
1
Recall the definitions of 'ariyah, hibah, and qard
'Ariyah: loan for use, ownership not transferred, item must be returned. Hibah: gift, ownership transferred without compensation. Qard: loan for consumption, ownership of the specific item is transferred, but the borrower must return an equivalent.
These are distinct contracts with different legal effects.
2
Understand Al-Karkhi's principle
Al-Karkhi states: 'Everything that is used for benefit like dwelling, wearing, or riding: it is 'ariyah. And everything that is not benefited from except by eating it or consuming it: there are two narrations.' The key factor is whether the item is durable (non-consumptive) or perishable (consumptive).
The principle is based on 'urf (custom) and the nature of the item.
3
Apply to examples from the text
Giving a camel, sheep, garment, or house for use is 'ariyah (non-consumptive). Giving food, milk, or dirhams is ambiguous: one narration says gift, the other says loan (qard).
For consumables, the second narration (qard) is more cautious because it requires repayment of equivalent.
4
Note the two riwayat and their implications
The two riwayat reflect a difference of opinion among Hanafi scholars. If classified as hibah, ownership transfers without obligation to return; if as qard, the recipient must return an equivalent (like lending money). The text does not resolve which is preferred.
This ambiguity affects liability: if the item is destroyed after consumption, under hibah the donee is not liable; under qard, the borrower is liable for the equivalent.

Source Text

If it is not yet divided, the gift does not transfer ownership until possession is taken. The author notes that the same rule applies to charity (sadaqa). Gifts of indivisible property (like a slave or a garment) are valid even without physical division because dividing them would destroy their utility. The text then quotes Imam Al-Karkhi clarifying the distinction between a loan for use ('āriya) and a gift or loan for consumption (qarḍ). If the item is given for non-consumptive use (e.g., a camel to ride or a house to live in), it is 'āriya. If the item is consumed by use (e.g., food eaten or money spent), there are two transmitted opinions: one considers it a gift (hiba), the other a loan (qarḍ). The underlying principle is based on the nature of benefit: non-consumptive use yields 'āriya; consumptive use yields a dispute. The commentary then explains the matn phrases: 'la tajūz' here means that ownership is not established in the gift before delivery and division, not that the contract itself is invalid. The gift is valid (jā'iza) but incomplete until possession. For undivided shares (mushā') in indivisible property, the gift is fully valid because co-ownership does not harm the gift's effect.

Key Terms
عارية ('āriya): A loan for use; the item is given for benefit without consumption; ownership is not transferred.
قرض (qard): A loan for consumption; the borrower owns the item but must return an equivalent in value or kind.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Al-Karkhi, if someone gives another a garment to wear, what is the classification?
A) Gift (hibah)
B) Loan for consumption (qard)
C) Loan for use ('āriya)
D) It depends on whether the garment is returned.

Key Takeaway
Al-Karkhi's principle classifies gratuitous transfers by consumptive vs non-consumptive use: non-consumptive yields 'ariyah; consumptive yields a dispute between hibah and qard.

Gift of an Undivided Share and the Three Principles
Ruling statement with three clarifying principles

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on gifting an undivided share in divisible property and explain the three principles that determine its validity.

Core Idea
Gift of an undivided share is invalid in divisible property but valid in indivisible; the decisive moment is possession.

The matn states a clear ruling: a gift of an undivided share (shiqs musha') is invalid (fasid). However, this ruling is not absolute. The sharh unpacks three subtle principles that qualify when the gift is truly invalid, when it is valid, and how to evaluate co-ownership (shuyū') and its timing. Understanding these will prevent misapplication.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary ruling and its exception.
Read the matn: 'Whoever gives a shiqs musha', the gift is invalid.' Then note the exception: in indivisible property (e.g., half a coin), the gift is valid. This is the first principle.
The pledge (rahn) of a shiqs musha' is invalid in both divisible and indivisible – a different rule.
2
Distinguish divisible from indivisible property.
Divisible property can be divided without significant loss of value. Indivisible (e.g., a single dirham) loses value if split. The gift of a share in indivisible property is valid, treated like a share in indivisible mushā'.
3
Identify which type of co-ownership invalidates the gift.
The second principle: only co-ownership that exists at the time of the contract (shuyū' muqārin) invalidates. Later arising co-ownership (tāri') does not, e.g., if the donor takes back part after delivery.
But if a third party successfully claims part after possession, the gift becomes invalid (per al-Yanābi').
4
Determine the decisive time for considering co-ownership.
The third principle: the 'ibra (consideration) of shuyū' is at the time of possession (qabd), not the contract. If the donor gives half a house and later adds the other half before delivery, and then delivers both together, the gift is valid because at possession there is no shuyū'.

Source Text

And whoever gives as a gift an undivided share: the gift is invalid. Unlike the pledge, for if he pledges an undivided share: it is not valid in what can be divided, nor in what cannot be divided. * He said: (And whoever gives as a gift an undivided share: the gift is invalid). Know that in this matter three principles are needed: First: the distinction between what can be divided and what cannot be divided. Second: the corrupting undivided share: is it the concurrent one or the subsequent one? Third: the statement of the consideration in the undivided share: is it at the time of possession or at the time of contract? As for the first: if he gives him half a valid dirham or half a valid mithqal: it is valid, and this is the correct [view], and this is considered like an undivided share that does not bear division because dividing it causes a loss in its value. And as for the second: the corrupting [factor] is the concurrent undivided share [at the time of contract] not the subsequent one. So if a person gives a gift, then takes back part of it: it does not prevent its validity. Thus in 'Shahan'. And in 'al-Yanabi': if he gives him a house, and he takes possession of it, then some of it is claimed: the gift becomes invalid. And the third: the consideration in the undivided share is at the time of possession, such that if he gives him half a house and does not deliver until he gives him the other half and delivers: it is valid.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on gifts of undivided shares. The matn states: 'Whoever gives an undivided share (shiqs musha') as a gift, the gift is invalid (fasida).' This applies specifically to divisible property (e.g., half a plot of land). The commentary clarifies that the ruling is different for a pledge (rahn): a pledge of an undivided share is invalid whether the property is divisible or not. The sharh then introduces three fundamental principles (usul thalatha) needed to fully understand this issue. First: distinguish between divisible and indivisible property. An indivisible item (like a single dirham or mithqal) cannot be halved without destroying its value; gifting half is valid and treated like a mushā' in indivisible property. Second: the invalidating co-ownership (shuyū') is the one existing at the time of the contract (muqārin), not one that arises later (tāri'). If someone gifts a whole property, then takes back part, the original gift remains valid. But if after possession, a third party claims part of the property, the gift becomes invalid (as per 'al-Yanābi'). Third: the decisive time for considering co-ownership is at possession (qabd), not the contract. So if someone gives half a house but does not deliver until he gives the other half as well and delivers both together, the gift is valid because at time of possession there is no shuyū'.

Key Terms
شقص مشاع (shiqs musha'): An undivided share or portion of a property that is owned in common with others.
شيوع مقارن (shuyū' muqārin): Co-ownership that exists at the time of the contract (concurrent).
شيوع طارئ (shuyū' tāri'): Co-ownership that arises after the contract, e.g., by partial revocation or legal claim.
عبرة في الشيوع ('ibra fī al-shuyū'): The time at which co-ownership is considered for validity: at possession (qabd) not at contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Co-ownership that arises after possession (tāri').
B) Co-ownership present at the time of contract (muqārin).
C) Co-ownership present at the time of division.
D) All co-ownership regardless of timing.

Key Takeaway
The validity of a gift of an undivided share in Hanafi fiqh is determined by three principles: divisibility of property, timing of co-ownership (concurrent vs subsequent), and the decisive moment of possession.

Gift of an Undivided Share (Shiqs Mashā')
Commentary on the invalidity of gifting an undivided share

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on gifting an undivided share, distinguish between divisible and indivisible property, and evaluate the condition of possession (qabd) as the basis for invalidity.

Core Idea
A gift of an undivided share in divisible property is invalid because complete possession is impossible without joining something else.

In this lesson, we examine a classic Hanafi ruling from the Quduri matn: a gift of an undivided share (shiqs mashā') is invalid (fasid). The commentary unpacks the reasoning, focusing on the requirement of complete possession (qabd) and the distinction between divisible and indivisible property. We will trace the proof from the hadith, explore an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether ownership is established, and understand the practical application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is gifting an undivided share (shiqs mashā') of property. The ruling according to the matn is that the gift is fasid (invalid). Note that this applies only to property that can be divided.
The commentator clarifies that the ruling is specific to divisible property; indivisible property (like a half dirham) is an exception.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary proof is the hadith: 'A gift is not valid unless it is taken into possession' (لا تجوز الهبة إلا مقبوضة). The commentator notes its chain is weak as a marfoo' hadith but supported by athar from Ibn Abbas and Ibrahim al-Nakha'i.
The hadith establishes possession (qabd) as a condition for validity.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase 'لا تجوز' indicates prohibition of validity without possession, and 'إلا' restricts validity to possessed gifts. Thus, complete possession is required. An undivided share cannot be fully possessed without adding something else (which is not part of the gift), so the condition is not met.
This reasoning also prevents forcing the donor into an unintended division.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
If the donor delivers the undivided share in a fasid gift, there is disagreement among mashayikh on whether ownership transfers and whether the recipient must guarantee it (like in a fasid sale). The chosen view (al-mukhtar) is that ownership is not established.
This differs from the rule in fasid sales where ownership does transfer.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The commentator declares the chosen view as 'al-mukhtar', indicating the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school. The gift is invalid and ownership does not pass.
The tarjih is based on the principle that possession is incomplete and the contract is defective.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the Quduri matn regarding the gift of an undivided share (shiqs mashā'). The matn states that such a gift is invalid (fasid). The commentator clarifies that this ruling is specific to property that can be divided (like land or houses) as opposed to property that cannot be divided without loss of value (like a single dirham or mithqal). The commentary then establishes three principles: (1) the distinction between divisible and indivisible property; (2) that the invalidating co-ownership is the one existing at the time of the contract (contemporaneous), not subsequent co-ownership; (3) that the relevant time for assessing co-ownership is at the time of possession (qabd), not the time of the contract. The proof for the ruling is the hadith: 'A gift is not valid unless it is taken into possession' (لا تجوز الهبة إلا مقبوضة), although the commentator notes its chain is weak. The reasoning is that possession of an undivided share is incomplete because it requires joining something else (which is not gifted) to take full possession. Furthermore, validating it would force the donor into an unintended division. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: if the donor delivers the share in a state of co-ownership (invalidly), some mashayikh hold that ownership is established and the recipient must guarantee it (like in a fasid sale), but the chosen view (al-mukhtar) is that ownership is not established. However, if the donor later divides the property and then delivers the specific portion, the gift becomes valid because possession is then complete. The commentary also discusses a related issue: a gift of something attached (like fruit on trees) is valid by istihsan if the donor allows the recipient to take possession after separation.

Key Terms
شقص مشاع (shiqs mashā'): An undivided share or fractional portion of a jointly owned property that has not been physically partitioned.
هبة فاسدة (hibah fāsidah): An invalid gift that does not transfer ownership due to a defect in the contract (here, incomplete possession) but may carry liability if possession occurred.
قبض (qabd): Possession or physical taking of the gifted item; a necessary condition for the validity of a gift in Hanafi law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because the recipient does not know the exact amount.

Key Takeaway
A gift of an undivided share in divisible property is invalid (fasid) because the condition of complete possession cannot be fulfilled, and the muftā bihi position is that ownership does not transfer.

Gift of Undivided Share (Musha') in Divisible Property
Ruling on gift of undivided share in divisible property with proof and intra-Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on gifting an undivided share in divisible property and explain why possession is a condition based on the hadith.

Core Idea
A gift of an undivided share in divisible property is invalid without prior division, even if delivered.

This passage from Kitab al-Hibah addresses a key issue: can you gift a share of something that hasn't been separated? The Hanafi school says no, because possession (qabd) is a condition for a valid gift, and you cannot fully possess an undivided share without including what isn't gifted. The Prophet's hadith establishes this requirement, and later scholars debated whether delivery without division can ever confer ownership.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is gifting an undivided share (musha') in divisible property. The ruling is that it is invalid (fasid) unless the donor first divides the share and delivers it.
Divisible property means it can be partitioned without loss of value, e.g., land, a house.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites the hadith: 'لا تجوز الهبة إلا مقبوضة' (A gift is not valid unless taken possession of). This is the primary evidence.
The hadith is classified as gharib (weak) according to the footnote, but it is accepted by Hanafis for its meaning.
3
Analyse the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase 'مقبوضة' requires complete possession. An undivided share cannot be fully possessed without including the rest of the property, which is not gifted. Thus, the condition of complete possession is not met.
Compare with a sale of musha', which is valid because sale does not require possession as a condition for validity.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Later Hanafi scholars differed: if the donor delivers the musha' knowing it is invalid, does ownership arise (like in a fasid sale)? The chosen view (mukhtar) is that ownership is not established.
This distinguishes a fasid gift from a fasid sale where ownership and guarantee (daman) are established.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قَسَمه وسلَّمه : جاز.
وإنما لم تجز هبةُ المشاع فيما يُقسَم؛ لأن القبض منصوص عليه في الهبة، قال عليه الصلاة والسلام : «لا تجوز الهبة إلا مقبوضة». فيشترط كمال القبض، والمشاعُ لا يقبله إلا بضم غيره إليه، وذلك غير موهوب.
ولأن في تجويزه: إلزامه شيئاً لم يلتزمه، وهو القسمة.
وقوله : فالهبة فاسدة: أي لا يثبت الملك، فلو أنه وهب مشاعاً فيما يُقسم، وسلَّمه على الفساد: هل يثبت الملك، ويقع مضموناً كما في البيع الفاسد، أم لا؟
فيه اختلاف المشايخ، والمختار أنه لا يثبت الملك.
If he divided it and delivered it, it is permissible. The gift of an undivided share in what is divisible is not permissible because possession (qabd) is stipulated in gifts; the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: “A gift is not valid unless it is taken possession of.” Thus complete possession is required, and an undivided share cannot be possessed except by including something else with it, which is not gifted. And because permitting it would obligate him to something he did not undertake, namely division. His saying: “The gift is invalid” means that ownership is not established. If one gifts an undivided share in what is divisible and delivers it invalidly, is ownership established and is it guaranteed like in an invalid sale? There is disagreement among the shaykhs; the chosen view is that ownership is not established.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage from Kitab al-Hibah (Book of Gifts) discusses the ruling on gifting an undivided share (musha') in divisible property. The author states that if the donor divides the share and delivers it, the gift is valid. However, if the share is undivided, the gift is invalid (fasid). The reason given is that possession (qabd) is a condition for the validity of a gift, based on the hadith “A gift is not valid unless it is taken possession of.” Since an undivided share cannot be possessed without including something else (i.e., the rest of the property) which is not gifted, the condition of complete possession is not met. Additionally, validating such a gift would force the donor to do something he never agreed to, namely division. The text then addresses a dispute among later Hanafi scholars (mashayikh) about whether an invalid gift of musha', if delivered, confers ownership and makes the gift guaranteed (madmin) like an invalid sale (bay' fasid). The chosen position (mukhtar) is that ownership is not established, unlike an invalid sale where ownership is established and the item is guaranteed. This distinction is rooted in the nature of gifts: possession is a pillar (rukn), not just a condition, so without valid possession, no ownership arises.

Key Terms
المشاع (al-musha'): An undivided share in jointly owned property that has not been partitioned.
القبض (al-qabd): Taking physical or constructive possession of the gifted item.
الفاسد (al-fasid): An act that is defective in its conditions; ownership is not established without additional factors.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling on gifting an undivided share in divisible property if the donor delivers it without dividing it?
A) The gift is valid because possession occurred.
B) The gift is invalid, and ownership is not established.
C) The gift is valid and the donee is owner.
D) The gift is suspended until division.

Key Takeaway
A gift of undivided share in divisible property is invalid unless the share is first divided and then delivered; delivery alone does not establish ownership.

Gift of Attached Items: Istihsan Overrides Qiyas
Ruling on gift of attached items by istihsan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on gifting attached items, identify the conditions of separation and donor permission, and explain why istihsan overrides qiyas in this case.

Core Idea
Gift of fruit on the tree is valid by istihsan only if the donor separates and delivers it with permission.

This passage addresses a common question: Can you gift fruit still on the tree? By strict analogy (qiyas), the gift would be invalid because the fruit is not yet in the donee's possession. However, Hanafi jurists preferred a lenient view based on custom and ease, allowing the gift upon separation and delivery with the donor's permission. This introduces the important legal tool of istihsan as a means to achieve practical convenience in transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? Gifting an item attached to property (e.g., fruit on a tree). The ruling: valid (sahih) by istihsan when conditions are met.
The default ruling by qiyas would be invalid, but istihsan changes it.
2
Understand the qiyas argument
Why would it be invalid by qiyas? Because possession (qabd) of the fruit is not possible while it is still attached. Qiyas compares to gifts where possession is required at the time of the contract.
Zufar holds this strict qiyas position.
3
Locate the istihsan basis
Why do Hanafis override qiyas? Istihsan is based on ease (taysir) and custom ('urf) – such gifts are common and people would be harmed if they were invalid.
Istihsan is a source of law in the Hanafi school.
4
Note the conditions for validity
What conditions must be met? (1) Separation (fasl) of the gifted item from the property, and (2) possession (qabd) with the donor's permission.
Both conditions must be fulfilled; permission alone is insufficient.
5
Distinguish unauthorized action
If the donee separates and takes possession without the donor's permission, the gift is invalid by both qiyas and istihsan.
The donor's active role is crucial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو وهب شيئاً متصلاً بغيره : لا يصح إلا إذا وقع عليه الفصل والتمييز، والقبض بإذن الواهب، فحينئذ يجوز استحساناً، مثل أن يهب ثمراً على رؤوس النخل والشجر ، وخلَّى بينه وبينها من غير فصل: فالهبة باطلة، فإن ميّزه وفَصَلَه ، وأقبضه : جاز؛ استحساناً.
والقياس: لا يجوز، وهو قول زفر.
فإن فصله الموهوب له، وقبضه بغير إذن الواهب: لم يصح قياساً واستحساناً، سواء كان الفصل والقبض بحضرته، أو بغير حضرته.
وكذا إذا وهب الأشجار دون الأرض، أو الزرع دون الأرض.
If one gifts something attached to something else, it is not valid unless separation and distinction occur, and possession with the donor's permission; then it is allowed by istihsan. For example, if one gifts fruit on palm trees or trees and leaves the donee with it without separation, the gift is void. But if he distinguishes and separates it and delivers it, it is valid by istihsan. The qiyas (analogy) is that it is not valid, and that is the opinion of Zufar. If the donee separates it and takes possession without the donor's permission, it is not valid by qiyas or istihsan, whether the separation and possession occur in the donor's presence or absence. Likewise, if one gifts trees without the land, or crops without the land.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage addresses the gift of items that are attached to property, such as fruit on trees or trees attached to land. By strict analogy (qiyas), such a gift is invalid because the item is not separate and cannot be possessed independently. This is the view of Imam Zufar. However, the Hanafi school adopts istihsan (juristic preference) and permits the gift if the item is separated and delivered with the donor's permission. The example given is gifting fruit on the tree: if the donor allows the donee to take the fruit but does not separate it, the gift is void. But if the donor separates the fruit and delivers it to the donee, the gift is valid. A crucial condition is that the separation and possession must be done by the donor or with the donor's explicit permission. If the donee separates and takes possession without permission, it is invalid whether the donor is present or not. This rule also applies to gifting trees without the land, or crops without the land, as they are considered attached items. The passage underscores that istihsan overrides qiyas in these transactions to facilitate gifts, but only when the donor actively participates in making the gift deliverable.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas to achieve ease and convenience.
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical reasoning; here, qiyas would invalidate the gift due to lack of possession.
الفصل (al-fasl): Separation of the attached item from the property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a donor gifts fruit on a tree and the donee separates and takes possession without the donor's permission, what is the ruling?
A) Valid by istihsan.
B) Invalid by both qiyas and istihsan.
C) Valid if done in donor's presence.
D) Valid if done without separation.

Key Takeaway
Gift of attached items is valid by istihsan only when the donor separates and delivers them with permission.

Gift of a House with Donor's Furniture: Ruling and Legal Stratagem
Ruling on gift of house with donor's furniture and the legal stratagem

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition of vacancy (faragh) for gift delivery and apply the Hanafi legal stratagem (hila) to validate a gift of occupied property.

Core Idea
A house occupied by donor's furniture cannot be gifted; the hila is to first deposit the furniture with the donee, making it his possession.

Gifting a house that still contains your own furniture seems problematic—and in Hanafi law it is, because vacancy is a condition for valid delivery. But what if you want to gift the house without removing the furniture? The classical scholars provide a clever legal stratagem that preserves the gift's validity. In this lesson, we will dissect the ruling and the hila step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the obstacle
The act is gifting a house. The obstacle is that the house contains the donor's own furniture. Vacancy (faragh) is a condition for valid delivery (taslim).
2
State the basic ruling
The gift is invalid if the house is delivered while occupied by donor's furniture. The text says: 'If one gifts a house containing furniture belonging to the donor, and delivers the house... it is not valid because the house is occupied by the furniture and vacancy is a condition for valid delivery.'
3
Analyze the hila
The donor first deposits the furniture with the donee (as amanah), giving the donee access. Then the donor delivers the house. The house is now occupied by furniture in the donee's possession, so the condition of vacancy is not required. The gift becomes valid.
The hila works because the furniture is no longer the donor's property in the donee's hands.
4
Examine the order scenarios
If gifts are made separately: if the house is gifted first, it is invalid; if furniture is gifted first, both become valid. This reinforces the principle that the condition applies only to the donor's possessions.
If furniture is gifted first, at the time of house delivery the furniture belongs to the donee.
5
Generalize the principle
The condition of vacancy is only for the donor's possessions, not for the donee's or third-party property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو وهب داراً فيها متاع للواهب، وسلَّم الدار إليه، أو سلمها المتاع : لم يصح؛ لأن الدار مشغولةٌ بالمتاع، والفراغ شرط لصحة التسليم.
والحيلة فيه: أن يودع المتاع أولاً عند الموهوب له، ويخلي بينه وبينه، ثم يسلم الدار إليه، فيصح؛ لأنها مشغولة بمتاع هو في يده.
وبعكسه لو وهب المتاع دون الدار، وخلى بينه وبينه: صح؛ لأن المتاع لا يكون مشغولاً بها.
وإن وهب له الدار والمتاعَ جميعاً، وخلى بينه وبينهما: صح فيهما جميعاً.
وإن وهب أحدهما، وسلَّم، ثم وهب الآخر وسلَّم:
إن قدم هبة الدار فالهبة فيها : لا تصح، وفي المتاع تصح.
وإن قدم هبة المتاع صح فيهما جميعاً؛ لأن الدار وقت تسليمها كانت مشغولة بمتاع الموهوب له، فلا يمنع القبض.
If one gifts a house containing furniture belonging to the donor, and delivers the house to the donee, or delivers the furniture, it is not valid because the house is occupied by the furniture and vacancy is a condition for valid delivery. The legal stratagem (hila) for this is: first deposit the furniture with the donee and give him access to it, then deliver the house to him; it becomes valid because the house is occupied by furniture that is in his possession. Conversely, if one gifts the furniture without the house and gives him access to it, it is valid because furniture is not occupied by the house. If one gifts both the house and furniture together and gives him access, both are valid. If one gifts one of them and delivers it, then gifts the other and delivers it: if the gift of the house is first, the gift of the house is invalid but the gift of furniture is valid. If the gift of furniture is first, both are valid because at the time of delivering the house, it was occupied by furniture belonging to the donee, which does not prevent possession.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage deals with a specific scenario: a donor wants to gift a house that contains the donor's own furniture. The gift is invalid because the house is occupied by the donor's belongings, and vacancy of the gifted property is a condition for valid delivery (taslim). The author then provides a legal stratagem (hila): the donor first deposits the furniture with the donee (i.e., gives it as a deposit or amanah, not as a gift), and gives the donee access to it. Then the donor delivers the house to the donee. Since the house is now occupied by furniture that is in the donee's possession (as a deposit), the condition of vacancy is not required because the donee himself has the furniture. Thus the house gift becomes valid. Conversely, if the donor gifts the furniture without the house, it is valid because furniture is not considered occupied by the house. If both are gifted together and delivered together, both are valid. If they are gifted separately, the order matters: if the house is gifted first, its gift is invalid because it is occupied by donor's furniture; the furniture gift later is valid. If furniture is gifted first, its gift is valid, and then when the house is gifted later, the house is occupied by furniture now owned by the donee, so the house gift is valid. This showcases a key principle in Hanafi fiqh: the condition of vacancy (khala') is for the donor's possessions, not for the donee's.

Key Terms
الحيلة (al-hila): A legal stratagem to achieve a permissible result without violating the law.
الفراغ (al-faragh): Vacancy; the property must be empty of the donor's possessions for valid delivery.
التسليم (al-taslim): Delivery of the gifted item to the donee.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the legal stratagem (hila) to make a gift of a house containing the donor's furniture valid?
A) The donor should first remove the furniture.
B) The donor should first deposit the furniture with the donee, then deliver the house.
C) The donor should gift the furniture separately before the house.
D) Both B and C are correct.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a gift of property requires vacancy of donor's possessions; the hila of depositing furniture with the donee allows valid delivery.
